THE RED PAGE
BALLADE OF DREAMS.
Across the loom the shuttles fly,
TT Like random' rippled lights at play
Upon the road where you, where I,
Drift down the Valley of To-day;
White snowdrop stars beside the way
Illume the flight of fancies fled,
In some far Springtime’s snowdrop spray
Our dreams shall live when we are dead.
We quibble over how and why,
Or vex our souls with “yea” and “nay” ;
Turn all the golden years awry,
And bid the wheel of pleasure stay;
And still our webs of hodden grey
Are shot with many a wizard thread
That passes not with passing clay—-
Our dreams shall live when we are dead.
The good, the strong, the brave shall die—
All flesh shall perish e’en as they;
Nor love, nor life, nor duty’s tie
Shall hold the fateful hour at bay:
Biit past restraining barriers, yea
On universal pinions spread,
A phooenix phalanx o’er decay,
Our dreams shall live when we are dead.
With cypress gather blooms o’ may,
Beyond the dark the dawn is red ;
reace, sad one, though the gods shall slay,
Our dreams shall live when we are dead.
Marie Pitt, in The Horses of the Hills.
R. L. Stevenson
The official biography of Robert Louis
Stevenson, by Graham Balfour, though it
contained much interesting material, was an
unsatisfactory book. It appeared too soon
after the death of the subject, and it was
written by a cousin who pruned away the
eccentricities and weaknesses of li. L.S. in
the pious endeavor to make him appear a
Great Man. Henley protested in an article
which raised howls from the hero-worship-
pers. The tumult has subsided now, the
works' of Stevenson have virtually settled
into their place, and we can see the man in
something like his true proportions. These
are displayed by himself in his letters better
than anywhere else.
The original collection has been enriched
by 150 new letters, and the whole has been
arranged in chronological order by Sidney
Colvin, who has supplied biographical links
and explanatory notes. The Letters of R.
L, Stevenson, in four handsome pocket vol-
umes (published by Methuen at ss. net per
vol.), is the best kind of biography one could
wish of a man who was not of the heroic
proportions which deserve a Boswell, and
who was above all things a writer.
In his generation Stevenson was easily
first of. the class of professional writers for
whom the term “men of letters” should be
reserved. There were greater poets and
novelists; but no other writer appealed so
strongly to craftsmen and to general
readers, big and little ; and no' other, save
Andrew Lang, could do so many kinds
of literary work so well. There is in all his
books —from Virginibus Puerisque onwards
—the subtle essence of a personality more
charming than any since Charles Lamb’s.
It is that element in his letters which gives
them a high place in epistolary literature.
They reflect every phase of a gay, romantic
nature, with a stiffening of 'Puritanism;
they have something of the essayist and
something of the Shorter Catechist, some-
thing of the freakish humor which made
Symonds call him The Sprite, and something
of the quality'of talk.
All through his short life Stevenson was
in the grip of Disease, or dogged bv its
ghost. Though references to “Bluidy Jock,”
as Ire called his hemorrhages, are numerous
in these letters, there is no whining; ho
whistled bravely to keep up his spirits, and
acted upon his own advice in “Aes Triplex” :
* By all means begin your folio; even if
the doctor does not give you a year, even
if he hesitates about a month, make one
brave push and see what can be accom-
plished in a week.
He died at 44, and his printed works fill 25
volumes. A brave man, certainly; with a
love of lusty life, of open-air adventure made
poignant by ill-health. Perhaps none but a
sick man could have played so often upon
fear as a master passion as Stevenson did in
his stories, and, at its highest expression,
in Admir'al Guinea. He wrote from Vail-
ima:—
I have always feared the sound of wind
beyond anything. In my hell it would always
blow a gale.
And, in telling about his struggles with the
weeds, there, he said
I wonder if anyone had ever the same
attitude to Nature as I hold and have held
for so long? This business fascinates me
like a tune or a passion; yet all the ,ikhile I
thrill with a strong distaste. The horror
of the thing, objective and subjective, is
always present to my mind; the horror of
creeping things, a superstitious horror of
the void and the powers about me, the hor-
ror of my own devastation and continual
murders. The life of the plants comes'
through my finger tips, their struggles go
to my heart like supplications. I feel my-
self blood-boltered; then I look back on my
cleared grass and count myself an ally in
a fair quarrel, and make stout my heart.
In the same letter he said: “I scarce pull
up a weed, but I invent a sentence on the
matter.” There you have the utterance of
one who was not born fof action, but des-
tined to find words to describe action.
The letters are full of interest to all who
are concerned with the craftsmanship of writ-
ings They lay bare so much of his profes-
sional machinery, and give the reader a,
clearer perception of the pains taken to ac-=
quire that distinction of style which is the :
nineteenth century equivalent of Addisonian
grace. Most people know his confession of
having played the sedulous ape to this writer
and that when forming his style. He held
that style is the one quality in which a
writer may improve himself at will, and in
a letter to William Archer he defined the
amateur:—
Why was Jenkm an amateur in my
eyes ? . . . The reason is this: I never, or
almost never, saw two pages of his work
that I could not have put in one without
the smallest loss of material. That is the
only test I know of writing. . . . Then you
will bring me up with old Dumas. Nay,
the object of a story is to be long, to fill up
hours; the story-teller’s art of writing is to
water out by continual invention and yet
not seem to water; seem on the other hand
to practise the same wit of conspicuous
and declaratory condensation which is the
proper art ot writing.
“I would rise from the dead to preach,” he
said in that letter, and preach lie did in
many other letters to hardy veterans and be-
ginners. He tells one of the latter-
Every gay, every bright word or picture
like every pleasant air of music is a piece
of pleasure set afioat; the reader catches
it, and, if he be healthy, goes on his way
rejoicing, and it is the business of art sp to
send him as often as possible.
A few pages further on is a letter to Goss-?,
in which he throws off some irritation with
“the fatuous rabble of burgesses called ‘the
public’ ” and his own profession
As for the art that we practise I have
never been able to see why its professors
should be respected. They chose the prim-
rose path ; when they found it was not all
primroses but some of it brambly they be-
gan to think and to speak of themselves as
holy martyrs. But a man is never martyred
in any honest sense in the pursuit of his
pleasure, and delirium tremens has more
of the honor of the cross. We are full of
the pride of life, and chose like prostitutes
to live by a pleasure. We should be paid
if we give the pleasure we pretend to give,
but why should we be honored?
Stevenson, the popular author, forgot that
respect is for power, not pleasure; and that
many of the pleasure-giving writers received
little pay or honor in their day. They were
driven on to t-he “primrose path” by a con-
junction of the stars, or anything rather than
hope or honor.
However, to Stevenson the inky way whs
the “primrose path.” He revelled in the art
of writing, had plenty of praise, was happy
in his friendships, and was sustained in phy-
sical trials by a sturdy optimism. He
studied himself for literary purposes, and
exploited “K.L.S.” thoroughly. All that was
of interest in the man is in his work and
letters. Dr. Jekyll, the romancer, will be
read and remembered with pleasure along
with all but the greatest of his tribe ; Mr.
Hyde, the moralist, already shares the honors
with Elbert Hubbard, Ralph Waldo Trine
and other uplifting writers of America.
Verses by Marie E. J. Pitt
While the horse-poet is resting, Marie E.
J. Pitt makes the gee-gee gallop for sym-
bolical purposes in her book, The Horses
of the Hills and other Verses (T. C. Lo-
thian). In Ogilvie’s version of the “Riding
of the Rebel” the fiery steed was tamed by
the hand of a woman. Mrs. Pitt’s “Rebel”
evaded all would-be captors, and “raced for
freedom by a track we dared not follow.”
The horse to her is a symbol of freedom,
and a passionate desire for freedom per-
vades her book. There are verses written
for their own sake with lines that are
musical, as in the ballade printed above,
and this in spite of its sibilants:
The slumbrous serenade the brown bee
sings.
Here is a pretty figure for Autumn:
In her stranded gold is the silver thread
Of the first grey hair in the head of the
year. 7 •
And here a river in flood:
Like a hooded serpent ready to smite
Old Mitchell rears in his straitened bed.
Sometimes her notes are almost as soft as
Kendall’s as in
Myrtle, myrtle, watching yet,
Where old Montezuma races
Down the water-worn rock faces,
Singing songs to lonely places
Set in ways of wood and wet!
You can see them hardening under the in-
fluence of O’Dowd in “The Heathen of To-
Day.”
Their altars mock the sullen skies
They worship unafraid
. With daily human sacrifice
The Juggernauts of Trade.
Hyssop and rue and mandragore
Fringe Fashion’s fetid marge,
The helot sinks beside the oar
Of Luxury’s gilded barge.
And you get at least one pile of well-
baked O’Dowdian bricks in “Society.”
If Mfs. Pitt does not sing with the sweet-
ness and abandon of a bu§h magpie it is
because she sees the bars of the cage around
her, and her song is a song of protest and
invective against her captors. Her book is
a contribution of real value to the poetry
militant of Australian democracy.
Flaubert’s "Temptation of St.
Anthony"
Lafcadio Hearne’s translation of Flau-
bert’s La Tentation de St. Antoine has just
been published for the first time in America
by Alice Harriman, of New York, the only
woman in the publishing business.
Hearne began his version 36 years ago,
but could never persuade the contemporary
publishers to even read his MS.; the subject
was vetoed on sight as “immoral.” Yet
this masterpiece of Flaubert’s genius (which
occupied him 29 years, and which he en-
tirely rewrote 4 times) presents the “Temp-
tation" as a distinctly intellectual one.
The Devil (disguised as a former disciple,
Hilarion) appeals to the reason of the Saint
by raising before him a series of visions,
representing the downfall of every belief held
by man, from the beginnings of history. At
last the voice of great Jehovah Himself is
heard—the dying utterance of a god van-
quished and passing away. As the echo
fades into silence and blackest night, An-
thony, terrified, gazes upward at Hilarion,
who with each succeeding vision has grown
vaster and vaster: finds him grown great
as the Universe itself, beautiful as an arch-
angel, luminous as a sun: stammers “Who
art thou?” and hears the answer, “Men call
me SCIENCE.”
Forthwith the great angel carries him into
the deeps of space among the stars; mute
witnesses of the impersonality of God, and
we hear Anthony’s heartbroken cry:—
What! my prayers, my sobs, my groans,
the sufferings of my flesh, the transports of
my love—have all these things gone out to a
lie —to emptiness, unavailingly—like (he cry
of a bird, like a whirling of dead leaves ?. . .
Science cannot prevail, and abandons him.
Then, after visions of the Spirits of Lust
and Death, and of the animals of fable,
comes the final temptation. The Saint’s
sight turns inward upon Matter itself. He
beholds how universal Life underlies the ap-
pearance of rock and crystal, even as animal
or star: in a delirious ecstasy he hovers on
the verge of belief in Pantheism —when day
breaks: the dreams vanish: the sun rises—
and in the very disc of the sun itself beams
the face of Christ.
The “temptation” is over. Anthony has
won, Faith has conquered Disbelief —or (ac-
cording to another standard) Anthony has
lost, Emotion has vanquished Reason.
Flaubert has infused this gigantic drama
of the soul with that genius for word-color
of which he was master. The whole has a
bewildering effect on the mind : it pours for
us a torrent of exotic riches, the variety of
which, like much of the complex chord-
weaving of Wagner, the understanding al-
most vainly struggles to hold. The visions
press on each other with exuberance of color
and motion, always verging toward the de-
corative and bizarre. We are shown Diana
of Ephesus “black with enamelled eyes,”
while Anthony sees the gods of Indian-
Riding upon birds, rocked in palanquins,
enthroned upon seats of gold, standing in
ivory niches —they dream, voyage, command,
drink wine, respire the breath of flowers:
dancing girls whirl in the dance; giants pur-
sue monsters; at the entrance of grottoes
solitaries meditate. Eyes cannot be dis-
tinguished from stars; nor clouds from band-
erolles: peacocks quench their thirst at
rivers of gold dust; the embroidery of pav-
ilions seems to blend with the spots of leop-
ards : colored rays intercross in the blue air
together with flying arrows and swinging
censers and all this develops like a lofty
frieze, resting its base upon the rocks and
rising to the sky.
Yet so exact is the intricate presentment
that each heretical sect of the early Chris-
tians, for instance, finds room for treatment
in a brief picture which displays not only
the outward garment of the belief but its es-
sence as well. And with a nobler faith the
glittering prose can rise to impressive sim-
plicity, as in that lament, sung by the
Earth over dying Mithras, which drew An-
thony to dream of the mother of Jesus:—
. Thou didst emerge from the Orient, and
didst take me, all trembling with the dew,
into thy arms, O Sun! Doves fluttered upon
the azure of thy mantle ; our kisses evoked
low,breezes among the foliage; and I aban-
doned myself wholly to thy love, delighting
in the pleasure of my weakness.
Alas! alas! —Why didst thou depart, to
run upon the mountains! A boar did wound
thee at the time of the autumnal equinox!
Thou art dead ; and the fountains weep—
the trees bend down. The wind of winter
whistles through the naked brushwood.
My eyes are about to close, seeing that
darkness covers them! Now thou dwellest
in the underworld near the mightiest of my
rivals.
O Persephone, all that is beautiful de-
scends to thee, never to return !... .
Lafcadio Hearne’s English has the sole
defect that at times it adheres too
closely to French idiom. This in some
cases obscures the meaning, as where Hil-
arion, a tiny child, is described as Anthony’s
“ancient disciple”; while in others it les-
sens the beauty of the presented idea. Thus,
in the passage quoted above, the transcrip-
tion would have gained if for the brazen lit-
eral words, Our kisses evoked low breezes
among the foliage, some more rhythmic para-
phrase had been employed, such as, Our
kisses whispered through the leaves like
sighs.

This from the Literary Supplement to
London limes : “Among the treasures of
Coronation literature, ‘Pearls of Poesy a
Biographical Birthday Book,’ by Popular
Poets of the Period, issues its prospectus,
wherein 06G poets—one for each day of
the year, including leap-year,’ are invited
to contribute each a poem and autobio-
graphical notice, which will be inserted (as
far as possible) on his or her birthday in
'Fook of the Year.’ The printers,
Messrs'. Japp and Joothill, have engaged
as editor ‘Dr. Chas. Forsliaw, than whom
no better name is known in the world of
poetry and have printed specimens and
hints tor the poets’ guidance. The poems
inust not exceed 24 lines, and ‘must all be
of a joy-inspiring and soul-ennobling na-
ture —rippling with smiles, as it were.’ The
autobiographies are limited to 200 words
apiece ; and if the vain child of the Muses
should exceed this allowance and make - his
chronicles too long, then Dr. Forsliaw will
foreshorten them in the manner, presum-
ably, in which he has dealt with the speci-
mens. Thus we read of one of the poets that ‘lie
is nephew of the Archbishop of Armagh
and has written verse since he was 16, and
lus poems have met with the direct ap-
proval of Royalty’; of another, ‘Mr.
Barker s muse is ever lofty and chaste,
whether it be from grave to gay, or from
lively to severe.’ £lO is offered for the best
poem; but Messrs. Tapp and Toothill have
not extended their confidence to Dr. For-
shaw so far as to allow him to adjudicate
this prize, although he is a Fellow of the
Royal Society of Literature, and ‘his de-
cision regarding all matters pertaining to
Pearls of Poesy must be regarded as final.’
Ihe scheme is that the contributors shall
purchase the volume, and vote after read-
ing it. Should they simply and honestly
prefer to vote each for himself—which, to
judge from the specimens, they might
reasonably do —then the £lO will, we pre-
sume, be equally distributed among them,
which will give 61d. per head, and, reduce
the cost of the book to them at subscribers’
price from ss. 6d. to 4s. ll^d.”
Shaw and his American biographer, Hen-
derson, _ have been quarrelling over the re-
sponsibility for the latter’s big .eulogistic
book, which has produced some sarcastic com-
ments from English reviewers. Shaw says
that the biography is already attracting a
stream of American pilgrims to a house in
which he never lived; he did not say that “If
Henley had been a good fellow I should
doubtless have influenced him,” and he did
not draw Dubedat in The Doctor's Dilemma
from Aubrey Beardsley. “I must add that
the fault is mine, and not Prof. Henderson’s.
He strove to make me read my own life in
manuscript, and strove in vain ; I had had
enough of it while living it.” Henderson re-
plies that Shaw not only supplied a large
part of the material, but read the manuscript
of the book and annotated it. He is there-
fore responsible for the statements attributed
to him, and for the photographs. The dis-
pute makes Shaw look foolish.
There is no falling off in the supply of
American millionaires who turn naturally
from the collection of coin and securities
to the collection of books, autographs, Old
Masters or anything else that may be in
fashion. The supply of Gutenberg Bibles
and letters in the handwriting of Burns,
Cromwell and such people is not increasing
?° HP goes the price of curios. At the
sale of Iluth’s collection of autographs in
London last month a letter of Mary Queen
of Scots, which had been bought in 1862
tor two guineas, was sold for £1025, a let-
ter of Shelley which cost £6 12s. brought
£mo a letter of Keats cost £27 and sold
ror £450, a letter of Katherine of Arragon
brought £BOO (previous price, £26), the
MS. of Burns’ “Jolly Beggars” £490 (cost
£l2), and Fielding’s receipt for the £6OO
paid for the copyright of Tom Jones, which
cost Iluth £l2 125., was sold for £lOl5.
Altogether, the autographs purchased 30 to
50 years ago at £2OOO realised £13,000.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wanderlust.
N.S.W.
The main road I
The plain road !
Oh, far it goes and wide.
The highway to
Fields fresh and new;
The track of “What betide.”
The young and strong,
A gallant' throng,
Along its length essay
To break a lance
With “What may chance,”
Or welcome “Come what may.”
But —I must take my own road,
The Town road,
The down road,
Though would to God again I trod
The path to Better Day.
The green road!
The clean road!
The way of wood and wild!
Where bird and brute
Reign absolute —
The highway undefiled.
Across the plains
The sweeping trains
Of wanton breezes urge
Such shine and sheen
Of billows green,
Such grassy surge on surge!
But I must go my own way,
The dry road,
The by-road!
Though it were sweet
To find my feet
Upon the bushland’s verge!
The old road!
The bold road!
The road adventure cleared 1
Such way as Drake
And his would take
To singe the Spaniard’s beard.
Columbus took
That route, and Cook
Made plain the path for us;
The bones of brave
In thousands pave
The pathway perilous.
But I must go my own road —
The back road!
The black road!
Though I am fain
To dare again
Ways more precipitous.
The last road!
The vast road!
The road that all abhor!
For Death and Fate
Stand at the gate,
Grim guard and janitor.
The straight, the bowed,
The meek, the proud,
With fixed and awful stare,
Unclad and stark,
Pursue the dark,
The dreadful thoroughfare.
But I must go my own road.
The same road,
The tame road.
Till Warder Death
Me beckon eth,
And I go hence to —where?
JOHN WALTERS.
Briny Bards.
Oft in vivid song and story, long before the world was
hoary,
Did the ancient bards pen-picture all the vast and
restless sea;
Wondrous stories, pantheistic, stories marvellous and
mystic,
Did they write, until the waters seemed the home of
Mystery ;
Broad Pacific and Atlantic, these were names un-
known-romantic
Stanzas told, in metre frantic, of one sea which was
the whole.
And the people read and wondered, till their reason
tripped and blundered,
And they took for truth the gleamings of each simple
poet’s soul!
Far along the path of ages they have trekked in easy
stages,
All the vaporings and visions of the long-forgotten
clay;
We have heard their voices trolling, through the aeons,
soul-controlling;
We have locked the Past’s grim records in the
archives of To-day—
Of the “tempest” and its “howling,” and the “thun-
der’s muffled growling,”
And, the “sky in anger scowling,” Noah saw from
out the Ark ;
And the “Ocean for my pillow,” and. the “sea-mew”
and the “billow”:
They were shipped from “Port Oblivion” in “the
brave and gallant bark.”
All the old, familiar wheezes, wafted by those “gentle
breezes”—
That have “wafted” hard for ages—reach us still
from near and far;
And a curse I am maturing, solid, subtle and enduring,
For the bard who writeth vainly of the “Sailors’
Guiding Star.”
All the poets of the Ocean, on this Earth, I have a
notion,
Get their “poetry of motion”, from a source of bottled
ale;
And the sea-songs sad or cheery of these writers fat
and beery,
Should be “shivered,” like the “timbers,” by a “howl-
ing, seething gale.”
Yea! the boosted, briny ranter makes my choler rise
instanter,
And my feelings daily fatten each relentless curse of
mine,
For it is a sore affliction to be chased by salty dic-
tion,
And I pray the gods to send me some unfailing
anodyne.
Ship the bards in some old packet, void of boot,
or sock, or jacket,
Let them stand the blessed racket, if they can, for
twenty years;
Let them feed on “pantiles” stony, and on salt-
horse, green and bony,
And their poetry shall perish, in a flood of briny
tears.
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper {and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters {with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. ‘‘The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for ‘‘copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

fused.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.

dential.

when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, JULY 27, 1911.
TAKING DOWN THE DECORATIONS.
The Coronation is over, the Crown is put
away, the bottle of oil has been corked up,
and the last of the decorations has been
taken down. The tinsel (a little worn
with wind and weather), the flags (a trifle
bedraggled), the electric wires and the tin
casings (something the worse for wear) have
been stored for possible future use; and the
Royal portraits which graced the shop win-
dows have given place to other advertising
devices. The people cannot jubilate forever ;
and it is recognised od all sides that a
mei'ely ornamental display hampers the or-
dinary round of a work-a-day world. A
nation must earn its living; and the decora-
tions must come down to make way for the
butcher’s cart and the baker’s basket. “God
Save the King” is an occasional song used
to indicate the time when hats and over-
coats should he looked for; hut the cheerful
vocal discord of the morning Milk oh ! is
of far more permanent importance, just as
the legend telling us that John Smith sells
Australian pickles is of infinitely more en-
during value than the six yards ot cheap,
ported cotton which for a few days obscuied
the regular trade sign. Pageantry and dis-
play have little real part in a nations Me.
People go to see the lights, trappings and
music because there is something out of the
common to be seen, but they do not worry
over the purpose or meaning of the display ;
and the King is not a whit more really
King because the plumber and tinsmith used
their best skill in putting a gauciy and in-
jurious-looking crown on a public building
during a Coronation week. The decorations
have come down, and the purpose for which
they were set up becomes more and more
obscure.
Once the political edifice was one vast
decoration. There were crowns and coronets
plastered in every available place. Coats
of arms were of vastly greater importance
than any other kind of clothing; flags were
held in higher esteem than bread; and a
gorgeous pageant, interspersed with turns of
fierce fighting, filled up the days and nights
of all the men and women who really
counted. The other men and women fed,
clothed and housed the important ones, and
supplied the ready cash for the royal or
ducal entertainment. If discontent stirred
among them, and they demanded rights or
called for better conditions, their masters
provided a public show of lavish magnifi-
cence ; if they cried aloud with hunger, the
great ones of the earth allowed them to look
through the barriers at a glittering panto-
mime, set with ermine and velvet, or paraded
in military pomp before their eyes. Wealth
of barbaric ornament w r as spread on every
side, and splendid titles rolled on the lips
of heralds and court flunkeys. Nearly
everything that stands out on the pages of
popular history was in the nature of de-
coration. Orders of knighthood, ranks of
the peerage, chambers of alleged legislature,
embassies, dignities of the Church, and even
the very beheadings, crusades, wars and
international intrigues, were the many-col-
ored embroidery thrown over the dull, grey,
hopeless monotony of the real mediaeval life.
Tite whole art of politics was to keep the
colors bright on the decorations, and make
them as dazzling and blinding as possible.
* * * *
The long story of the struggle for freedom
in Europe is merely the record of how some
of the ancient decorations were taken down.
The man on the ladder, with the claw-ham-
mer and pincers, removing the emblems of
“loyalty,” is himself an emblem of those who
have for centuries battled for the people’s
rights. Their tool was mostly the political
claw-hammer, and their business the removal
of nails which fastened the huge, tawdry
ornaments of the past on the living bodies of
the nations. That which was of use and
purpose must remain; that which was mere
tinsel and colored paper must come down.
The masquerade of kings, dukes and other
noble persons which had filled centuries with
radiant riot was blocking the road for the
grocer’s basket and the coal waggon. At
Runnimede John signed his name to an
order for taking down some of the gilded ob-
structions ; at Naseby the Ironsides chopped
away some more with their swords as they
dashed in on the flanks of Prince Rupert's
tired horses; the bullets at Bunker’s Hill
riddled quite a number of fanciful devices
which were interfering with progress ; and
the Reign of Terror, with its surging red
flood drawn from the veins of some of the
most ornamental persons in France, washed
away a host of shining things which, if res-
cued, might have set up all the pantomimes
of the world in perpetual business. So the
work of removing the decorations has gone
on. The plain little man in the bell-topper
and scanty grey whiskers, who is jostled by
the blatant bookmaker with a pound of gold
hanging across his abdomen, is the modern
representation of the glorious individual who
once walked in cloth of gold and carried the
pride of a Lucifer in his jewelled sword-
hilt. Nevertheless, some decorations re-
main, toned down in color certainly, but
numerous still, and the job of taking them
down is likely to keep Europe busy for some
time to come.
* * * *
Australia, for all her distance from the
older lands, began her development too early
to be wholly free from the decorative tradi-
tions of the past. Almost before she had
constructed a road, or put a roof upon a
public building, she began to cumber her
path with meretricious toys and emblematic
designs. To-day the country from Cape
York to the Leeuwin is filled with Gov-
ernors, Governors’ flunkeys, Legislative
Councillors, Mayors—either Lorded or plain
- —Aldermen, Shire Councillors, and other
more or less beautiful persons. Most of
these, being quite useless and exceedingly
costly, must be accounted for on the score
that they are intended to be decorative.
Truth to tell, they are not very ornamental
(the alleged decorations seldom are) ; but
thev can on occasion at least shine in varied
and extensive rhetoric. It is their delight, in
rolling periods, with or without the doubtful
aspirate, to extol the decorations of the older
days, of which they feel that they them-
selves form a belated but not inglorious
part. In Australia, as elsewhere, these de-
corations must come down, to make room
for the people who have real work to do.
The printed calico which, a fortnight ago,
hid the trade sign, is an emblem of all the
cumbering and unnecessary institutions
which stand in the path of a nation’s pro-
gress. As no reasonable argument can be
advanced in favor of peroetually hanging
bits of tawdry, striped cloth to cover John
Smith’s intimation that he .sells pigs’ feet,
so there is no defence to be set up for the ob-
trusion of the cocked hats of six State Gov-
ernors between those States and the actual
and serious work in w-hich they are engaged.
The ancient formalities of Parliament —the
mace, the Speaker’s wig, Black Rod. the
constant bobbing to the Chair, and all the
irritating process of repetition and dull,
droning inanity which accompanies the pas-
sage of a Bill—are in the same case. Sys-
tem and order are necessary, but there is no
reason why the old, portentous, time-wasting
forms which did duty in the fourteenth cen-
tury should remain to block the Parlia-
mentary pathway to-day. We might as well
re-introduce trial by ordeal, or insist that
our Australian army should be recruited
according to the methods in vogue under the
Feudal System. Australia has large work
to do, and asks for elbow room in which to
do it; and so she must reach for the claw-
hammer. A nation can have no use for a
triumphal arch which marks no triumph, or
for a legislative body which frames no
worthy legislation. Our national building
is still in process of construction, and the
decorations which overlie the simple lines
of the architectural design are not merely
tawdry, hut are injurious in that they
hamper the free use of mallet and trowel.
In the interests of good, sound, enduring
work, the decorations must come down.
AUSTRALIA’S TARIFF, WHICH IS
CHIEFLY HELPFUL TO JAPAN.
It is a hoary truism that a nation’s pros-
perity, as well as its ultimate security, rests
on production. Constantinople, Venice, Rome,
and a dozen other great States and cities, tried
to live by trading alone, and went down. Un-
der modern conditions there is only one way
in which a new country can achieve manu-
factures, and that is by high Protection.
Australia was born a Cobdenite; yet so
clearly do the electors see the need for as-
piring to be something more than a sheep-
walk and a cow-run, that the Australian
Freetrade party has, officially speaking, died,
and no political candidate dares to exploit
the fiscal gags that did service a quarter of
a century ago. The voice of the nation has
for years past been crying for a tariff that
will really protect; but, owing to a number
of causes, its politicians have doggedly re-
mained deaf. The importing interests are
still strong, financially and otherwise, in the
cities; three-fourths of the daily papers,
though they no longer barrack openly for
free imports, fight as a matter of course
against every move in the direction of raised
duties; representatives of the oversea con-
cerns which dump their goods here are
tireless in their efforts to keep the. trade
door ajar; and the Australian Parliament
has members on both sides of the political
fence who are painfully amenable to the re-
presentations of these lobbying wire-pullers.
A lesson in the way in which the Freetrade
crowd has contrived so far to defeat the
wishes of the people may be learned from
any annual statement of Customs. Thougli
this country supplies the world with wool, it
imports annually over £2,000,000 a year of
woollen piece-goods alone; and the bill for
imported apparel runs to over £14,500,000.
The country grows sound wines, and its
beers are at least as good as those of the
United States. Yet during last year it im-
ported so much ales and other alcoholic
goods that it paid duty amounting to
£2,541,152. Also, it imported over three
and a-haif millions’ worth of iron in bars,
sheets, and other forms, and such vast quan-
tities of machinery that a million and a
quarter was collected in duty. The total
Customs receipts were £10,440,936. That
represents an increase of just on a million
sterling over the receipts of the previous
year, when more goods were imported
than during any previous 12 months in the
country’s history.
Obviously there is something rotten about
an allegedly protective tariff which, after 10
years, and despite revision, allows the coun-
try to put up a lifetime’s record in the mat-
ter of Customs receipts. The principal
reason why Australia’s tariff is of little
more value than a burst umbrella in a rain-
storm, is that it is too low in regard to
almost every item on the schedule. The
piano-manufacturing industry will serve to
point a fiscal moral as well as any other.
The present duty on imported pianos is 20
per cent., as against 4» per cent, in America
and 40 per cent, in Japan—a country where
skilled labor offers freely at Is. a day. An
idea of the sort of service a 20 per cent, duty
is to this industry may be gained from the
following Customs returns, relating to the
imports of pianos :
Number. Value.
1908 .. 10,404 .. £242,637
1909 . . 11,556 .. 259,618
1910 . . 14,068 . . 335,675
The big Beale factory, at Annandale
(N.S.W.), is one of many nationally-valu-
able concerns which are being hit by the
same insane system that is driving the Eng-
lish manufacturer out of the business. For
years Beale’s have been giving regular em-
ployment to over 300 artisans and crafts-
men ; also to a small army of laborers and
carters. Outside the factory there are tim-
ber-cutters and carters working in the bush,
and there are clerks, shop-hands, etc., all
over Australia. One way and another prob-
ably 3000 Australians depend on this busi-
ness for a livelihood. And obviously, in
view of the rapid increase in imported
goods, they are liable to find their means of
livelihood lost. The Australian, rightly,
insists on high wages; and, since lie cannot
insist upon his trade rival also paying high
wages, his one hope is in a high tariff, since
that alone is in his own hands. Three-
fourths of the imported pianos come from
Germany; and the average earnings in the
musical instrument-making trade's of that
country, according to British Board of Trade
returns, is 28s. 2d. for 54 hours’ work —
equal to 245. lid. for 48 hours. The Aus-
tralian workman, who has known the benefits
of Arbitration awards, would have no use
for a wage like that.
* * * *
The Japanese wage-rates are not more
than one-fourth the German, which is to say
they are about one-tenth of those paid in the
Commonwealth. This fact, added to Japan’s
40 per cent, duty, is shortly going to render
the Jap as dangerous a rival to the Aus-
tralian piano-manufacturer as he already
is to the far better-protected Australian fur-
niture-manufacturer. The demand for Euro-
pean musical instruments is fast increasing
amongst these most imitative people. There
are already a dozen factories at work in
Japan, supplying that demand, and with the
feeble barrier of a 20 per cent, duty, the
Australian market will get the surplus stock
of those factories. The calamity may not
injure the established Australian music con-
cerns. The only effect may be to transform
them from manufacturing into importing
houses. Indeed, that process is already to be
seen at work in other directions. Till re-
cently, for example, thousands of locally-
made sewing machines were sold in Aus-
tralia, some patented parts only being im-
ported. A fool-body of legislators placed
duties on parts when imported separately,
but they let the completed articles in free.
Consequently, no sewing machines are now
being made in the Commonwealth, and the
country is flooded with foreign-made goods
which sell at an inordinately high rate. In
a land like this, whose existence may depend
in a few years’ time on the possession of a
decent-sized white population, a system that
encourages importing, which employs tens,
as against manufacturing, which employs
hundreds, is plain insanity.
* * * *
It is not the wish of the Australian people
that this state of things should prevail. Nine
out of ten citizens are fully alive to the
perilous need of population. Opposition to
universal service and an Australian navy is
so utterly dead that last week the Sydney
daily which two years ago was most acid
on the local Defence question implored the
Commonwealth to “employ the next 10 years
in building up her defensive strength with
unremitting diligence.” It will be granted
that the first essential of defensive strength
is numbers—the material out of which the
“big battalions” are formed. .Nothing is
surer than that, if Australia's’population
continues to linger in the vicinity of five
millions, part or all of the continent must pass
into other hands, and colored ones at that.
The nation’s very first concern is to averi
that horror. The experience of nearly half a
century proves that immigration societies
and immigration propaganda work do no-
thing worth talking about to attract large
numbers to these or any other parts.
The true prosperity which goes with
settled industries is the only effective lure,
as America and Canada have learned since
they went in for whole-hog Protection. Yet,
year by year, Australia, which wants settled
industries and the immigrants that flock to
them as badly almost as it wants the air, is
content to dodder along importing millions of
pounds’ worth of goods which it might be
making itself; or, in other words, it is satis-
fied to go on supporting an arniy of poten-
tial invaders where it might be giving em-
ployment to that number of white defenders.
And meanwhile its population remains al-
most stationary, and its brown enemy
crouches expectantly on the mat. The guilt
in this matter has hitherto been mostly with
the politicians. The Smiths and Cooks
on the one hand, and the W. M. Hugheses
and Mahons on the other, have joined in
Parliament in an unsavory alliance to defeat
the popular will. But if they, and others like
them, are allowed to continue indefinitely
emperilling the Commonwealth, and assist-
ing its possible foes with a flagrantly bogus
duty schedule, the blame will shift to the
shoulders of the people themselves. Between
now and next Federal elections every
Australian should put to the candi-
date who is after his vote the blunt
question: “Will you swear, if you are
elected, to go bald-headed and at once
for such a revision of the tariff as will
effectively protect Australian industries?”
If he says “No,” or attempts to hedge, it is
up to the patriot to oppose him as warmly as
though he were Satan himself. As a mat-
ter of fact, the Australian politician who
fights for the importing cause in the present
state of world politics, is the Devil, or
nothing.
AUSTRALIA, UNLIMITED.
E. J. Brady issues a prospectus:—
When I went to school in the Bush 30 years ago
there were several maps hanging on the walls. One
of them was the map of Australia. I cannot re-
member that we specialised on this map ; it came in
casually with the geography lessons, along with the
other continents. We took more interest in Africa;
there were lions there, and mummies, and a great
desert. Our early educational system evidently did
not regard patriotic stimulus as essential to the
development of the callow Australian mind. I ques-
tion if its true value has yet been realised.
I was taken from my Bush school and enrolled
among the scholars of the Peabody Institute in Wash-
ington, U.S.A. I found that the American system
specialised on the geography of the United States,
and devoted scanty attention to Australia. No-
body knew much about Australia. My school-
mates were surprised because I wasn’t black.
My own knowledge of the country I had left was
not . very great, so I drew largely on imagination.
I was in a minority of one in that school, and 1
had to hold Australia’s end up. But they
taught me one thing in America which I have
never forgotten; they taught- me how strong national
sentiment can he made by patriotic education.
There was no time for pessimism when it came to
the United States. They had an arid West, but they
guessed they would turn it into good country by-and-
bye. They have done so. American enterprise was
capable of dealing with minor propositions such as
deserts. Later on, American enterprise came over
here and showed the way at Mildura. American
enterprise has its faults, but we could do with
some of its healthy optimism in this country.
Up to 10 years ago I was doubtful about Aus-
tralia ; I had lived too long in Sydney, then the
centre of the anti-Australian sentiment. About that
time I began to study this continent at first hand,
to examine its possibilities for myself, to see with
my own eyes, to think with my own brains.
.1 fitted up a covered waggon and went west of
Sydney into the Wheat Belt. I saw thousands of
acres covered with yellow grain. I crossed millions
of acres capable of similar results. I said: “Aus-
tralia need not trouble herself about the bread supply;
she can grow flour for a hundred millions, and spare
wheat enough for export as well.”
I went out of the Wheat Belt into the Sheep
Country. I saw that Australia was capable of grow-
ing more than enough wool to clothe a hundred mil-
lion people, and still have wool to send away to
other countries. I said: “This question of fine
fabrics and fresh mutton need not trouble us. No
Australian woman will have to abbreviate her skirts
because of a shortage of woollen material; no Aus-
tralian need send abroad for his morning chop. With
anything like an equitable distribution we can all
have good clothes to wear, and roast lamb at regular
intervals —-with mint sauce.”
When I went out of the Sheep Country into the
Cattle Lands, I saw where the national beef problem
had been already solved by beneficent Nature. The
continent would grow beef —good, juicy, Australian
beef, with a nice succulent edge of fat around it; and
the by-products—hides for the leather, and horns for
my lady’s back-hair comb —came in along with it.
How much beef for use and surplus for export the
continent was capable of producing, I left to statis-
ticians. I saw that enough could be raised to meet
the requirements of a hundred million local inhabi-
tants, anyhow.
I came upon iron ore, which a visiting expert
informed me ought to be supplying all Australia with
suspension bridges, and boiler plates, and gun barrels,
and all the thousand manufactured articles of modern
life. He told me there were about 40 known deposits
of a similar capacity spread over Australia and Tas-
mania. I said: “What would be the output of these
iron mines if they were all working? Do you think
they would turn out enough ore to supply the needs
of a hundred million people?” “Let ’em have the
blast-furnaced chance!” the expert replied. “As far
as iron is concerned, Australia, in my opinion,
possesses the best ores in the world, and the largest
quantities of them; and you can take it from me that
iron, not gold, is the bed-rock metal on which any
country’s greatness is based. The sooner this con-
tinent gets down to that fact, the better for its
future as a white man’s country.”
The expert travelled some way with me. We went
through diamond country, and incidentally he ob-
served that precious stones —in which he took little
stock —were plentiful in many parts of this continent.
He was more concerned with useful metals. I heard
from him in the tin district of Inverell. He was
enthusiastic about Australian copper and tin; and
what he said I verified by travel and investigation.
So 1 have now no fear that Australia will suffer a
shortage of gold, copper, lead, tin, iron, or any of


the other metals that are necessary to complete
national development. As for coal, the already-
proved fields are sufficient for our requirements for
centuries to come.
I drove —and when I didn’t drive I floated —through
hundreds of miles of forests. Once I rather despised
the homely gum-tree. Nowadays I feel inclined to
take my hat off to it. I appreciate its worth and
value. The durability and adaptability of Australian
timbers are proved. These timbers exist in enormous
quantities, and are diffused over wide areas. They
form an important item in our national stock-taking.
We have abundance for local requirements, and vast
quantities to sell to less-fortunate neighbors. In-
telligent Forestry Departments are seeing to it
already that afforestation is carried out as a pro-
vision for the future.
I descended upon tropical Australia —a committee
of inquiry composed of one. Here I got a new con-
ception of my native land. I saw sugar, arrowroot,
rice, coffee, spices, cocoanuts, dates, bananas, man-
goes and custard-apples growing as plentifully as I
had seen apples and oranges growing elsewhere. Then
the full significance of Australian climates came home
to me. I realised that the gooseberry and the
granadillo are both products of this continent; that
damsons or dates are equally possible. For tobacco,
cotton, or tea, we need not go abroad if we choose
to supply ourselves.
I wandered through dairying districts. Hills that
had once been regarded as of no value except for the
native cedar and pine that grow upon them were
dotted with fat dairy cattle, up to their knees in rich
pasturage. I looked on the Australian cow with a
respect born of Knowledge newly acquired; I realised
her potentialities, and saw where her wealth-producing
functions could be indefinitely extended.
“By Cape York and Cape Howe”! I said, “but
there will be no lack of fresh milk for that priceless
immigrant, the Australian infant, nor of butter for
the Australian breakfast-table, nor of cream, nor of
cheese.”
It was a land flowing with milk, where the honey
was good, and the molasses of the best quality.
I drove through districts where vineyards flourished,
and red and white wines lay in the cellars. I saw
potato crops, and maize crops, and onion crops, crops
of almost everything that is grown in any part of
the world; I saw chicory growing, and sandalwood,
and I bought 18 fine pineapples for a shilling.
I sampled Australian-distilled rum, and quenched
my thirst with Australian-brewed beer.
My journey ended far north of the Tropic of Capri-
corn. It was the best and pleasantest lesson I ever
had. It taught me what every Australian youth
should learn at school —that Australia is the richest
and best country on earth; that it holds within its
four corners all the potentialities of a supreme nation-
hood, the most powerful, advanced, humane and en-
lightened the world has ever seen; and it should be
the dream and ambition of every native-born to help
that ideal of Nationhood to become a living historical
fact. If that spirit were inculcated into the minds
of Australian youth in every bark-roofed, iron-
roofed, tile-roofed academy beneath the south-
ern skies; if it were made the dominant
note of our politics, State, Federal and muni-
cipal ; if it were emphasised on every platform,
and admitted in every pulpit; if the leaders of this
young nation would quit the petty considerations
that obscure the larger national issues, in 50 years
Australia would do what Japan has done since she
made up her mind to become a powerful nation.
I am over 40 years of age. I have waited and
watched for 25 years to see my native country wake
up. I have waited to see the development of a dis-
tinctive national sentiment such as I found as a
schoolboy in America. Australia is a better country
than America; it is the best, the richest, the fairest
land on earth, but it has not yet in spirit joined the
march of nations. The imagination of her rulers
must be widened. Continental conceptions must re-
place parochial considerations. A general enthusiasm
for all that is Australian —and best —must be culti-
vated. Sectarianism must be choked, self-interest
repressed, the worship of false gods and shoddy idols
cease. Above all, the empty spaces must be filled.
If we dared to emulate, we might cease to dread
Japan. She has demonstrated what a country can
do in a comparatively few years, with a strong public
spirit and a determination to achieve. Japan is a
mere group of islands. Australia is a vast continent
which for over a hundred years has been content to
remain as an outpost of European civilisation. It
cannot be expected that the next 50 years will be a
repetition of the last. In the next 50 years Aus-
tralia will have to prove herself. Her strength must
be developed from within, not from without.
The opportunity for a beginning is here. The
time is now.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Law-deriding Class.
The most openly lawless class in Australia,
if one may judge by its chosen representa-
tives, is that which for years has claimed the
title “law-abiding.” Last week a N. S. Wales
M.L.C. named Wetherspoon publicly an-
nounced that if, as a result of rural workers
being included in Minister Beeby’s Indus-
trial Bill, a Grown official attempts to “in-
terfere” with the Wetherspoon estate, he
(Wetherspoon, M.L.C.) will chase that
official with a gun. During the Referenda
campaign Bruce Smith solemnly warned
the electors that there would be a bloody
revolution if the cause of Monopoly was de-
feated ; and he inferred that he would be in
the gore up to the knees. Waddell
(N.S.W.’s tragically-incompetent ex-Treas-
urer, who secured a gleam of cheap limelight
the other day by libelling a muzzled Federal
Judge) joined with Smith in threatening
civil war if the Referenda voting went
against “Liberalism.” When the fight in
connection with the Federal Capital site
was at its height, prominent solicitors and
such-like stated they would “stir up a
secession movement, with all its attendant
bloodshed,” if the district which a Commis-
sioner, appointed by their own State, had
declared to be the most suitable was selected
by the Australian Parliament. Joseph
Carruthers’s wire-stealing feat evoked
deafening cheers in the importing press and
other Anti-Labor circles. And, as a
final instance, at the time of writing
that form of insulting behavior which
is called Orangeism is in full swing.
Twenty-five per cent, of the inhabi-
tants of the Commonwealth are being mad-
dened, to no earthly purpose ; and the authors
of all this rage and grief are chiefly self-
righteous purveyors of religious bellow-
drama, who would sizzle with fury if one
hinted to them that they were common dis-
turbers of the peace. It suggests, among
other things, that in Australia our “upper
classes” are, in a grotesque sort of way, re-
verting to type. Originally the very rich
enjoyed what might almost be termed a
corner in deeds of violence. The poor might
pilfer and cheat furtively, conscious the
while that detection would mean a horrible
death, but they rarely aspired higher, in a
criminal manner of speaking. Assassina-
tion ; the fomenting of revolutions ; big, un-
abashed steals; the stirring up ot popu .ui
frenzy on a large scale —these were the
specialties of the rich, whether they hap-
pened to be Old Roman nobles, or Feudal
castle-owners, or members of princely Italian
houses. There are obvious differences between
these old-time swashbucklers and the rutile
suburban lawyers and clerics who yell fight
and slaughter in our midst to-day. But though
there is more iow comedy than tragedy about
these modern stirrers-up of strife, they are
capable of doing considerable damage if they
ale allowed to blither unchecked. Stern
measures (say a Bill imposing penal servi-
tude on all who incite the public to blood-
shed) would probably discourage politicians
like J. Wetherspoon, M.L.C., Bruce
Smith M.11.R., Waddell, M.L. A., etc.,
from behaving periodically like crosses be-
tween Penny Dreadful heroes and Pyrmont
larrikins.
The Failure to Grow Cotton and Other
Articles.
A recent Customs return, showing the
bounties paid by the Federal Government on
Australian products during the first three
quarters of last financial year, should set
patriotic Australians thinking. The items
were: —
Raw Products: Ginned cotton, £9l; flaxen hemp,
£67- sisal hemp, £112; oil from cotton seed, £39 ;
coffee, £112; tobacco leaf, £9B; dried fruits, £164;
combed wool tops, £696. .
Manufactures: Pig iron, £18,451; puddled iron,
£1917; steel, £1725; galvanised sheet-iron, £121;
wire-netting, from imported wire, £4149.
In none of the years since the bonuses were
offered have the claims been anywhere near
the amounts available. Thus:—
Amount Available. Amount Paid.
1907- .. £46,000 .. £1,107
1908- .. 97,000 .. 5,730
1909- .. 151,000 .. 38,202
1910- (three „ „„
quarters) •• 27,192
As the Bounties on Produce Act expires
next July, and as the attempt to foster the
production of these raw products has been a
much worse failure than that to encourage
the production of iron and steel, the I ed-
eral Government will have to consider and
decide just what it is prepared to do. See-
ing that the persuasive method has failed so
lamentably, the coercive might have more
effect. One way would be to impose an
import duty, so nicely adjusted _ that
it would discourage the importation of
the raw material, while even more
effectually hampering the importation of the
manufactured article. Also it should be
graduated so carefully that, while the im-
post would be moderate at first, its full
effect would be felt when the Australian
grower had had a reasonable time in which
to supply the demand. Or an increase of
the bonuses, together with an assurance of
their extension over a longer term, might
produce the effect desired. A man is hardly
likely to go into coffee-planting, for instance,
when he knows that the bonus may be shut
off at the end of live years, leaving him to
compete against the cheap labor of niggers
and such. So with cotton, hemp, and to-
bacco. Clearly, something has to be done.
Cotton-growing is just as feasible as sugar-
growing, and ought to be about three
more valuable; but it will not be started by
offering a bonus that may he chopped off
short in five years, just when the grower has
begun to feel his feet.

When the Australian Parliament imposed
its progressive land tax, one of the most
scandalous examples in the way of land mon-
opoly cited was the old Van Diemen’s Land
Co., which, for over half a century, had been
blocking land settlement in Tasmania.
The tax has not been in existence a year
yet; but it has already shifted this
dog out of his manger. A recent
cable announces that a Melbourne syndicate
has bought all the land —330,000 acres —for
subdivision. At the end of May last the
company held its annual meeting at London ;
and, on that occasion the chairman handed
out the following free testimonial to the
Labor party :
We much regret the land taxation recently brought in
by the Labor party in the Commonwealth Parliament.
. . . If the Act is ruled constitutional, it is certainly
going to have a serious effect upon our revenue, as
the extra taxation would amount to about £lO,OOO
per annum. When this legislation was first intro-
duced we saw at once that if it became law it would
be necessary to arrange for a more rapid development
of our estates.
This is a testimonial that the party should
frame and hang in a prominent place. An
Australian Fat chairman would have been
far too wily to give the show away in such
a fashion.
Hall Jon-es, Maoriland’s High Commis-
sioner in London, will shortly scratch his
name off the iront door and retire. So far
nobody has been named as his successor,
and, at latest, lou Ward was busy an-
nouncing that the appointment had been
hung up till his return to the Loan Land.
The suspicion that lousepii has an idea of
collecting the job for himself certainly
hasn’t been laid out by another denial on
his part of any such intention. lousepii
has protested too much about that
billet since he arrived in London. May-
hap the long voyage to Britain gave him
time to reflect that things are not so well
with him in M.L. as they appeared when
he looked at them from the end of a hundred
banquet tables, and listened to the loud
cheers that rose as the loan-built bridge was
declared open. Perhaps, too, the cold goods
handed to him by the Imperial Conference
have given him that political indigestion
which has led better men to lay up. Any-
how’, it is persistently stated that Attorney-
General Findlay, M.L.C., is going to make
another attempt to win a seat in the Repre-
sentatives at the November elections, and
the exact direction of Findlay’s ambition
is well known. In any case a reconstructed
Ministry cannot be far off; the wonder is
that the party has stood the present rattle-
trap concern so long. And when the recon-
struction starts, the proceedings are likely
to be far more strenuous than they have
been in M.L. politics since Seddon inaugur-
ated the system of knocking Cabinets to-
gether with a waddy. Also, M.L. is begin-
ning to have gloomy doubts of the borrow-
and-boom policy, and when it finally con-
cludes, as it will sooner or later, that Bust
is the next item on the programme, some-
thing extremely unpleasant will happen to
lou. Labor, meanwhile, is rapidly growing
out of the habit of taking for granted the
great advantages which the Liberals glow-
ingly explain are the rewards for wliole-hog
support. There have been advantages, but
nowadays they arrive with about the same
infrequency as angels’ visits; and at any
time in M.L. the advantages passed on to
Labor were light-weight compared with
the advantages given the commercial and
landed classes. Altogether there are all the
signs of a revolt against the political scheme
of the past 20 years, and. when it arrives,
London wall be a good place for lousepii
Ward to view it from.

Tite protest of Bill :
Last week the Ma State Government carted about
200 alleged delegates of the Farmers and Settlers’
Association conference to see the Burrinjuck dam.
The State —that is, plain Bill and Tom- —provided
sleeping berths for all hands. The Government—
or, rather, Bill and Tom —likewise fed this crowd.
Feed alone ran into the bigger part of £IOO, with-
out extras. A trainload of sleepers must have cost
the State — Bill and Ton again—several hundreds.
Why this waste of public money? Considering that
the same crowd spent over a week blackguarding the
same Government, it looks very like a case of loath-
some grovelling. As an advertising dodge, the busi-
ness was not worth five bob. The farmers that were
carted up are all landowners —and a narrow, conser-
vative, ignorant type at that. They are not the
class of men that will make the irrigation scheme
“good.” Neither will they have any influence on
the type of men who will make it good. The same
money expended in taking up a trainload of subur-
banites, with an eye turned longingly to the soil,
or a selected crowd of working-men representatives,
would have been infinitely more sensible. That, of
course, would have at once been severely censured by
the daily press as corrupt and immoral. Another
bad feature is that it is said that several of the
deadheads were not even delegates, and had no right,
to be present. For that matter, no one had any
right. The picnic was “worked” by friends, and a
complaisant Government licked the hand that had
been pulling its nose. Surely a Labor Government
might be expected to turn down this deadhead busi-
ness instead of encouraging it so grossly and inex-
cusably.

Thus Professor Hart, of Harvard (U.S.)
University, iu his book, “The Obvious
Orient” :
The really pressing question of English dominion
in the East is whether she can hold on in case of
European war, when the attempt would be made
to break off some of the British territories. Except
Aden, which is a first-class fortress, and Hongkong,
which has some batteries, not a single British Asiatic
port can protect itself; it all depends on the navy
and— unless Australia builds a fleet —that means a
navy built in England, sent from England, coaled as
far as Aden from England, and reinforced from Eng-
land. Every captain of the North German Lloyd
carries sealed orders, to be opened if war breaks
out; and somewhere —probably at Tsintan—are sup-
plies of guns and ammunition for their ships.
In other words, British dominion in the
East must rest largely upon the existence of
a powerful Australian navy. Nevertheless,
the blind men or the toadies who fought and
still fight against an Australian navy called
themselves Imperialists! In some times, in-
stead of being accepted as patriots, they
would have stood a much better chance of
being shot for traitors who were really try-
ing to give their beloved nigger a chance to
get back his own and a bit more to boot

“Tag” : I picked up recently, on the front
step, a book dropped by the 12-year-old
school-girl next door. Its title is “A School
Treasury of English Literature (Part I.),
selected by Walter Murdoch, M.A.” It is
published by Lothian, of Melbourne, but
printed in England. It is made up exclu-
sively of extracts from the works of English
and American poets and writers. Not one
single line is included from the works of
any Australian writer, living or dead;
yet at the back of this book of 21S
solid pages I find a sheaf of the same
publisher’s advertisements, including “Dainty
Editions of the Best Australian Poets,” in
which are listed Gordon’s “Sea Spray and
Smoke Drift,” Kendall’s Poems, O’Dowd's
Poems, “Busliland Ballads,” by E. J.
Brady, and other publications. The man
who collated this School Treasury claims, I
believe, to be an Australian; and the book,
although printed in England, is issued by an
Australian publisher, and is used in Aus-
tralian schools! On inquiry I find that
the various State Departments of Education,
and the teachers and trainers of Australian
youth generally, are persistently preserving
a “conspiracy of silence” as regards Austra-
lian Literature. Does this help to make
patriots? Is it the way to imbue the minds
of Young Australia with the idea that Aus-
tralia is a country worth working or fighting
for t There are plenty of patriotic Austra-
lian songmakers, and not a few good descrip-
tive writers, passages from whose works
ought to be, and some day will be, included
in every Australian school book.

Many, if not most, of the so-called Agricul-
tural Societies of Victoria are telling a
doleful tale of the lack of support they get
from farmers; and several of them, after in-
specting their overdrafts, are considering
the question of shutting up shop. Before do-
ing so, however, they are making a final
appeal to the local public and the farmers,
with a side-glance at the Treasury. The
truth is that the farmers and the public
which isn’t a farmer, have dropped to the
fact that very few agricultural shows have
any educational value at all; and if it were
not for the circus and sporting element
the high jumping, hunting and trotting
events —not one of them would draw a crowd
big enough to pay the gate-keepers’ wages.
The societies are usually bossed by men
who scarcely know a cow from a Berkshire
boar, and whose real object is to push the
Fatman along in politics. On the approach
of every election or by-election, or when an
important Bill is before Parliament, they
therefore meet and, in the name of the
farmers, pass resolutions against democratic
measures and proposals. During the Ref-
erenda campaign they spent quite a lot or
money in getting speakers, hiring halls and
so on, in ‘ the interests of machinery and
other rings, trusts and combines. The pub-
lic is naturally beginning to sniff at all this:

“I am pleased to say that I am VERY WELL
SATISFIED with the 6-H.P. engine I received
from you. I have given the engine two very
good trials, one being from Newcastle to Bung-
wahl, a distance of 80 miles, of which 22 were
by sea, without the least bit of trouble; and a
return trip from Newcastle to Clarence Town,
that being 84 miles in the one day, without even
a misfire. My boat is 26ft. x 6ft. 4in., heavy
planked, and gives a speed of 8 miles an hour.
The engine wiil run 7 hours at full speed on one'
tin of oil.”

Andrew Fisher has the whole tribe of
Union Jackals snapping at his heels. Talk-
ing to Stead —and he might easily have got
a better ear to pour his ideas into —he seems
to have said that “the Empire” wasn’t really
one Empire, but a loose bunch of five Domin-
ions ; and—repeating what Latjrier, of
Canada, said the other day, and what
S’Africa would say any day of any week —-
he added that the children, being adults,
would want to know what a row was about
before taking part in it. Given a just quar-
rel, Mother could rely upon the children for
support; given an unjust quarrel, the chil-
dren might take a hand, or they might not.
If they believed that the quarrel was so un-
just that help was impossible, then Fisher
recognised that the only alternative would
be to go their own way under their own flag.
The crowd that yells “My country right or
wrong,” distorting both Fisher’s argument
and Fisher’s conclusion, demands Andrew’s
scalp, though, if it told the truth, it would
say that it isn’t so much concerned about
getting Fisher’s scalp as it is about getting
a knife into the Labor party’s back. It
knows well that, once the cry of “Disloy-
alty !” is set going in earnest, thousands of
usually sane citizens become demented ; and
this political pack would just as soon have
the vote of a mad citizen as a sane one.
In the circumstances, and before the insanity
spreads, it will be as well for citizens
to ask themselves a question or two. For
instance :
Which is “my country”? Is it Australia, or is
it some other bit of the earth? If it is Australia,
isn’t that country ever going to have a mind of its
own? If it had a population of 50,000,000, would
it still be bound to follow blindly wherever the other
might lead-—even if it should lead into another
Opium War? And is it an.v more righteous for
5,000,000 to follow blindly than it would be for
50,000,000?
Also, if the Mother Dominion can rely absolutely
upon the children joining in her quarrels, whether
they understand anything about those quarrels or
not, and whether they approve of them or
not, what earthly reason is there for the Mother to
take the youngsters’ opinion? Australia, presum-
ably, want's to have her interests considered ; but
why should they be considered if Australia is “in
the bag” ?
Fisher is probably prepared to do more for
“the Empire” than the jackals who are yap-
ning at him. The only difference is that
Fisher wants to do right as well: and, if
be or his party is gibbeted, let. it be distinctly
understood that that is what it is for.
♦
This is how A. J. Balfour, leader of the
British Conservatives, talked just over a
month before Fisher made his alleged re-
marks on “Empire” :
Ido not quarrel with those —I do not accuse them
either of want of perception, imagination, or pat-
riotism—who say that the British Empire, as we
know it, is but a transitory arrangement . . . that
the time must come when these great and growing
communities will feel that all that could be gained
from the Empire as it used to be understood has
been gained, and that, in all kindness of heart, and
with every sympathy, each member of the Empire
had better go its own way like adult members of a
human family. _
In fact, it seems almost certain that the
view which Fisher submitted was the view
adopted at the Imperial Conference; so
that what he said is the Imperial view,
and those who howl at him (mostly tor
party purposes) present the anti-imperial
view. That, of course, would be simply re-
peating history ; it was just what liappenea
in connection with the Australian navy.
The Jingoes raved against that proposal, and
presently they woke up to the horrible fact
that the Labor narty had been presenting
the accredited Imperial view all the time.
J. Bull couldn’t understand Australia’s
White Australia policy once; but he is be-
ginning to get a glimmering ot it at last,
though so far it is only on the industrial side
that it appeals to him. Among others,
Houston, M.P., of the Houston line Liver-
pool, and one of the head serangs of British
shipping circles, is raising trouble about the
Yellow Pest on British ships, and pointing
out that it is about time the British Govern-
ment passed a Bill to save the British sea-
man. Chink sailors and firemen are in the
first place engaged in Chinese ports at about
a third of the pay of a Britisher, and ted
on the smell of an oil rag, with helpings ot
belaying-pin soup. When they get to Lon-
don or Liverpool they promptly swallow the
anchor,” and afterwards sign on _ another
ship for about £1 per month, displacing
white men, or compelling them to accept star-
vation wages —their wives and children getting
most of the starvation. Thus regular China-
towns, with joss-houses and all complete, are
rapidly growing round the Docks in London,
Liverpool, and other large ports, and the
usual social horrors connected with a
Chinese invasion can’t be far behind.
+,
If Victoria’s Willy Watt wanted a lesson
on the folly of making progressive legisla-
tion “optional”—which is what he proposes
to do with his Unimproved Land Values
Bating Bill—he got it the other day from
Berwick Shire Council. One reason why
the Murray State roads are such a disgrace
is that they have to carry loads for which
they weren’t built; that is to say, the load
is too heavy for the width of the tyre of the
vehicle that carries it. Parliament long ago
passed a Width of Tyres Act, which aims at
compelling the use of tyres wide enough to
help make the road, instead of cutting it up;
but the “farmers’ friends” got the measure
made “optional.” The other day Berwick
decided to enforce the Act in one riding,
where the roads were something extra-
awful ; but presently “great dissatisfaction
arose through the farmers being prevented
from carting as much as they would like on
ordinary spring drays with narrow tyres,”
so the Council instructed their officers to
hang the matter up. This paper’s Bill
Sikes is writing by first post to Watt on
the subject. Being naturally a virtuous
man and a Right Thinking person, he does
not mind the law setting its face against
burglary so long as he hasn’t a job on hand
himself'; but he thinks that, when he lias a
little burglary boom in full swing, O Calla-
giian should be instructed to go home and
go to sleep, and take all his men with him.

A cultivated person delivering a 12th of
July oration; an Archbishop conducting- a
Salvation Army disturbance; Melba singing
a coon song—such weird incongruities are
suggested by the new production at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney. The superb work of the
Irving Co. avails not to hide, but rather
shows up in strong relief, the imbecilities of
“The Lyons Mail.” And how many and
varied and preposterous they are! Not one
stock bellowdrama incredibility is missing.
Lesurques wishes to help his almost bank-
rupt father. He doesn’t say frankly: T
have more than I need. Take what you
want.” Instead, he creeps furtively to the
“THE LYONS MAIN' AT HER MAJESTY’S (SYDNEY).
Lesurques , sen. (Frank Tyars), mistakes the villainous Dubose (H. B. Irving) for his son.
The poor horse is perturbed at the mistake.
old man’s dwelling, and leaves a bag of gold
on the table. Dubose, Lesurques s double
(the human tiger who, despite years of
crime and dipsomania, so resembles the good
and temperate Paris banker that none can
distinguish the one from the other), carefully
omits to slay either Joliquet or Jeanette,
though he has the best of chances to exter-
minate both of them, and though his life must
be in danger so long as they are alive.
Lesurques watches a procession of witnesses
of unimpeachable honesty —amongst them his
own father —identify him as the malefactor
who robbed the mail and murdered the guard,
yet the explanation of the mystery never
strikes him. He is prepared to go to the
scaffold without so much as hinting to Magis-
trate Dorval, his lifelong friend, that the real
offender is possibly some needy ruffian bear-
ing a resemblance to him. Dubose stays in
Paris to be seized, when, by all the laws of
caution, lie should be anywhere else on earth.
And so on throughout the tiresome drivel.
H. B. Irving is the most, natural and
charming hero of melodrama in the world
till his troubles begin. Thereafter he rants
and snarls, and calls on “Gard” to proclaim
his innocence, with the utmost staginess. Plis
Dubose is a grim and convincing character-
one who freezes the marrow of all but the
veriest cynics. It is significant that the
audiences which crowd to Her Majesty’s re-
serve their loudest cheers for the situation
where Dubose dodges behind a door and re-
appears with amazing slickness as Lesurques.
Evidently to them the player is not so much
the thing as the quick-change artist. Miss
Dorothea Baird plays Jeanette with much
emotional force. It is impossible to speak
too warmly of the rest of the cast. Eric
Maxon’s Courriol (who preserves a subtle
suggestion of relentless villainy even when
he is most foppish) ; Arthur Whitby’s Chop-
pard, and the Fouinard and Durochat of Tom
Reynolds and W. Gibbs are excellent. So
are the Jerome Lesurques of Frank Tyars
and the Dorval of Henry Yibart. Miss Irene
Browne, Arthur Gurtis and Stanley How-
lett all do fine work. Never, in fact, does
the writer remember to have seen so much
intelligence and meticulous care and technical
skill lavished on such piffle.
** * *
“Sergeant Brue” returned to the Criterion
on Saturday night in the custody of Percy
Clifton, Edwin Brett having proved an
aliU. Also Willie Cromwell, who 15
months ago was in business as Crooky
Scrubbs, has sold out, and Tom Graves has
taken on the job of helping the Sergeant to
become an inspector, so that he may save
his brother’s fortune from reverting to the
Society for the Propagation of the Habit of
Wearing Braces am oner the Kaffirs. Ruth
Lincoln (Lady Bickenhall) has transferred
her right, title and interest in the heir to
Florence Imeson; and, among many other
changes in the C. and M. Co., Nell Finnis
limps on with an injured foot to help get
the Sergeant’s tea. The piece itself is one
of the best of the new crop of musical come-
dies. It has a coherent plot, and for the
most part the lyrics don’t have to chloro-
form the story in order to get a hearing.
The dialogue, moreover, actually sparkles
in spots, and the music is tuneful. The
principals might get better support at times;
on Saturday night a good-tempered house,
recognising that in particular Brue’s police-
men wanted more rehearsal, brought them
back three times to see that they had it.
The success of the manoeuvre suggests
whether the most profitable house of the
average theatrical season wouldn’t be the
one that could be got, say, a week before
the first night.

“The Balkan Princess” promises to estab-
lish a record reign at Sydney Theatre Royal
unless she is burnt or dynamited out of the
house. The crowds at this show are even
thicker and more enthusiastic than they
were seven weeks ago.
