At the Adelphi (Sydney) George Marlow’s
Co. continues to hint at a connection between
a vegetable diet and the birthrate ; and this
and the other points of interest in “The
Mother of His Child” are -not likely to be
blunted before the next issue of The Bulle-
tin rolls around.
** * *
The noiseless ghosts of the Coronation are
now passing across the sheet at Sydney
Tivoli, and, for a reasonable sum, the plain
citizen who stayed at home and minded his
job may witness the best part of the expen-
sive show, less the color, noise and incon-
venience. The black and white procession
Avhich trundles out of the one-eyed hissing
machine lacks the pantomime glitter of the
original; but it is a fine thing nevertheless,
and the spectator with corns relishes the
thought that none of the police horses can
back out of the picture and stamp on his
feet. The living people of the show are
practically the same as last week. Binns
and Binns, the graceful Alcide Capitaine,
Spissell Brothers and Mack, Keldine and
Klimo and comedian Kalman divide. the
capital letters among them. The two Lillies,
Fanny Powers and the Australian Dartos
are also mentioned.
** m *
The monkeys of Anita Diaz are packing
their grip-sacks preparatory to leaving the
National Amphitheatre; and Miss Toby
Claude is also shortly due to remove her
brief skirts elsewhere. On the other hand,
Millie Doris continues to evoke shouts of
amazement and delight, and the ball-
punchers from America remain among the
notables present.
#* * *
Coronation pictures everywhere are flick-
ing overtime in their hurry to let Sydney
see them before they have their heels trod-
den on by the scurrying feet of Time.
West’s (Palace and Princess) and Spen-
cer’s (Lyceum) have commenced continuous
shows; and Williams’s Lyric and Colonial
1 and 2 worK 12 hours a day. Besides a
fine film of the procession to and from West-
minster Abbey, West’s show the sceptre,
the spoon, the crown, the orb, etc., with
their respective bearers. The usual pro-
gramme of comedy and drama is provided
for other tastes. The Lyric, besides the
usual mixed pictures, has several thou-
sand feet of Royal procession, colonial
troops and presentation of medals; also
some new pictures of the Prince of Wales
going for his Garter. Spencer’s Lyceum
gallery includes pictures of Mary and her
husband passing through the crowds. At
tne matinee on Saturday the Lyceum will
burst into song as an accompaniment to the
shifting scenery.
** * *
A non-professional “Hook of Holland”
performance at Melbourne Princess’s sug-
gests that an amateur’s shortest way to
efficiency in broad-comedy mumming is the
way of mimicry, wherein Albert Whelan
was a great Australian pioneer. Whelan
formed so complete an imitation of Oscar
Girard, the original old Bronson in “The
Belle of New York,” that he could take the
sick man’s place without being detected in
the fraud. He croaked like a raven when
he could have sung like a tenor, and was
content to produce only a close copy of the
original when he might have improved upon
it. Possibly the amateur Ottey, who imi-
tates Cromwell as Simon Slinks, could be
more droll than Cromwell ever was. Cer-
tainly he adapts a moderately musical voice
to the mimicry of tones which were rasp-
ingly saw-like, and in his various tricks of
Cromwellian absurdity he seems less me-
chanical. Anyway, the amateur Slinks
is an interesting result. Likewise the Mina
of this revival (Miss Daisie Gove), who
copies the business of Emmeline Orford
whilst displaying a marked resemblance to
Celia Ghiloni. With her strong, mellow
voice, good looks, bright intelligence, and a
turn for dancing, Miss Gove raises her part
of the show to the professional standard.
The Ludwig Schnapps and Captain Paap
are quite up to their work in everything but
confidence; the Gretchen is a nice girl in a
minor part, and for the rest of the princi-
pals there is nothing very wicked to say.
Miss Hook has a sweet soprano voice which
shares the blame with a “scratch” orches-
tra when it gets out of tune, and she is
charmingly natural as an amateur; the
bandmaster suggests a melodious curate try-
ing to do his best; and Mr. Hook—a weird
person with a strange enunciation—is to be
pitied for having a part in which he was
bound to be dull. He “creates” a char-
acter, but the creation is weak and weari-
some. A chorus of 40-odd brings plenty of
intelligence and fresh young vocalism to
bear on the proceedings, but is deficient in
fresh young beauty. Scenery and props are
borrowed from the C. and M. production,
and the stage management earns more
laurels than the orchestra, which, however,
is not too bad.

Her Majesty’s is the place where nothing
new is to happen at the end of next week.
But on that eventful first Saturday in
August the Melbourne career of “Our Miss
Gibbs” will close. Miss Gibbs was lately
said to have come to stay, but now she is
getting ready to go. Before “Hamlet” ar-
rives from Sydney, on the 2Gth, the audi-
torium of J.C.W.’s broad, expansive play-
house will be built in, as it were, with a
new proscenium arch, and two lots of private
boxes will come to pass where two barren
wastes of space are now located. These de-
sirable alterations will keep the house in the
hands of the architect for three weeks.

Local manufacturing industry in melo-
drama is getting further encouragement at
the King’s. “The Man from Outback” ap-
pears to grip Anderson’s devoted public
closer to its Australian heart than it did
aforetime. The villains pursue their evil
course with a more familiar knowledge of
the track, and Redgrave has naturally
grown more desperate in his misguided efforts
to thwart them. Also, Bailey has added
new touches of human feeling to his imper-
sonation of a young man who stimulates his
avowals of love with bush whisky, and some-
times squeezes the bottle until the wardrobe
in his bedroom changes into Noah’s Ark, and
the lions and unicorns walk out on stilts.
“The Man from Outback” will escape from
the burning hut for yet another week after
this.

The German attitude on the Moroccan
trouble is justified by her power. The
nations which neglect military preparations
must always submit to dictation. The wisest
nations develop their powers for the futher-
ance of justice and progress. And there is
no limit to the wise progress of the pro-
gressive nations which are inspired to their
best wisdom by D.C.L., the Whisky of the
Wise.*

Spencer’s Melbourne picture house has
been starring "The Tramp,” a yarn in
which the humor is more conspicuous than
the plot. Other stories that appeal to the
domestic instincts are “His Mother’s Scarf”
and “Winning the Step-children.” Among
topical troubles the Gartering o£ the youth-
ful Wales has served as a sort of introduc-
tion to the Coronation avalanche; and The
Bulletin can only express its sincere sym-
pathy with the central figure, who seemed
to be cruelly weighed down by a robe and
a hat at least nine sizes too large for him.
*# * *
“Civil War,” a four-act play by Ashley
Jukes, a new writer, was given its first Aus-
tralian hearing by the Literary Theatre
peonle in Adelaide. It is a kind of political
“Romeo and Juliet.” His father is a hide-
bound pedant, a country baronet with ideas
of bringing up a youth on Plato. Her
father is a red-hot Socialist (English brand),
who curses the Rich, but doesn’t do anything.
At the end, a previously quiet person takes
charge of the play, and annoys (and sur-
prises) them intensely by pointing out
that they are both cranks, and make a very
well-matched pair. So they agree to let the
young people run in double harness too.
There is too much talk in the play, but only
one bit of it sticks in the memory. “Send a
youth to Eton and Oxford, and he’ll go
through life acting as if the eyes of Eton
and Oxford are upon him. Bring him up on
Plato, and he’ll act as though the eyes of
Plato were upon him ; and he’s as likely as
not to kill a policeman !” The rendering of
the play is not up to the usual high-all-
round standard of the Literary crowd. James
Anderson was good as the Socialist, and the
lovers (Len Chamberlain and Ethel Lewis)
were-natural; but two players of proved
capacity were much at sea this time, in parts
that did not suit them.
** * *
“The Woman in the Case,” at Adelaide
Royal, is “superior melodrama.” (The chief
difference between the superior and the or-
dinary kind is that the former does not need
a Villain, human weakness or Fate being-
enough to battle against.) The acting is
strong, the two ladies, Mabel Trevor and
Elinor Foster, having most of the chances.
Buckler’s lawyer seems younger than the
author probably meant, just as Kay Souper’s
husband is probably older; but they play up
to the woman antagonists strongly. Buckler
gets more scope in “A Fool,” which has
started on a vampire week. Leonard Bor-
wick continues at the Exhibition, and Ade-
laide is gradually waking up to the fact that
he is one of the world’s first XL at the
piano. Elsewhere all is Coronation films.
Even the Tivoli has given in to the popular
clamor, and stars a picture in between the
Black and White Girls, wire-walking Delino,
Fred Bluett, and the other favorites.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Sydney City Council is abolishing theatre queues,
“except in those cases where the proprietors open
the doors at least three hours before the advertised
time of commencing the performance, and at their
fixed prices for each particular class of seat.” The
“stacker” must go too, because the next reform
foreshadowed by the City Council is the numbering
of seats. When that happens every buyer of admis-
sion will buy a specified seat, which will mean the
stoppage of ticket-selling as soon as the numbered
seating capacity is exhausted. With the “stacker”
out of the way, theatres will accommodate fewer
people, and new Saturday goose-shows will be started
for the overflow, or they’ll spend their .money in drink
-—until they learn that Monday or Wednesday is as
good a night to be in a full theatre as Saturday used
to be. People will spend as much on amusement, but
they won’t spend most of it on Saturdays, and they’ll
have time to be more critical, and that ought to
mean better stuff before long.
Ethel Irving began her career as “little Birdie
Irving,” a child dancer, and from that she worked
on to the Gaiety, where she appeared in “Ruy Bias”
with Leslie and Farren.
“One Who Pays”: Ethel Irving may be likened
to Raphael and Goethe in this way: you are not so
impressed with first acquaintance as you are when
you come to do some thinking afterwards. Then you
pick up skeins of Duse, Ellen Terry and Mrs. Pat
Campbell. Ethel resolutely ignores stage traditions,
and even matters thought essential in technique.
Do we not know how the star enters? About five or
10 minutes after the play starts there is a visible
clearance of the stage. On comes Julius, or Julia,
for the big round. Ethel Irving slinks on with her
back to the audience, and just sidles up the stage;
so she does not get a hand. Nor is there any ap-
plause throughout the play, except when Hie curtain
has descended. She wipes out any possible chance
of applauding just as Wagner did. Yet, to my
mind, the drama is a bit underacted by all hands.
We have a London replica, whereas it is. usual for
Londoners in Australia to cayenne a little; and
Williamson will advise them to do it.
Adelina Patti, 68. gave a concert in London re-
cently for the benefit of William Ganz, the “Sing,
Sweet Bird” composer. The old lady’s singing of
two operatic numbers and a couple of ballads is
said to have been wonderful for depth of tone and
clearness of enunciation, considering the present age
of her voice and the early age at which she started
to use it in opera. Fifty years ago she appeared as
La Sonnambula at Covent Garden, and previously she
had starred in America. The story of Patti is an
eternal satire on the modern musical criticism
which discovers great “promise” in the warble of a
25-year-old soprano, and tells her to expect a great
future after she has persevered with her studies for
a few more j r ears. Also, it is a sad reminder to
Melba of the time she lost before starting her
career. The “finishing” lessons she got from Mar-
ch esi when she was 26 or more might have accom-
plished just the same result for the healthy, early-
developed songstress when she was only 18.
“Snell”: “C. Ockney,” having dumped himself in
judgment on me, is thrown into a sort of brain
storm (B. 20/7/’ll) over my reasonable effort to
upset his verdict. After getting through 20 lines of
excitement, I find the gist of his argument, in these
sentences: “I submit there is only one right way
with the playing of Peter Doody. If the original is
not copied, there is simply no part.” There was no
part when the first actor took in hand the author’s
script. The London pro. created it. . The other sing-
ing Comedians .who couldn’t do likewise, putting
their own craft, their own intelligence, and their
own personalities into the realisation of the figure
as it appeared on paper, are not actors, and are un-
worthy of a critic’s respect. If what “C. Ockney,”

“Mime”: Someone has written to Sydney. Tel-
egraph claiming that the gallery at Her. Majesty’s,
Sydney, corresponds to the upper circle in. most of
the West End of London theatres, and hence is cheap
at the present entrance price. The nerve Of him.
The upper circle at (say) His Majesty s, London,
bears about as much resemblance to the ga lerv at
Her Maiesty’s, Sydney, as Beer does to Mud. You
can book at the libraries for the upper circle in
London, and turn up three minutes before the show
starts and be led to your seat by a civil usher ; the
seat is roomy and otherwise comfortable, and the
occupier’s head is not over against the. roof of the
playhouse. The horror of having to wait sometimes
for hours, in a draughty street, if one is to get a
decent view of the show; the crush; the infuriating
manoeuvres of that barbarous institution, the
packer” ; and the crowded and insanitary atmo-
sphere—all these combine to render our Australian
galleries fair imitations of Sheol shortly after a
flood has occurred in the Yang-Tse-Kiang.

Inez Bensusan, formerly of Sydney, is nowadays a
shining light of the Oncomers’ Society (London).
This body consists largely of players and playwnters
who are coming on along a side-track, and find out-
let for their talent at the Little Theatre. At a re-
cent performance of the Oncomers the. most success-
ful of four items on the bill was a little drama, of
Breton life by Miss Bensusan, commended for being
both sane and humanly interesting. So many of
the playlets produced at the same theatre, by one or
other society, are either insanely unconventional or
conventionally feeble.
“L.H.P.”: Goodness knows how many English
versions there are of “The Courier of Lyons.” The
best is undoubtedly Charles Reade’s, known as “The
Lyons Mail.” This denouement is the most effective.
In the original French play, produced in 1850, Les-
urques is about to be led to the guillotine as the
curtain falls, and Dubose’s fine closing scene, as it
is acted in Reade’s version, has no existence. The
dual role of Dubose and Lesurques has not always
been done by one actor. In the London Standard
production of 1851 (the first in England), J. Rayner
was Lesurques and R. Honner Dubose. Thq, cele-
brated brothers Webb, who were best known as the
Two Dromois in Shalcspeare’s “Comedy of Errors,”
made a big noise in the English provinces in the
’so’s, with a version that showed Henry as the vil-
lain and Charles as Lesurques. Squire Bancroft was
the Courriol of that show. Sir John Hare was also
in the cast. Charles Kean was the first London star
of note to make a hit in the piece; in that cast was
Jimmy Cathcart, who in later years was an Austra-
lian favorite. Wilson Barrett’s wife, Miss Heath,
was the Jeanette of Kean’s show.
From London: —The journalistic humors of the
Backblocks have figured in many a Bulletin, but no-
body has so far tried to make a drama out of them.
There are possibilities in the idea. Lady Gregory,
the patroness of the Irish National Theatre, has just
produced at the Court, London, a little piece of the
kind. Rival editors in a remote Irish village agree
to end a long quarrel by dining together; but be-
fore the meal is over they quarrel worse than ever.
They angrily snatch up their overcoats, or think
they do; but the fact is that in their heat and tem-
per each takes the other’s garment. It so happened
—the “long arm of coincidence,” you know—that
each had in the pocket of his overcoat a candid
obituary notice of his rival, ready to put into print
at a moment’s notice. The discovery of these obi-
tuaries and the reading of them aloud constitutes
the fun of the piece. It is a far-fetched idea, but
distinctly humorous while it lasts, although it may
not bear thinking over afterwards. One of the new
pieces presented by the Irish players during their
London season at the Court is “The Casting Out of
Martin VVnelan.” Martin is a wealthy Irish-Aus-
tralian, who returns to the old sod with a plan of
bis own for the regeneration of Ireland. But the
Cork farmers to whom he expounds it treat it with
derision and disdain, and when someone discovers
that Martin’s grandfather was a traitor and an
informer the fat is in the fire, and Ireland is made
too sultry for him. “What a people! What a
country!” Martin exclaims as the curtain comes
down on his immediate return to Australia.
“W.F.”: Re the revival of the story of Margaret
Catchpole on the London stage. On Easter Monday
of 1844, Margaret Catchpole plays were produced
at the Surrey and Victoria Theatres in London. I,
then 12 years old, saw both. At the Surrey, Will
Land (Margaret’s sweetheart) was played by Henry
Hughes, and Margaret herself by Mrs. R. Honner.
The artists were among the best on the London
boards of that day. The following Monday I saw the
Victoria version, which was in four acts, the last
laid in Australia. Harry Howard, a fine picturesque
actor, was William Land, the smuggler. Miss Vin-
cent, then called “the heroine of domestic drama,”
was Margaret Catchpole. In the fourth act, laid in
Australia, Cony and Blanchard, famous swordsmen,
played two aboriginal chiefs, and fought as to who
should possess Margaret Catchpole. I know now
that thev were much more like North American
Indians than Australian natives. Those versions so
impressed me that I’m sure if they were as well
played now they would be a great success.
“C. Ockney”: Charles Frohman, the theatrical
boss, has been talking to a Referee (London)_ in-
terviewer much in the style of our own Bill Squires.
He says that after looking out for a long time for a
“play with a punch,” he has found it at last. It is
“The Butterfly on the Wheel,” in which Lewis Waller
is at present playing at the Globe. Waller will
doubtless bring it along with him when he embarks
for these shores next year. I shan’t be surprised if
I meet “Boshter Bill” among the audience on the
first night.
The association of mane and music is practically
at an end. Leonard Borwick says there is now more
willingness on the part of the public to listen to a
bald pianist than ever there was, and a musician
may at last have his hair cut with comparative
safety.
LEONARD BORWICK, PIANIST.
“A.W.R.”: “Vulcan” (B. 13/7/T1) quotes a
London paper’s announcement of the arrival .of
“Cleopatra, the Australian snake-charmer,” and asks,
“Is she yet another of the ‘Australian artists who
gained their experience in the ’roo-infested wilds of
Clapham?” No, she isn’t. She has been “show-
ing” around Australia for years, and for a while
got into the Rickards halls. Two others were
usually on the same menu—Bonita, a skilful girl
rifle-shot, and a gentleman known as Don Carlos, the
latter being generally accepted as the father of the
two girls. I don’t think they are Australian-born
performers ; more likely Spanish-Americans.
“Heavy Lead”: A family of showmen in the South
have been rechristened the Buttinskis, owing to their
habit of butting in whenever other and usually
smaller men have found or invented a payable pitch;
and the daughter of a small bioscope showman was
made bitter thereby. One day she was selling the
weekly product of dead marines to the “bottle-oh
man. The mariner boasted that trade was good, ( and
that sometimes he “made four quid a week.” For
Heaven’s sake keep it to yourself,” said the moving-
picture girl. “If the Buttinskis get to hear of it,
you’ll be ruined.”
“Star-azer”: It is a kindness to whisper to Mrs.
Brough that she does not know how to make up, and
never did. Mrs. B. is a pleasing actress, and an ex-
cellent craftswoman, and thousands of Australians
owe her a debt of gratitude for past favors; yet
most actresses with a twentieth part of her ex-
perience can give her points in making-up the face
to counteract footlight vagaries. I remember this
defect existing even in the Boucicault days, and see
it exaggerated in “Nobody’s Daughter” and “Lover s
Lane” nowanights, where it has happened that the
effects produced were nine times out of 10 exactly
the opposite to those the actress sought.

Dr. Orville Wright, the Baconspeare crank, con-
tinues to waste pots of money in his attempt to
prove that Francis Bacon was the real Divine Wil-
liam. Workmen employed by Wright to discover
documents buried in the bed of the river Wye (Eng.)
have lighted on the foundations of an ancient Roman
bridge, and now they are shifting to a shallower
part of the river. This digging in the Wve seems
appropriate. Wye, O wye should F. Bacon be for-
ever suspected of having taken extraordinary pains
to transfer his alleged authorship of “Hamlet,” etc
to an impudent pretender, whilst he was taking even
more trouble to plant cipher keys to the mystery in
river-beds'! 1 The most astounding foolishness of Or-
ville Wright’s delusion is his hope of bracketing
Ben Jonson, Beaumont and Fletcher and others with
the “impostor” Shalcspeare. Poor old Shale’s claims
to his own writings has been soberly questioned on
the ground that no one man could have done so
much, but the crank theorists are giving Bacon
credit for about twice as much as the Bard ever put
his name to.

“Vulcan”: Tenor Caruso, some time back, became
violently “mashed” on a little Milanese milliner,
and the correspondence became, as he says, “vairy
intimeet.” The lady dreamed of orange blossoms,
etc. ; the tenor was thinking of other things. The
lady shrieked “breach” ; the tenor scoffed. After
an ominous silence, the lady sweetly inquired what
the full publishing rights of (say) “The Passionate
Love Letters of a Middle-Aged Tenor” were worth.
The operatic luminary hurriedly replied that they
would fetch £2OOO. The lady thought they were
worth twice that. And the last indications were
that the stricken warbler would agree with her !
“0.K.”: In saying that “nobody seems to have
definite ideas about the result of the reckless picture
competition which is coming along,” I meant nobody
who has a knowledge of the character of the business.
“G.L.C.” (B. 20/7/’ll) says that “The flicker shows
will all finish up precisely as those in London and
N’Yark have done, i.e., give way to an interlarding
of variety show,” as it was in the beginning. But
“G.L.C.’s” definite idea is obviously false. The
picture-shows have not finished up like that in Lon-
don. Take the advertised time-tables of the Oxford,
Tivoli and Pavilion music-halls for last month. The
bioscope at each hall was the last item on the pro-
gramme—ll.ls p.m., 11 p.m., 11 p.m. And so it
was elsewhere. Pictures at the ’alls are for the
people who come early to get a cheap seat, and stay
late to get their full money’s worth ; and meanwhile
the shows which are wholly pictorial continue to
flourish. What “G.L.C.” seemingly doesn’t know is
that the solid bulk of patronage for picture theatres
comes from the class that rarely, or never, ventures
into a place where girls dance in scant attire, and
jokes are sometimes “blue.” In fact, many of the
most persistent picture-eaters would sooner die and
go straight to Heaven than enter a music-hall and
be d —d.
“Fungoid”: Henry Neil Warner is referred to in
“Poverty Point.” I knew a relative of his, a Mrs.
Harvey, who told me his name was Lockwood. He
was no relative of Charles Warner, whose real name
was Lickfold. H. N. Warner first came prominently
before the Melbourne public at the opening of the
Theatre Royal, in 1855, when he acted Charles Sur-
face in “The School for Scandal.” Later, he dropped
too much into imitating Brooke—actors get hypno-
tised by superior men. Warner went to America,
and did well for many years. His wife, a beautiful
lady of high culture, was much honored.

Premiers remain in the flesh. The first in
point of importance is old George Turner,
who still potters about in well-worn clothes
The second is Iceberg Irvine; the other
the raucous Peacock.

The just-dead relict of Melbourne’s first
brewer (John Mills) must have established
a record for widows. Under John’s will she
got all his property, including the brewery
and an adjoining pub in Flinders-lane, and
this pitch became immensely valuable. On
her death the estate was to be shared among
the daughter and brothers and sisters of
deceased. But she lived to see all the re-
.versionists go under, whilst Flinders-lane
was being made richer and richer for the
ground landlords. In fact, she married
again, and lived for full 70 years after the
death of brewer Mills.
Though he had ability of a kind, George
Jenkins, late Clerk of Vic. Parliament, was
the most humorless of persons, and took
his precious title with awful seriousness.
Just after he had received it, he made the
rounds to receive the congratulations of the
barmaids. One of them, at one house, pro-
fessing to be unaware of his new nobility,
greeted him with “Good morning, Mr. Jen-
kins ! It’s quite an age since we saw you.”
The fatuous Jenkins, stepping out of ear-
shot of his companions, beckoned the
lady to lean across the counter, and, beam-
ing on her as though he were paying a com-
pliment, whispered, “Call me ‘Sir George.’ ”
It didn't strike him that the demure Hebe
would turn the incident to anecdotal ac-
count. But she did ; wherefore Jenks’s plain-
tive appeal remains as his epitaph—“ Call
me Sir George.”
The cantankerous Wade disgusted a few
more of his alleged followers the other day by
raising a scare over the State’s most recent
IO U’s. He buzzed that the action of
xlcting-Treasurer Carmichael, in signing cer-
tificates of inscribed stock, was quite illegal,
on the ground that Carmichael wasn’t a port-
folioed Minister, and had no Constitutional
status. Thereabouts Carmichael, who is a
duly sworn-in member of the Executive
Council, produced proof that Wade, as At-
torney-General in the Carruthers Govern-
ment, had raised no question of legality when
“Billy” Dick was signing stock certificates,
though Dick didn't even have a seat in the
Executive Council. Wade lapsed into a
profound silence, whilst his “Liberal” host
asked itself what sort of a leader it was who
blundered into glaringly obvious ditches like
that.
THE ETERNAL TEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I saw General Booth. He put a lovely kiss on
my face, and said, ‘That is for Commissioner Hay.’
When I arrived home I gave it to the Commisioner.
(Laughter.) The General gave me another kiss and
said, ‘That is for Mrs. Hay.’ When I arrived here I
offered it to her.” (Great laughter.)— -“Colonel”
Hoskin, at a Salv'army beano in Melbourne.
In ancient Eden, I believe,
When fig-leaves first became “de rigger,”
With certain smiles and signals Eve
Made Adam snigger.
I wonder why it is, so oft,
When Cupid’s cup is there to quaff,
His offer should provoke a soft
And silly laugh?
Because it seems (as you’ll agree)
An anti-climax very stupid
To let the rosy angel be
A giggling Cupid !
ITow beastly, when in amorous toils
You’ve fairly caught the teasing Miss,
To meet the little laugh that spoils
The hard-won kiss !
The playwright finds, when in a jet
Of passion rare his soul he’s pouring,
Canoodling on the stage will set
The audience roaring.
And even when our Wowser friends
Are praising God with fearful sound,
The merest hint of cuddle sends
A chuckle round.
The custom’s old ; deserves no taunt;
Is necessary, too. Then, drat it,
Why in the devil should we want
To giggle at it?
Yet vainly do I deal my stroke,
For fools still gurgle in their necks
Before this never-resting joke,
The joke of Sex.
VICTORIA.

TAKEN SICK IN SYDNEY.
“When in Sydney some time ago I had a very bad
attack of indigestion, with sickness lasting for some
hours,” writes Mrs. J. F. Holmes, 231 Auburn Road,
Hawthorn, Vic. “It left me so weak I was unable
to go to business. A friend gave me a sample of
Chamberlain’s Tablets, and after taking three tablets
I was so much better that I was able to resume
work, and in less than a week I was quite well.”*
At an Extraordinary General Meeting of O.T.
Limited, it was unanimously passed to increase the
capital of the company by the issue of 160,000
shares of 10s. each. The object of increasing the
capital is for the purpose of acquiring the London
business, and to develop and extend trade in every
country in the world. 100,000 shares of 10s. will
be offered for public subscription, payable ss. on
application and ss. on allotment. The Prospectus
will be issued at the beginning of August,*
SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
A Victorian P.M. wearied the other day of
the persistent adulteration of milk by a Mel-
bourne dairyman, and, it being the swindler’s
eighth conviction, fined him £5O, and ordered
the conviction to be notified in a daily paper.
This was following the French plan of
making such swindlers notify the public of
their conviction by placarding their shop
doors; but as it was this man’s eighth con-
viction, and as most if not all of the pre-
vious ones had been reported in the papers,
the penalty apparently has little effect.
Many people, it seems, on reading of Smith’s
conviction, remark: “Oh, Smith has just
been fined ; he’ll probably sell good milk for
a while now” ; and Smith trades on that as-
sumption and recoups himself. A better
plan is the Cold Country one of putting
Smith in gaol after his second conviction,
and giving him more and more gaol every
time thereafter. Gaol is a shockingly bad
market for dangerous goods of any other
than the human variety.

The Queensland Government has settled
with the owners of the Waipara, who were
suing the State for £lOO,OOO damages caused
to their vessel while in charge of a Govern-
ment pilot. The Government denied lia-
bility and responsibility, but has compro-
mised by paying a comparatively small sum
as compensation, reserving the right to still
disclaim legal liability. This decision, while
it probably will save Bananaland and the
co. much good money in Costs, leaves in
doubt the question as to the liability of the
State which makes shipmasters employ a
“compulsory” pilot in pilotage waters. Most
of the States when handing over a valuable
ship and cargo to the tender care of a pilot
calmly disclaim any responsibility. Vic-
toria limits a pilot’s liability to £lOO. But
as Victoria as well as the other States
compels the engagement of a pilot, it has
been held by shipowners that the Govern-
ment cannot dodge its liability at, law by
merely passing an Act. Still, the F. and O.
wasn’t game to tackle the legal problem
when the Australia was lost at Port Phillip
Heads. Now the parties in the Waipara
case have funked it. As a proposition in
Costs this question when it is settled will
probably be a record.

A Melbourne woman, lunching at a city
restaurant, found a mouse’s head in a pie,
and writes to the papers complaining about
it. Women in these matters are so un-
reasonable. Now, a man would have been
quite satisfied that a head was all he had
any right to expect for the money.
“Billy B.”: I’m overpowered with the
scheme to erect a “dignified” statue to the
late Edward in Sydney, especially the sug-
gestion to plant it at the top of Moore-street,
when that thoroughfare is completed through
to Macquarie-street. It is amazing with
what tenacity flunkeyism clings to life in
this picket nation of Democracy, and as-
tounding with what tolerance it is allowed
to masquerade as “the feeling of the people.”
Who has heard “the people” clamor or even
whisper for 50 or 60 tons of tombstone to
any king to be dumped down in the fair-
way of one of their streets?
P.M.G. Thomas will probably turn with
unholy joy to Sidney Colvin’s “Letters of
Robert Louis Stevenson,” just published,
and look up a letter from Vailima to Henry
James, written in 1891. Therein Stevenson
laments that things have disappeared in the
“capacious maw of the Post Office,” and adds :
“Many letters both from and to me I know
now to have been lost in transit. My eye is on
the Sydney Post Office, a large, ungainly
structure with a tower, as being not 100
miles from the scene of disappearance.”
Thank Heaven, Federation can’t be held re-
sponsible for what happened in 1891 —days
when dear old Dan O’Connor was letting
his whiskers flow out over the post office
carvings, what time he wasn’t writing a
minute to the effect that his under-sec. had
better “app’int Maloney!”
The S. Aus. Supreme Court has given a
sensible decision about some irregularities in
a family ’ An old lady left all her Property
*° J lo ' andcllildren, but these proposed to
exclude (1) a cluld admittedly born before
marriage, (2) another born three years after
the separation of husband and wife. The
< 'ourt said that the old lady knew all about
the first inopportune infant, and doubtless
meant it to have a share; and that the second
was (in the absence of strict proof) entitled
to assume that its legal father had been “in
the vicinity at the time it was begotten.”
The newspapers, by the way, were pointedly
requested to suppress the names, while pub-
fishing the principles.
The outcome of Auckland's (M.L.) recent
discoveries in regard to its food supply is
that, for the future, the City Council will
have control of the city’s food and drink
production under last session's Pure
Food and Drugs Act; and the municipal
inspector is to advise the Licensing Com-
missioner as to the condition of hotel food-
factories. When it was pointed out that the
yellow market-gardeners in one suburb
“cleaned” their vegetables in a stream that
also accepted sewage, one excited councillor
declared for letting Lionel Terry out with a
roving commission, a gun, and the run of an
ammunition factory.
THE RIVALS.
One of Victoria’s policemen lately made a
painful exhibition of the system under which
he works. He watched a “suspicious”
stranger enter a Caulfield bike shop, where,
it seems, the stranger told the person in
charge that he had called for his bike. The
man behind the counter disclaimed all know-
ledge of a bike, and while they were arguing
the point the Law came forth and arrested
the stranger on a totally irrelevant charge of
“having no lawful means.” After being
locked up for a week the man was presented
at court, when it was proved (1) that he
had actually left his bike at that shop to be
repaired; (2) that he was in regular em-
ployment on a farm at Bunyip. Even the
Vic. honorarium was staggered at the auto-
cratic proceedings, and at once dismissed
the case. Someone later on drew Attorney-
General Dry Brown’s attention to the mat-
ter, and that luminary undertook to con-
sider the question of compensation. But
that isn’t the main thing. Granting that it
may be impossible to keep callow constables
from making occasional asses of themselves,
it ought to be quite impossible for a man
to be held in gaol for a week when half-an-
hour’s diligent inquiry should prove the
utter groundlessness of the charge laid
against him on no evidence whatever. It is
all very well for O’Callaghan’s men to bal-
ance the non-arrest of the criminals they
can’t catch by arresting innocent citizens
whom they can; but the State doesn’t main-
tain gaols for the detention of innocent per-
sons on absurd charges.
Half a dozen feather-duster laws having
failed to sweep Ah Chow out of Victorian
cabinet-making, it is now proposed to make
it illegal for John to sleep on factory
premises, or to be discovered pervading the
establishment after hours. This is rendered
necessary by the discovery that the Chow
joiner is a somnambulist, and works in his
sleep. Reformers reckon that if Wun Lung
and Ling Fit are compelled to give the
white competitor an absolutely square deal,
J. Chow will go out of the furniture fac-
tories, and it is probably true. But the
Chow problem has many features in com-
mon with the “social evil,” and the Good
Ones of the earth are just learning that you
don’t abolish the “evil” when you shift it.
If Wun Lung is bustled out of the wood-
working industries, he will reappear as the
same old problem in some other department
of trade or labor, so that it will be necessary
to go on chivvying him ; and in the end we
shall be pretty certain to pull up out of
breath and decide that there is no cure for
Yellow Agony short of amputation.
Wheat and babies are closely related in
S. Aus. Presumably in gratitude for a suc-
cession of line harvests, in the past six
months 2005 Billjims undertook to feed
and clothe and buy hatpins for the daughters
of 2005 other people, which is by far the
best record for the State. The births were
5451, the best since the reckless times of a
quarter of a century back, and 656 above the
recent half-yearly average; and the deaths
of infants under one year were kept down
to 356.
The Westralian Timber Workers’ Union
formed four years ago, sat down in a cool’
quiet place the other day and valued its
harvest. It reckons that it has secured an
extra £25,000 a year at least for its *>3oo
members —something over £lO a year on"the
average—and all that it has had to pay out
over strikes and lockouts is about £lBOO A
while back it provided scholarships for the
sons and brothers of members, at a cost of
£1600; and the idea of a wood-chopper want-
ing to provide the higher education for his
youngsters nearly choked some of the union's
Fattest critics.
From the Oldest
Inhabitant’s Under-
study :
Apropos G a rdiner
and the early owner-
ship of the Toorak
district. Old Victorian
plans show “Elgar’s
Special Survey,” an
enormous tract, ad-
joining Gardiner’s. El-
gar was a mystery.
A large number of
people squatted, or
rather selected, on his
survey without paying
anything. It included
rich parts both
of Toorak and Haw-
thorn. When the selec-
tors thought them -
selves covered by the
Statute of Limitations,
an Elgar turned up.
alleging that he had
been a minor, and so
still had claims. It
is alleged that some
selectors gave him a
good deal of money;
others refused, and
pulled through all
right. All this is, how-
ever, vague and misty;
and something more
ought to be known
about Elgar. Toorak-
road was first called
Gardiner’s Creek-road,
and South Yarra rail-
way station was first
called Gardiner’s Creek
station. The Creek will
be found running into
the Yarra opposite the
Richmond survey pad-
dock. Jackson, who
built Toorak House,
was an ironmonger,
founder of McEwan
and Co. For some time
Toorak House was used
as Government House.
Hotham, Barkly, Dar-
ling and Canterbury
lived there. It was in
this place that Hotham
disgusted his free-feed-
ing society guests by
giving them colonial
beer instead of cham-
pagne. “Cold Pig”
they called him, no
doubt to show that,
though they might love
the Throne and Person
much, they loved free
fat feeding and drink-
ing more.
“Red Tape Junior”: The old, one-eyed
Herald annoyed me so much to-day that I
melted all the sealing wax. She was on
“Federal Chicanery” again, and wavin'? her
brolly furiously at the proposal of the Com-
monwealth Government to pay 8, instead of
3% or more, per cent, interest on the value
of the properties transferred by Australians
to Australia when they became one people
instead of six insects. Said she: —
This chicanery should be combated at every point
if the Federal compact is to endure on a firm and
lasting basis.
But what about the State’s treatment of
me! When 1 joined this department, Ma
started to pouch 4 per cent, of my salary,
without even saying “by your leave,” and
got away with £65 of my hard-earned money
up to 1896. In that year I was given a
chance to cease contributing, but Ma would
not hand over the £65. Instead, I was
coolly informed that the principal would be
held until I died, and that, though 3 per
cent, interest (not 3J or 3% p.c., but just
3 p.c.) would be credited, I would not be
allowed to draw the accumulations. What
has Granny got to say to that? If the Com-
monwealth is guilty of “chicanery,” the
State, arguing the case from the same prem-
ises, is guilty of daylight robbery.

Sydney Daily Telegraph describes the
Coronation: —
Australian delegates and other oversea represen-
tatives, with the exception of the Prime Mimsters,
were seated far down the hall, and saw little more
than the procession of Royalty. It was primarily
the crowning of the King by England’s lords. The
House of Commons and the people whom tney re-
presented had nothing to do with the great cere-
monial. The only seats which commanded an un-
interrupted view of the theatre and altar were occu-
pied by the peers and peeresses. The only hands
which played a part in the service were those of the
aristocracy and the Church. The Westminster boys
responded to all the Archbishop’s proclamations.
The shout of the boys was vigorous and ringing
enough, but it was theatrical and cold. . . Each time
the boys thundered “God Save the King,” but the
congregation was silent. . . Too much of the old
ceremonial borders on the burlesque.
So George was crowned by the lords, and the
only hands that played a part were the aris-
tocracy and the Church. And the Church
has lost its power and the Lords are dying—
and yet the papers remark upon it when the
King wears a worried look.
Melbourne’s Australian Church, long the
platform of that good Democrat and white
man, Dr. Charles Strong, but now in the
hands of people to whom the congregation
pays rent, was offered by auction last week,
but elicited no bids. There was a rumor
that a syndicate had an idea of turning it
into residential flats, but the solemn atmo-
sphere of the place choked the speculators
off.
STRIPE WITHOUT END, AMEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. Bro. Langdon said that if the cause arose
Protestants would wade in blood to defend their
liberties. (Applause.) —Cheerful note from an
Orange celebration in Perth.
’Twas in the Pit, the Sulphur Pit,
AVhere most of mortal things have end,
I saw a figure in a fit.
Methought I recognised a friend—
A face oft seen on earthly ways,
Untainted by the Devil’s blaze.
He waved his arms, and cried aloud
Of Bible, Liberty and Blood.
About him turned a frantic crowd ;
The flames swept o’er them like a flood.
“AVith Luther’s sword we must,” cried he,
“Protect our sacred liberty.”
I led Old Nick apart, and asked:
“Whence comes this madman? Who is lie?”
A grin the Master Fiend unmasked.
“An Orangeman,” he answered me.
“Know, each July, with zeal austere,
We celebrate the Boyne down here.
For ancient creeds and causes dead
They still fight on till all is red.”
VICTORIA.

Recently the M.L. Conciliation Council,
while considering a dispute betwen the Mer-
chant Service Guild of Australasia (M.L.
section) and the employers, found itself up
against the question: Should ship captains
and other officers be dealt with in the same
case? Being unable to make up its mind on
the matter, it knocked up its neighbor, the
Arbitration Court, for an opinion. The
Court decided that it was desirable to divide
the dispute into two branches —one dealing
with the claims of the masters, the other
with the claims _ of the officers —the idea
being that a skipper’s authority might be
impaired if he were a member of the same
union as. his officers. If that result was
likely, said Judge Sim, then, in the interests
of commercial travellers, jockeys, judges, and
even politicians and the general public, the
“old man” should be. prohibited from belong-
ing to the same union. Nevertheless, the
Court had no power to order that much, and
merely threw it in for some other authority
to consider. Judgment accordingly.
The Sydney wharfies have intimated that
ihev will not unb sugar from Queensland
ports if it has been loaded bv blackleg labor.
The Hon. W. M. Hughes, as Attorney-Gen-
eral, has advised W. M. Hughes, Esq., Presi-
dent of the Wharf Laborers’ Union, that
this action does not constitute a strike. The
wharfies, it seems, are not refusing to work ;
they are only refusing to do a certain class
of work. . This may be good law; but if
the staff in the Attorney-General’s office re-
fused to conduct any office business with any
one particular State, though sweetly agreeable
(o write letters to all the others, The
Bulletin fancies W. M. Hughes wouldn’t
view their action quite so dispassionately.
Hughes, by the way, threatens to resign the
presidency of the wharfies if the trouble
becomes acute. He should have done it im-
mediately he became Attorney-General, just
as he would have expected Wade to resign
had he been President of the Coal Owners
when he became State Attorney-General.
A Warburton (Vic.) citizen lately had
a bout with the Railway Department, and is
£1 poorer in pocket and x richer in experi-
ence, It seems he desired to consider the
Yarra, and discovered that by taking separ-
ate return tickets from Warburton to Lily-
dale and thence to Melbourne, instead of a
return through from Warburton to Mel-
bourne, he would save Is. 9d. Lilydale.
however, suspected his infamous intention,
and refused him a ticket, whereupon he rod*'
to the city without one, and was fined in

consequence, the department further rub-
bing it- in by demanding the price of the
ticket it refused to sell him. Of course his
proceedings were quite immoral and con-
trary to the regulation in such case made
and provided. It obviously pays the depart-
ment to carry a man from Warburton to
Lilydale and back for 2s. 6d. or so, or it
wouldn’t do it; also, it makes a profit by
shifting him thence to Melbourne and back
for 3s. 3d. But to imagine it is going to
carry him the whole distance for the sum of
the two fares is manifestly absurd. In the
Department’s eyes 2d. and 2d. make 5d. —
which leaves Id. profit.
Kink O’Malley, Commonwealth Minister
for Fireworks, has an idea for resuming the
whole block of which the Sydney G.P.O. now
forms the northern end. The king wants
more accommodation for the Post Office,
and for the Customs, and for his National
Bank when it comes; _ and he proposes to
concentrate it all in this central block. He
wouldn’t want much of the ground floor;
so he figures on putting up shops on the
frontages, and making a business proposi-
tion of it that way. Presumably, if the idea
can be hatched out, this is to be the real
headquarters of the Commonwealth’s busi-
ness : Yassberra will be a sort of back-yard
or dog-kennel, as it was intended to be all
along. The Bulletin has no over-power-
ing objection to any State-resumption scheme
on reasonable terms ; all the same, the craze
for packing people into the middle of a
bloated city has been carried too far, and
this merely carries it farther. About two-
thirds of the work that is done in the ex-
pensive parts of the crowded city could be
just as well, if not better, done in some airy
suburb —or country town, for that matter.
Huge concerns like the Norwich Fire, Insur-
ance Office have their headquarters in com-
paratively small provincial towns in Britain.

[For The Bulletin.]

our Transformations for £5 5s. — Advt.
“A woman’s crowning glory is her hair” ;
So ran the ancient proverb, saw or story.
Lo ! how times change. Now shopmen boldly swear
Their hair’s the modern female’s crowning glory.

Acting-Premier Holman (N.S.W.) easily
disposed of the “sentiment” argument put
forward by J. T. Walker (Senator) as one
of a deputation which protested against Gov-
ernment House being used for anything else.
Any “sentiment” attaching to the place will
be preserved, and, moreover, the public—
the mere Bill and Mary—will have an op-
portunity of viewing the interior of the
historical house and its decorations for the
first time, after being shut outside for nearly
70 years. True, when some former Gov-
ernors were sworn in, “the respectable por-
tion of the inhabitants” (vide the advts.) was
invited to witness the ceremony; but the
ceremony always took place in the entrance
hall, and the “respectable portion,” etc., oc-
cupied the dooymat.

A Melbourne doctor, in the course of a Uni-
versity lecture, pointed out that alcohol ex-
ists in milk in varying quantities. This con-
nects the Demon Cow of Australia’s bush youth
with the Demon Drink of Mr. Mauger, and
should serve to bring cow products under
Rechabitish suspicion. For its part, The
Bulletin has long suspected this; for it
has almost invariably found rum in the milk
of human kindness.
“Eddyson” : Wo-
man’s determina-
tion not to escape
attention drives her
to quaint devices at
times. One neigh-
bor of mine has a
lumpy, bow-legged,
splay-faced brute
that might be mis-
taken for a true
bulldog at 50 paces
on a dark night.
This precious ani-
mal haunts the

lanes, coursing
Brown’s ducks in

up a post during

Sundays he is cap-

a hot bath, braced
up with flash har-
ness, and his mis-
tress, whip in hand,
takes him forth on
a lons rein to
waddle ahead of her in frequented places,
that she may loom in the vulgar eye as an
uncommon object. Another young matron
resorts to an original device. She has a
particularly fine, fair baby boy. She puts
his little left arm in splints, swathes it in
snowy bandages, hangs it about his neck in
a swagger silk scarf, and takes him for long
tram rides, fairly wallowing in the universal
regard inspired by the pretty pitifulness of
the babe with a broken arm.

James Edmond calls on the Dago:—
I tore myself away from Venice with a loud rend-
ing sound and a feeling of profound grief. If it
hadn’t been that my apartment was already en-
gaged at Florence and the bill was accumulating I
would have stayed longer.
Venice is an ideal place for anyone who wants a
good sleep in the morning. In all the great city
there is no horse, mule, donkey, ox-cart, dray, wag-
gon, cab, buggy, taxi., motor-car, tramcar, omni-
bus, motor-bus, or (1 believe) wheelbarrow. 1 saw'
no bike or motor-bike. I saw no cripple propelling
himself in a wheeled chair. I saw not one dog, save
here and there a carefully muzzled animal leu by a
visitor at the end of a string. I came aefoss not
even a pet monkey, though 1 did see tw'o cats —
presumably Venetian cats. So the streets are, as a
rule, all pavement from w'all to wall, and the Vene-
tian walks in the middle or anywhere. He is safe in
his native city, but w'hen he goes abroad to Rome or
Paris the first hearse runs over him and he is no
more. One reason why the Roman or Florentine
cabby yells like a fiend at every pedestrian is the fear
that the man on foot may be a Venetian visitor and
not responsible for his actions. In the great square
of St. Mark the cafes even put scores of tables and
chairs out where the tram-line ought to be, and
their patrons sit there and drink and smoke and
listen to the music.
There is too much music in Venice. Every morn-
ing is Sunday morning by reason of its holy calm,
and every night is Saturday night by reason of its
bang and its unholy crash. My bedroom at the
Hotel Danielli looked out on the beautiful Grand
Canal in front. A side window looked out on a
cafd with a band of about 20 performers. I could
have slain the conductor with my water-bottle any
night. On Sunday night I took aim at him, but
hesitated. On Monday I did the same. On Tuesday
I got legal advice, and learned that under a law
passed in the reign of the Doge Marino Falieri, cen-
turies ago, anybody who threw a water-bottle at a
band conductor had to pay a heavy fine, also buy a
new conductor and a new bottle, and then be perman-
ently excluded from Venice. As I would hate to be
permanently excluded from Venice, I took to per-
vading the quiet streets per gondola till a late hour,
so that my band might tire itself out before I went
to bed.
You can traverse Venice by land, of course, but
as there are bridges everywhere, with a flight of
steps on each side of each bridge, there are few
points about it. The Venetian house has a front
door leading on to the street and a back door lead-
ing on to the water. And by reason of 1100 years
of experience the Venetian baby doesn’t seem to fall
into the water. Now and then the Venetian small
boy throws things at the passing gondola, but then
the small boy is the same everywhere.
The streets vary from a great w ; dth down to
about 4ft. They seem quite sufficient, as all the
traffic is on foot.
The animals of this city are (1) the tame pigeons
of St. Mark, which can be induced by a halfpenny
worth of corn to climb all over the visitor and sit
on his head, to the great detriment of his belltopper.
Then there is (2) the hotelkeeper, who belongs to
the carnivorous fauna. Further, there is (3) an
erect or partially erect creature with a strange habit
of holding out its right fore paw, palm upwards. I
believe it is called a waiter. It certainly seems to
be waiting for something. The wild animals of
Venice are the waiter who hasn’t been tipped and
the gondolier who ha*f received his exact fare. Wher
the gondolier recognises that you are a stranger he
often stops about 20 yards short of his destination
to ask if you will tip him. A penny extra will do.
Twopence will make him feel glad. If you tip him
well at the beginning of the trip he will make
heroic efforts to show his gratitude by. dodging past
rival gondoliers in narrow channel® and then wild
Italian language is sometimes exchanged, and the
path is strewn with objurgations. The best thing of
the kind I met with was when I gave the highly
respectable gondolier attached to the hotel 3d. on
condition that he made extra speed to the railway
station. A slow-moving barge blocked the way for
fully half a mile in a narrow canal, and when my
aquatic charioteer had failed in his third attempt
to dodge past, he (so far as I could guess) told the
bargemen frankly what he thought of them?
The railway gets into Venice across a bridge which
seems about 30 miles long, though I suppose it is
less. It stops on an island, like everything else in
the old half-drowned city.
You go through 45 tunnels on the way to Flor-
ence. This place looks beautiful from the sur-
rounding hills, but when you consider it closely
it isn’t Florence but Flossie. It is battered and
pretentious. It is faded, and unkempt about the
back hair, and frowsy- about the skirts—or out-
skirts. Its ways are narrow and crooked, and its
soul —which is the River Arno —is shallow. It is
noisy at night and untidy all day. Its great
cathedral is done up externally in four colors —
black, white, red and dirt. Florence, a few de-
cades ago, was much larger than it is now. Like
the drab lady who keeps the hash-tower, it has in-
deed seen better days.
On the hills above it stands Fiesole, which was a
town and a fortress before either Rome or Exodus
was born. It is a wondrous dirty place trimmed
with dogs, and garnished with gaunt mules. When
you look up at Fiesole from Florence it promises to
be a place of surpassing loveliness, and when yon
look down from Fiesole upon the great city of
Flossie it looks like a gem set in the midst of Para-
dise. There are two disordered sisters who in a
manner guarantee each other’s respectability, or two
insolvents who endorse each other’s bills.
FAMILY SKELETONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The heirs of a family in France are fighting a
curious case over a skeleton which has been for many
years in the family. —News item.
Family skeletons, stuffed away,
Rattle and stir when the feast is spread,
Chilling us through when we would be gay,
Turning us ashen, or pink, or red.
Ugh ! how we shudder when bones long dead
Mock with their sepulchre sounds our play !
Yet, brothers, why should we live in dread?
Sell off your skeletons! Make them pay!
People I know of could fill a dray,
Pile it with horrors from which they’ve fled;
Even the youngest would pipe “Hooray!”
And sounder sleep in his little bed;
Skeletons sit on our hearts like lead. . . .
If in their cupboards they will not stay,
Market them all at so much per head!
Sell off your skeletons! Make them pay !
Vain is it cither to curse or pray;
Useless your tears whether pent-up or shed ;
The bones will weather your final day,
And follow your hearse with rattling tread ;
They fret and gnaw at life’s shoddy thread ;
With muffled knockings they slowly slaj'.
Brothers, revolt! Be no longer bled !
Sell off your skeletons ! Make them pay!
Final Whocp.
Rags, bones and bottles! In these are bred
The sorrows that sour from June to May.
Send for a Jew with a cart or sled !
Sell off your skeletons ! Make them pay !
N.S.W.

ASSIMILATION.
In an address during the religious service con-
nected with the Feast .of the Weeks, known as She-
vous, Rabbi Abrahams declared that the greatest
menace to Judah was assimilation of the race
with other peoples.— Age.
ISAACSON: “ It'th true, it'th true! Loog
arount you, and you vill thee dot dthe Jew
ith Hoiking all hith dithinguithing feadureth .’
“Cross Cut” : Allow me to write on white
ant and teredo-resisting timbers, after 25 years’ ex-
perience. The reason why many of our timbers are
destroyed rapidly is because they are cut when
the sap is up in the tree, instead of down. Another
fact. Ti-tree and swamp oak, cut on the banks of
Cockle Creek, last in the water for 10 years, while
similar stakes from other N.S.W. districts do not
last a few years. Teak is a valuable timber, a
better ant-resister than turpentine, but it is not
found plentifully. I experimented a few years ago
with blaekbutt, turpentine and stringy-bark. Pieces
were retorted and placed in water, side by side with
pieces untreated. Found, in nine months, the un-
treated blaekbutt had been eaten througn by teredo,
while the treated timber was untouched. In my
opinion, if the Government sent small portable re-
torts to the timber-cutting districts, and insisted on
the timber being cut when the sap is down, and then
retorted, the lives of wharves, bridges, etc., would
be doubled.
“Clancy”: The most irascible party in the
bush is undoubtedly William Wagtail. My mate
and I once camped with him for weeks together, and
the way he behaved was disgusting. He never did
a stroke of work about the camp, except catch a
few flies, and no matter what we did he’d “rouse
on us. Jack and I were doing some contract fencing,
and at daylight, Bill, who slept out, would come
into the tent and start cursing because breakfast
wasn’t ready. He would tell us what a lazy pair of
blanks we were, and remark that how we were going
to clear enough to pay the store account he didn t
know. We would hurry through breakfast, and Bill
would hunt us out of the camp, swearing volubly,
and then return and have his own breakfast com-
fortably by the fire. Once we came home early from
work, and Bill gave us such a tongue-banging that
afterwards, when we did knock off a bit on the
early side, we used to go shooting or visiting rather
than go home and face Bill. Jack wanted to tell
him to clear out (he said it was worse than having
a cook in the camp) ; but just about that time Bill
brought a little lady along, and then what could we
do? He was a bit shamefaced over it at first, but
was shortly as brazen as ever, and used to force her
(she was a shy little thing) to come in and have her
meals with us the same as he did. He treated her
fairly well till she started flying about with bits of
straw in her beak ; then he showed his true character,
and used to curse her the same as he did Jack and
me. The sight of a snake used to drive Bill nearly
frantic; he would rip and tear as he fluttered around,
till Jack or I went and killed it, and those were the
only occasions he seemed to soften to us at all.
(P.S.- —Bill was about right. After squaring up at
the store we had 2s. 3d. left.)

Letter received the other day by a Sydney
firm, which had intimated that is was opening out :
Seeing your advt. in to-day’s paper, I thought it
just possible you might have an opening for another
hand. I have nothing to recommend me, but am
simply a walking Cyclopedia of general uselessness.
I’ve been a carpenter, painter, plumber, general
busliman, miner, explorer, farmer, engine-driver, sol-
dier, and God only knows what else. And m the
lot I’ve done nothing to brag about. I have
only one virtue left —1 don’t drink. But Ive been
into every other difficulty under the sun. And I m
still alive. Yours truly is 45 years old, and stony
broke; having lost all I had copper gouging, so please
reply by return post.
“Wang”: Will* some obliging expert bet
me drinks that black wattle isn’t the hardest Austra-
lian wood to cut in an absolutely dry state? I’ve as-
saulted it with a razor-like axe, and knocked more
chips off the steel than off the wood; and I’ve used
a medium sharp weapon on it, and mostly sprained a
wrist and hardly dinted the timber. If there is any
harder wood to carve in the continent I’d like some-
body else to be introduced to it —it’s no good to me.

“Tiaki”: The Maori has some peculiar
methods of supplying the larder with victuals at
times. For instance, dur-
ing a visit to the Ure-
wera country I accom-
panied Rongo to get some
tauhou, or white-eye
(sometimes known as the
blight bird), which is
about the size of a giant
bumble-bee, to frame a
stew. We went to a clear
patch in the bush, and
Rongo put two forked
sticks, about 4ft. high, in-
to the ground; then he
laid a small sapling from
one to the other, like a
ridge pole; next cut a
stick like the handle of a
bullocky’s persuader; and,
after that, squatted under
the contraption and chir-
ruped. The tauhou hur-
ried up to the ridge pole
to see what the chirruping
was about, and was imme-
diately swept to destruc-
tion by the persuader. In
an hour’s time a heap of
dead birds was ready for
the pot. I was told, too,
that, in order to have pork
handy to the kitchen, a
young wild boar, when
caught, is emasculated, his
eyes gouged out, and he
is allowed his freedom in a
patch of whinau (Eleocar-
pus dentatus ) trees. Piggy,
being very fond of the ber-
ries, and having neither
eyesight nor amorousness
to lead him astray, is al-
ways on hand when Ile-
nare’s stomach rings the
bell. There were no cruelty-
to-animals inspectors in
the Urewera at that time.
“Mimosa”: A few years back I bought in
Sydney some 300 pink galahs to take to Africa.
They were placed in a large cage, alongside the stall
of an unbroken mare, who kicked the side of the
cage in. Result: A speedy exit of parrots, 1400
miles west of the Leeuwin. The birds rose from the
hatch like “homers,” got their bearings, said “adieu”
to the steamer, and, steering in the face of a half
gale, made a “bee line” for Australia, the last dis-
appearing about 1 p.m. Nearing dusk, a lascar told
me a parrot had returned to the ship. While catch-
ing it a couple more returned, and, in ones and
twos, they kept returning till 2 o’clock next morning,
their arrival being heralded by a “flop” on the deck.
They were in a very exhausted state, but only about
40 failed to get aboard again. I’ve often wondered
how many of that number reached their beloved
Australia.
“Calafanc”: Have been helping to divest
the jumbuck of his overcoat at one particular station
in this district (Central Q.) for the past few years,
and last shearing and this have come across a fair
amount of the wool tinged with green and bluish-
green. Also, this year have secured a small quantity
with a pink discoloration. There is nothing extraor-
dinary about variegated wool —it occurs in most
flocks"—but, in the cases I mention, the discoloration
affected only a small patch in the middle of the
staple, with wool of the usual jaundiced hue at each
end. Most of the local cognoscenti, with whom I
have discussed the matter, look sceptical, and scorn-
fully inquire about the brand. Wish some of toe
more practical “Aboliars” would come forward with
a reasonable explanation.

“Jack Jay”: Ever met the vegetable
caterpillar in Australia? It is quite a common cir-
cumstance in Maoriland. The Maoris call it awheto,
and the women used to find it a great bore in their
kumara patches. Henare’s chief, occupation, in old
days, was looking after the fleshy part of the meal,
and the missus did most of the cultivation. The
caterpillar burrows in the ground in preparation for
its change to moth, and while lying there the
spores of a fungus settle on it, always at the back
of the head, and suck the life out of it. The outer
husk remains. Some have been found with the
wings of the moth showing. The caterpillar is from
lin. to 4in. long, and the vegetable that grows from
it is about the same length.
“Sheila”: At a banquet given in honor of
the Federal M.P.’s at Samarai, the 59-acre Papuan
patch south-east of Port Moresby, one of the Par-
liamentarians, in a burst of enthusiasm, declared the
place an “emerald gem in a sapphire sea.” Samarai
is a beauty-spot all right; but had the orator pro-
longed his stay for, say, a month, and experienced,
some of the discomforts one has to put up with
there, he would probably have considered the request
of the residents that £2OO per annum be the mini-
mum wage for a “white” employed on the island
anything but unreasonable. Rain falls on an average
20 days monthly, in short tropical downpours; at
times fresh meat is unobtainable, even from the
cattle farm on the neighboring island of Sareeba;
and to be forced to consume yams in place of pota-
toes is apt to make Little Mary rebellious. Further,
if the after-dinner speaker discovered on rising in
the morning that the cockroaches had had supper
off his finger-nails during the night, he might have
his doubts whether the island gem was of the first
water.

“Con. D. O’Bolin”: There was frightful
commotion at Gundybank. The morning of the races
dawned clear and bright after a week’s steady rain,
and the population of the neighborhood, whiskers
trimmed and spurs burnished, arrived at Holmes’s
pub, only to learn the awful tidings that the teams
with the complete supply of grog on board, were
hopelessly bogged eight miles out near the sand-
hills. A motion was being sorrowfully carried to
postpone the meeting, when Tea-tree Bill uprose.
“Blank it all,” he shouted, “what’s the matter with
holding the [blanky] races out there where the
waggons are stuck?” That night the stags lobbed
home with the barrels —empty—escorted by Gundy-
bank, waving a hiccough and declaring that it had
been to the besht [blanky] racesh ever held in Horse-
tralia. i
“Jack Jay” : The pipiwharauroa, or shining
cuckoo of Maoriland, at which “Konini” hurls a curse
(B. 22/6/T1), is not confined to the Dominion,
though that seems to be its main breeding-ground.
It is a migrant, arriving among the Maori birds in
the spring. That it loafs on other birds, and chiefly
on the grey warbler, for the hatching and rearing of
its young, is quite true; but it is doubtful if the
warbler suffers so much as “Konini” thinks. She
seems to be a motherly little thing, and many years’
experience of the cuckoos’ imposition has not greatly
reduced her numbers. Also, “Konini” may take heart
from the treatment the tui gives Mrs. Pipiwha’s egg
when she finds it in the nest. Out it goes with a
“biff, bang” ; or if by error the tui hatches the un-
clean thing, the chick’s life is not worth a moment’s
purchase. If Mrs. T. catches Mrs. P. near her nest,
Mrs. P. has to fly for her life, and gets such a scare
that she probably drops the egg anywhere.

“Pick” : “Ironbark” mistakes the reference
in his quotation (B. 29/6/Tl) from the yarn of the
“Plash Shearer,” wherein that “glassy agate” an-
nounced the packing of his “turkey.” The “turkey”
in the case was the “shrewdy-pot’s” pack-animal,
not his swag; and “turkey” is the term used for
“horse” by every shearer who disdains the common,
garden names of “moke,” “prad,” “alligator,”
“cradle,” “bone-rack,” “Lazarus,” “rocking-chair,”
“cushion,” and so forth. Anyhow, a “neddy” is pro-
bably often referred to by titles which “Ironbark”
will not find in his dictionary. A friend of mine,
who nightly, when it is cold, , commandeers the rug
from the back of a neighbor’s poultice-bearer, calls
the gee-gee the “blanket-warmer.”
“Berkeley”: Of course snakes mesmerise.
Here’s further proof. One of Brown’s cows sud-
denly cut off its evening milk supply, though it con-
tinued to dispense the fluid each morning; so Brown
took a day off —the first for years—to watch her.
Late in the afternoon he saw a black-and-tan snake,
9ft. long, crawl out of a tussock of cocksfoot and ap-
proach the' animal. The reptile emitted a gentle,
purring sound, beating time with his spiked tail.
When he had attracted the cow’s attention, he gazed
amorously at her until she consented, whereupon the
seducer wound himself about a hind leg and sucked his
fill of milk. Then he commenced to fill a small tin-
can that was hanging around his neck ; but at that
moment Brown fell upon him with the whisky bottle,
and he left hurriedly. Everyone knows Brown and
Brown’s cows, so this can easily be verified if neces-
sary.
THE GOOD SPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The racecourse out at Puntalong was thronged with
sharps and flats,
And for the Stakes the punters had a cert, inside
their hats;
Two nags—no more—were left to face the flag; and
to the crowd
The Saveloy seemed just the horse to do his backers
proud.
The Saveloy, hot favorite, was rushed by everyone
Who thought he knew a thing or two; yet, ere the
race was run,
With mirth upon his countenance, for all the world
to see,
One waggish book howled “Three to one the field!”
persistently.
It was the sort of battle-cry which instantly appealed
To those who heard him bellow, roaring “Three to
one the field!”
And every brand of punter came and rocked his
dollars in.
To let the moment pass, it seemed, would surely
have been sin.
The bookie’s pale assistant pushed his pencil all he
knew ;
His pallid gills turned greenish-grey and gradually
blue.
“Blank-dash!” he gasped. “Wot? Three to one!”
The bookie went ahead
And raked in all the cash he could. “ ’Ere, three
to one l” he said.
The flagster flagged the start, the horses ambled on
their way ;
The pale clerk almost wept to see them teeter past.
“I say!”
He yapped. “Wot, three to one the punters’ ’orse ?
There’s only two
Blanked starters in the bloomin’ race! It’s on the
rocks for you!”
The horses neared the entrance to the straight, the
Saveloy
Tn front; and backers yelled loud yells of almost
perfect joy.
The bookie gripped his bag, what time the pale clerk
did a groan.
“Ain’t I a starter, too?’’ he grinned—and tore for
parts unknown.
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
THE NEED OF THE MOMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIM.]
“We want reform,” the speaker said;
And bright the hall-lights danced upon
The bore’s bald, round, pink, shiny head;
His crimson nose with fervor shone.
“We need reform,” he said; “and one
Reform we can no longer spare;
We need it now!” He paused, to run
Quick fingers through his scanty hair.
“And what reform is that?” he cried,
Above a rumbling, rising storm.
“Dunno, old man,” a voice replied,
“Unless, maybe, it’s chloroform!”

“Ginger”: To “Yalgun,” “Border View,”
and the few others who have had the manly, British
sense of fair play and boys-of-the-cowbreed courage
to stand up for the dairy cocky, I hand up a pint of
the best milk. I owe a lot to the industry. Why,
even when I was only 10 I played—l mean I took a
humble part in its development. Every morning I
got in the cows, helped to milk, carried per boot
three gallons of the juice to the local hospital (which
was two miles away, and had to be reached before
6.30 a.m.), served various customers in the township,
returned to breakfast, school at 9.30, home again,
got in the cows, milked, had some sleep, then more
cows, and so on. By such a training did I acquire
those habits of sobriety, industry, hardiness and
thrift which have inspired the eloquence of pro-
truding politicians in a hundred school-rooms in our
country. Sober? Where are the cow farmer’s
“slaves,” as I have heard them called, who spend
their time idling over hotel bars stroking the bar-
lady’s hand ? Eh ? Industrious ? Can one of you
find even an infant loafing on a dairy farm? What?
Hardy? Well, if ability to toil steadily from 3
a.m. to 9 p.m., make a hearty meal of bread and
treacle, and do with six hours’ sleep on hard boards
isn’t a proof of hardiness, what is? And thrift?
Why, out of my 15 bob a week —but, there, I’ll
leave it to your imagination. Yes; I owe the cow
industry a lot, and if ever I get a fair chance I’ll pay
it back.
♦ * * *
“Pick” Black Mary is not always a down-
trodden and submissive worm. I know the Binghi,
who, among his campfellows, is reckoned the best
dealer of stoush on the N.S.W. South Coast; yet,
when at home, he is the most cringing husband of
my knowledge. He is lawfully married, and per-
haps that accounts for it. In spite of the chronic
lassitude that has always heavily afflicted his tribe,
his lubra drives him to work, and with his earnings
she holds high jamboree or dissipates the wealth at
euchre. When, some time ago, he became wearied
of his married blessedness, and got a two days’ start
in a bolt for Gippsland, the dusky three-quarters
caught him this side of Bendoc and landed him back
by the wool.
* * * *
“Q.C.” : If anybody has an attack of white
ants in fencing posts, this is how to cure it —
or, better still, to prevent the attack. Bore a hole
in your green timber half or three-quarters of an
inch in diameter, on each side of the posts above
the ground, deflected at an angle downward, to the
extent of four or five inches, according to the thick-
ness of the wood. If the posfe are narrow, one hole
at each end will be sufficient. Fill the holes with
salt, ram well, and plug the openings tightly with
short wooden dowels. The salt will eventually per-
meate the sappy timber, and make it immune from
anticitis.

“Scatto”: Saw the most-repaired vehicle
in Australia the other day. It was a sulky driven
by a bush cocky. The wheels were bound together
with wire; most of the spokes were made of sapling,
wired into place; wire held the tyres to their job;
each wheel, had four sapling pieces wired across it
inside and outside; the harness was held together
by wire, string, rope and raw hide; there was no
crupper or breeching; the collar was stuffed with
aged bagging; the reins were of binder twine; and
the splashboard of the contraption was made of the
sides of an old gin-case. As a matter of fact, the
only parts of the original that I could see were the
shafts.

“Jeroi” : “Salt Beef and Spuds,” talking
about fencing-timber, asks what teak is like to last.
Good, if Are doesn’t get near it; but it is the most
inflammable timber that grows on the N.S.W. North
Coast. Ordinary “stringy” takes fire easily enough,
though red “stringy” (also known as red mahogany,
redwood and mountain “stringy”) does not. “Dy-
nong’s” scrub-box must be different from the Rich-
mond River kind ; here it grows fairly sound. White-
box (quite another sort) is more often hollow than
otherwise, and is the most favored by white ants of
all the timbers here.
* * * *
“Jeroi” : Have noticed that ironbark will
soon die if used as a fowl roost. Two dozen fowls,
adopting it as a bedroom, will kill a young ironbark
in less than 12 months. Don’t know whether any
other tree is affected this way. Know a red-gum
tree that has been used for years, and it seems to
thrive on the treatment.

“Condot”: Re the par (B. 6/7/’ll) in
which it is mentioned that a new-chum cocky wanted
an injunction to prevent the shooting of rabbits on
his land, the story is beaten by a letter written to
the Vic. Lands Department. The intending cocky in
a previous letter had asked for some advice as to live
stock on a block which he wished to take up, and in
reply he had been advised to get a few head of a cer-
tain dairy strain. To this he replied that he had
no intention of keeping cows, as he was going to get
a milking-machine. He wanted to know which brand
gave the biggest supply.
“Leo Lear”: Have all the Abo-botanists
gone to the Coronation, that S’Africa has been al-
lowed to pilfer, without protest, the wattle as her
national flower ? Of about 400 known species of
acacia, 80 per cent, are indigenous to Australia, the
remainder being divided between the two Americas
and Africa. All the best of the flowering kinds
are Australian, and only known to Botha’s union by
importation. S’Africa’s chief acacia is the awful
camel-thorn, and bears as much resemblance to the
queen of our bush as a prickly-pear does to a rose.
* * # *
“The Hack”: What is the record length
of time occupied by a fired stack of hay in consum-
ing itself? Saw one burning near Keilor (Vic.) the
other night to the tune of a light southerly breeze.
It contained 100 tons, and was exactly 20
hours turning itself into a stack of ashes. Local
cockydom professed to be staggered at the rapidity
of the job; for myself I was staggered at the
gradualness.
“Leo Lear”: “A.W.A.” (B. 29/6/’ll) is
wrong in placing the white ant “at the top of the
ant scale in point of intelligence.” This so-called
ant is not an ant at all, but a termite, and belongs
to the insect order neuroptera, which includes dragon
and may-flies. Ants are first cousins to bees and
wasps, and are of the order hymenoptera. I should
say the American ant that harvests its own grain
would be somewhere at the head of the Upper VI.
form in the ant college.

“Spreader”: While Jack Maori is no
long-distance worker, he can keep his end up with his
white brother on casual jobs. Fencing, and all kinds
of bush graft of the eatch-as-catch-can variety, he
will tackle, no matter how hard, and on his long
suit, shearing, the pakeha can’t beat him for quan-
tity or quality. And I’d like to say a word for sister
with the golden-dusk skin —she is a bonzer nurse.
Nearly all the larger M.L. hospitals have one or more
Maori maidens on the nursing staff, and they are a
three-cheer success.
“Nitellub”: “Condamine” (B. 15/6/’ll)
might find an antidote for adder bite in muriatic
acid, more commonly known as spirits of salts. With
that chemical I have cured bites, or stings, from two
scorpions, three centipedes, and one red-backed black
spider. If the spirit is applied to the wound im-
mediately (use a finger for the purpose) the pain will
get the sack in half an hour. In addition to the
above-mentioned beasts, I have also had experience
with the business end of bees, wasps and bull ants,
and for causing pain I give the scorpion the cake —-
unhesitatingly.
