“Ah”: Some of the “navvies” imported
from Whitechapel to work on Bananaland’s new rail-
roads don’t know a sleeper from a clothes-prop, or a
crowbar from a bar of soap. The other day, on our
section, the ganger, Dinny Sullivan, wanted a few dog-
spikes to finish a crossing with, and, as there hap-
pened to be some lying loose about the camp, he
sent the Hon. George Hawkins down, with instruc-
tions to bring along all the dogs he could find.
Hawkins was some time away, and when he returned
with an old brindle slut on a string, with six multi-
colored pups trailing behind, Dinny fainted on the
track. When he recovered he wrote out the Hon.
George’s time, and enclosed it in a note to the time-
keeper. It read: “Pay this cove off, and give him a
reference as a rabbiter.”
* * * *
“Brenon”: Here’s another one. It was as
hot as the place the Wowsers are naturally afraid of,
the mercury recording a heat which made the leaves
on the trees turn on their backs and crackle. Jacky
met me at the slip-rails. “You big budgery judge of
cattle, boss?” he said. “Yes,” I replied, wondering
what was coming. “Well, boss,” he grinned, “you
bin tink it this a plurry cow of a day, eh?”
“Wee McGregor”: Tell me this, one of
you. Why does a dog always roll on anything stink-
some that he happens across? I asked a newspaper
the same question a while ago, and the paper-man
said he did not know, but advised me to ask the dog:
and when I knocked at the editor’s door he grunted
at me. So I was wrong again.
THE ISLAND OF BORES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All my life I’ve been a victim of that dreary beast,
the bore;
When I’m working in my office he comes tapping at
the door;
And he ambles gaily in
With a foolish, bear-like grin;
r hen he takes a seat and fastens like a leech upon
my brain,
Till the platitudes he mumbles send a red-hot twinge
of pain,
Like a telegraphic message, to my store of matter
grey:
“Here’s the Vampire feasting on you! Can’t you
drive the brute away?”
But I mutter weakly, “No !
Wait a minute! He may go!”
Then my guilty soul transparent does the monster
riddle through
With his formula eternal: “Now, I’m not disturbing
you! ”
“Not at all!” I brightly answer, and the foolish
coward’s lie
Sets him droning with fresh vigor when again he’s
caught my eye;
For he needs my optic dim ;
’Tis a sort of guide to him
If my strained attention wavers from his pointless
anecdotes;
So he holds me safe in thraldom while he blithers
and he gloats;
And I dare not even murmur, and I cannot look
around,
For the wretch has mesmerised me, and I’m fettered,
stunned and bound;
So till he is out of breath
He keeps boring me to death;
And I’m utterly exhausted when at last he goes away,
For he is the Boring Vampire, and I am his helpless
prey.
I have hatched a scheme for dealing with the mon-
ster dull and vile;
It requires a secret service, and a lone, mid-ocean
isle;
You will only need to write
To a secret head at night
Something in this awful manner: “I accuse Tobias
A.,”
And just mention where he’s living. He’ll be shad-
owed night and day;
Then if he has all the markings of the common deadly
bore,
He will one day get a warning—-just the simple
words, “No more!”
Through the medium of the post,
With the drawing of a ghost
Stalking closely in the footsteps of a fool with drill
and bit.
That will tell him he is labelled ; he must make the
best of it.
If there’s no improvement in him when a month or
so has gone,
And he’s walking home one ev’ning, he’ll be roughly
seized upon
By some masked determined men,
Who will truss him up, and then
Bear him helpless to a taxi, and speed swiftly from
the place;
Neither home, nor club nor office evermore shall see
his face;
But in harmless isolation, on the lone mid-ocean isle,
He will bore his boresome brothers at his peril all
the while;
There’ll be ev’ry sort of bore
On that lonely, surf-ringed shore,
And they’ll feed on one another and the seeds of
Hatred sow,
While the long and boresome seasons in a dull pro-
cession go.
Hermit bores will seek the mountains to escape the
droning pack,
And whenever bores pursue them, with large rocks
they’ll drive them back;
Sitting on a fallen tree,
Staring ever at the sea,
These will watch the passing steamers laden with
the prey of bores,
And look eagerly for shipwrecks when the cyclone
leaps and roars.
Woe betide the wretched sailor who is cast upon
that strand,
For the bores will swoop upon him when his hair is
full of sand,
And they’ll sit around and bore
Till the captive is no more;
Then again at one another they will dolorously
drone,
Or attack a hermit brother and get smitten with a
stone.
But there’s one defect about it, which I must admit
I see.
’Twould be dreadful if a warning one day came
along to me l
Just suppose that I was grabbed,
Bundled up, and taxieabbed,
And conveyed aboard a lugger to be dumped upon
that shore! . . .
What staccato screams of fury would go up when
each mad bore
Saw the founder of the System unprotected on the
Isle!
When I come to think it over, it seems hardly worth
my while! . . .
Sometimes in bad dreams I see
Something running endlessly!
As it flings the sand behind it, someone's mercy it
implores !
But it gets a howl in answer from the wild, pur-
suing bores t

A lady, who was lunching at a city restaurant a
day or two ago, discovered a mouse’s head in a
mutton pie. —Melbourne paper.
Overheard in a meat-pie under a miscro scope:
"Fancy meeting you! ”
“Colonel” Hoskin, chief-sec. of the Austra-
lian Salv’army, is back from London, with
despatches from “the General.” The old
man has lost the use of one eye, but his
voice is louder than ever, and, at 82, he is
meditating another trip to Australia.
9v Barron, who died in Wellington
(M.L.) the other day, at 76, was the founder
of Maoriland’s Hansard staff, and for 20
years its chief. Previously he had been
an interpreter during the Crimean War, a
purser on P. and O. boats, and an inky way-
farer on two of Maoriland’s early organs—
the New Zealander (Auckland) and the
Advertiser (Wellington).
Evidently it doesn’t take the right kind
of man long to learn the aviation ropes.
Henry Robert Busteed, who left Brunswick
(Vic.) three months ago to try to hook an
aviator’s certificate, landed the fish in six
weeks after first shaking hands with J. Bull.
He is, as far as this paper knows, Australia’s
first home-grown licensed aviator, though
Hammond is a Maorilander.
Rev. C. P. M. Bardin, one of Victoria’s
pioneer parsons, has crept silently across.
He was a Trinity College (Dublin) man,
and a schoolmate of George Higinbotham’s.
Coming to Tasmania in ’5O, he spent a few
years preaching to diggers and cockies, and
then for 40 years was rector of Christ
Church, Brunswick. For most of that time
he was chaplain at Pentridge, where he was
known as a kindly old man, always eager to
make “my flock” a trifle less uncomfortable.

The “Liberal” press lias backed up every such de-
mand. — Fact.
THE OLD LADY: “Now I’ve helped you get
it, you'll be sure not to throw any of it
over the fence, won't you!”
F. J. Edgar, who may succeed M. J. Cody
as Governor of I’entridge prison (Melb.), is
a member of a pushful Wowser family.
One brother, W. H. Edgar, was lately made
an acting member of the Weary Government,
and has been acting strenuously ever since.
Another brother, and the most important, is
the Rev. A. R. Edgar, who once figured in a
struggle over a will. As may be recalled,
the Law declared the will a won’t so far as
the Reverend w r as concerned.
E. A. Rennie, the fine, granitey old man
who, as Auditor-General, told the truth about
the finances of N, S. Wales for a quarter
of a century, died last week, aged 90. When
he retired, four or five years ago, a Bulle-
tin man had an idea that he would be full
of interesting things about all the poli-
ticians and others he had met since he joined
the Audit Office in 1846. But the old man
knew absolutely nothing about politicians or
politics: he had lived with the State’s ac-
counts under his pillow, and, as to them, he
had all along told what he saw, turning not
one inch to the right or the left. The son of one
University Professor, he was the father of
another, and of other men and women of
brains and pertinacity.
The M.L. branch of the Royal Humane
Society has been listening to an interesting
and apparently authentic yarn. A Chatham
Island boy, seven years young, named Her-
bert R. Clough, was left in charge of his
sleeping baby brother and a sister.
The room caught fire, whereon Herbert
led the girl out, then rushed back, wrapped
baby in a blanket, and brought him out;
and finally tore down the burning curtains,
dragged in a tub of water, beat the flames
out with a wet broom, and collapsed. After
a strenuous few moments like that, a little
faintness would be excusable even in an
adult.
Died at Liverpool (N.S.W.) the other
day, aged 82, J. D. Browne, who left an
interesting letter in his cabin. It was ad-
dressed to Browne’s father, by the Gover-
nor’s secretary in 1816, and ran this way:—
On account of the large capital, nearly £20,000,
which you propose to embark here in mercantile and
farming concerns, . . . his Excellency will make you
a grant of 3000 acres of land, and assign 12 cows
from the Government herds on a credit of three
years. ... You will also be assigned eight Govern-
ment men, to be victualled from the King’s store for
the term of 18 months, to facilitate the agricul-
tural pursuits You and your family will also
be victualled from the same period for 18 months
from the King’s stores. The Governor will further
direct an eligible town allotment to be marked out
for the purpose of your erecting a dwelling house,
stores and wharf, near to Dawes Point battery, in
the situation solicited by you.
Browne was a member of an old Irish
family, the head of which is the Marquis of
Sligo, who, as Lord Ulric Browne, a bache-
lor 70 vears of age, succeeded to the title
three years ago.
Donald Campbell, one of the S.A. Labor
members, will finish his course for the LL.B.
degree at Adelaide University this year. He
has done his studying and his politics to-
gether, without neglecting either, and has
generally got first-classes in his law subjects
He is articled to Glynn, M.H.R. Donald is
by profession a journalist, and suffers under
the handicap of being accounted the wittiest
man in the Assembly.
Premier Jack Verran is short and squat.
He generally occupies the middle of the
Treasury Bench in the Assembly, with At-
torney-General Denny beaming on one side
of him, and Treasurer Vaughan sardonically
smiling on the other. The vision evidently
gets on the nerves of the Opposition, for
Peake declared the other day in the House-
“ The Premier looks like Sir John Falstaff
between the Merry Wives of Windsor.”
Frederick Alderson, of N. S. Wales, who
has been appointed Chief Instructor in Phy-
sical Culture to the Commonwealth Cadets,
has been for years the brains behind the pub-
lic-school demonstrations in Sydney— -spec-
tacles which at various times have induced
visitors to dig into the dictionary to find the
thing that is more than “admirable.” Bv
the time Alderson has done with it, Austra-
lia’s kid army ought to be able to shoulder
its rifle without breaking its back.
Mrs. Margaret Hawdon Chambers, who
died a few days ago at St. Kilda (Vic.),
belonged to a family that did its share in
making Australian history. Her father ran
the first mail service between Sydney and
Melbourne, mainly with the help of Binghi.
Her brother Joseph was a well-known over-
lander, and was one of the leaders of the
first party to travel from Melbourne to Ade-
laide. The lady herself helped to found the
Melbourne Children’s Hospital.
James T. Boylan, who passed over to St.
Peter a few days ago, aged close on 90,
arrived in Auckland (M.L.) in 1647, and
watched the small collection of huts grow
for 64 years. He was a member of the first
Provincial Council of the province, and re-
presented City West for years. Though he
helped to found St. Pat’s Cathedral, Me-
chanics’ Institute, Auckland Savings Bank,
and a score of other local institutions, he
had for years past been very little heard of
in connection with any of them.
Melbourne’s oldest and best-known P.M.,
J. A. Panton, was at the laying of the foun-
dation-stone of the new city court the other
day. The ancient ex-beak, who last month
attained his 80th year, is still tall and
straight, but is almost blind. A special
toast was proposed for him, and, in the
course of a reminiscent response, he recalled
that in 1852 he was a Crown Lands Com-
missioner, in which capacity he had the same
status as a magistrate, and in those lively
days held courts mostly in the open air, or in
slab huts.
Robert Dodd, one of
the best-known press-
men in Victoria, died
a few days ago, after a
tough fight with a
painful disease. He
started his journey on
the Inky Way as agri-
cultural editor of the
Leader, and after-
wards became travel-
ling representative of
the_ Australasian, in
which capacity he vis-
ited practically every
station in Victoria and
Southern N. S. Wales.
Subsequently he was
promoted to be agri-
cultural editor, and
filled that chair with credit. He was
a good friend to many pressmen when they
were cubs at the game, and they will keep
him in affectionate remembrance.
Ex-Sergt. Ewing, of the N.S.W. police, who
died at Gunnedah lately, was the oflicer to
whom Spicer, the leader in the Lambing Flat
riots, surrendered. Ewing, of course knew the
whoie crowd, and winked as Spicer handed
himself over. The fact was that Spicer
and his mates had a run of claims that
"wanted capital,” and with Spicer’s consent
his mates gave him up to bag the reward,
lhey got it, and Spicer enjoyed the fruits
later on. The old ex-sergt., who had retired
-M years ago, formed the first police camp on
the Lachlan Goldfields—a camp that after-
wards grew into the town of Forbes.
Regarding a musician who has earned his
harp:—
J- Stevens, who died at Adelaide the other day,
,'?? was one of 10 boys who got their bread
tu t . an “ musical education at the expense of
the late Queen Victoria. He sang in her Chapel
Koyal, and wore her gorgeous gold lace (40 yards of
it) and white kid gloves. A fellow chorister was
air Arthur Sullivan, who, at 11, having written i
religious solo, dragged young Stevens along with him
to obtain from Jenny Lind permission to dedicate it
to her. It was after a mad chase of this lady—to
get her signature to a contract —that Stevens’s father
died suddenly. Stevens, sen., was musical; so was
his wife; and their daughter received music lessons
trom Mendelssohn, who was a frequent visitor at che
Stevens home in Birmingham. Before his voice
uro'ce A' Stevens sang at the opening of the big
Exhibition of ’5l, at the Duke of AVellington’s
funeral m St. Paul’s, and at Jenny Lind’s three
farewells. When he grew a bit he met Gounod, who
told him how to conduct “The Redemption” ; Richter,
who gave him the first baton he had used in Eng-
land; Sir Michael Costa (one of his curios was the
last baton this great conductor wielded) ; Dvorak.
Bridge, Stainer and other celebrities. He saw
Wagner conduct his own works, and personally waved
the little stick before Santley, Foli, Tietiens, Tre-
belli, Albani, Patey and Sims Reeves at his best. He
conducted for the first time in Australia on Christ-
mas night, 1887. and founded the Adelaide Orpheus
Society, which lately had its ccming-of-age birth-
day. Stevens knew two million anecdotes, and told a
good story. He wrote a great deal on musical mas-
ters, but his best reminiscences go with him.

Mrs. James Simpson, who died recently at
Brighton (Eng.), aged 96, was the widow of
one of the first settlers from Van Diemen’s
Land to follow Batman and Fawkner
across Bass’s Straits to the settlement by
the larra. He presided at the first meet-
ing held in Victoria (June 1, 1836), and
was appointed (on the motion of John Pas-
coe Fawkner, seconded by John Wood) to
arbitrate between individuals disputing on
all questions, excepting those relative to
land, with power to name two assistants
when he might deem fit. The Arbitrator at
that meeting was, practically, created the
chief man of the settlement. One of the
first, if not the first, arbitrations was that
between Henry Batman, brother of John,
and “John Pascal Fawkener.” Simpson on
that occasion called in Alexander Thomson,
M.D., and John Aitken to assist. For a
crust he kept a store on the eastern corner
of Flinders and King streets.
Touching the art of wearing knee-
breeches :
The late George Jenkins, of Melbourne, was re-
markable for more things than his bushy beard. He
stood alone among the knights of his Order as the
one man on this continent who had achieved the
“Sir” by flunkeyism, pure and simple. Mayors are
supposed to be public-spirited citizens, and Speakers
and Presidents are elected, by their fellows to authori-
tative positions. But Jenkins was never other than
a flunkey. When he was a young clerk in the rail-
ways he dropped into the Leg. Assembly hall one
night with a friend, and, pointing to the chair
where the clerk was dozing, said; That’s where /
mean to be.” And he got there. He was always “Oily
George,” and he greased his way from one position
to another. Meanwhile, a clerk of the Assembly
had “risen” to the softer clerkship of the Leg,
Council, and was styled Clerk of the Parliaments; So
George Jenkins, in due course, became that boss
flunkey, _ and was bo assiduous in biting the ears of
his official masters that after a while lie got himself
recommended for the proud distinction of C.M.G.
And so he went on, oozing oilness in Parliament,
and at “functions,” and everywhere that a free feed
was available, until Federation arrived. Then he
got himself made Clerk of the Commonwealth Par-
liament in order that he might figure as master of
the ceremonies incidental to the opening thereof.
The result was a bit of York jewellery, with knight-
hood to follow. Feeling himself to be a lethargic
object of ridicule in his new billet, among a crowd
of strangers, George Jenkins greased his way back
to his old place in the Victorian Leg. Council, and
there he stayed until his retirement last year.

The little Protector’s new commander is
A. C. Dnnn, a S. Australian who was at-
tached to the Australian Squadron from 1901
till 1904, and has lately been in charge of
the Maine, in Britain’s stay-at-home fleet.
After the Boer war the Maine was presented
to J. Bull by Uncle Sam, who sent it to the
scene of trouble as a hospital ship, and was
too tired or good-natured to whistle it back.
The S. Aus. Government has decided, after
thinking hard, that it is compelled to “retire”
J. G. O. Tepper from his post as Entomolo-
gist at the Adelaide Museum. The Septua-
genarian Act no fine distinctions; but
was it meant to touch cases where no initia-
tive is needed, and next to no action of any
sort, but only experience? There seems to
be no obvious reason why a man should be
a less capable bug expert even at 90 than
he was at 45.
Died in Perth last week Captain “Dick”
Piper, who was mine manager of the Broken
Hill Prop, in the days when Australia first
began to realise that it had a mountain of
silver in its innards. Afterwards he moved
on to the B. H. South, where he was general
manager. Those were days of rapid promotions
—when a mine that could only afford a man
ager at £2OO one year could (or thought it
could) afford one at £2OOO the next; and, if
“Cap’n Dick” had been able to stand oats
better, he would have taken permanent rank
then as one of the big mining men of Aus-
tralia.
TRUCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W. H. Judkins is very ill of inoperable cancer, and
the desperate remedy of trying to kill the cancer by
injecting cultures of the most malignant erysipelas
is proceeding. The patient accepts the position
calmly and with fortitude.— Daily paper.
When you were in the fight,
And while we yet were foes,
Each had his views of right;
But now the issue goes.
You, with your narrow view,
Suspecting every smile—
Joy must be banished too,
That you might banish guile !
O'erweighted with the task,
Shooting beyond the mark,
Seeing not Man, but a Mask,
And struggling in the dark.
IVe who can see the earth —-
Poverty, pain and stress—
Pale with the pangs of birth,
Waking to heaviness.
Vice in the sealskin coat.
Chartered to do its will;
Virtue without a groat,
That never eats its fill.
Crime of the sweated wage,
Crime of the rentlord’s slum,
IVorse than the wild beast’s cage;
Crime of the pain that’s dumb.
Crime of all sneaks and thieves,
In gaol or in cushioned pew ;
Debt that the Christian leaves
For payment to the Jew.
Crime of the hypocrite,
Banning the chance-got child;
Crime of the wed-unfit;
Crime of the babe defiled.
Crime of the smug, content,
Hypocrite pulpiteer,
Saying that pain’s God-sent,
Bleating that “God is here.”
Crime of disease and pain—
Cancer, consumption fell—
And the filthy father’s stain,
That makes the child’s life a hell.
These are the things we fight,
For the Common and the Elect,
Using Logic to help the Right,
And the Cause to scotch Effect.
Now that there comes to you
Fight so stubborn and dree,
You have one task to do —
Have but one enemy.
Over the Party cries,
Over the shrieks of clan,
The oldest forces rise—-
Kindness from man to man.
Old enemy of debate —
Once that your fight began,
Banished the party hate,
Seeing no Mask but the Man !
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

Like the Bank of New Zealand the Commercial
of Australia passed the sickest-looking of its assets
over to a Trust to nurse and realise upon as oppor-
tunity offered. Apparently the idea was that the
bank might show a better face to the world if it
didn’t have to always own up to having this dread-
ful amount of wreckage on the premises. Nowadays,
however, the bank apparently feels strong enough
and reformed enough to admit its old misdeeds; and
it does that all the more readily because the people
who produced the disastrous results in the boom-time
have long since disappeared. The accounts now ac-
cordingly put the matter quite plainly:—
Capital £2,212,969
Deduct estimated deficiency in con-
nection with Assets Trust . . 952,635
Net actual capital .. ..£1,260,334
From that, of course, has to be deducted the reserves
accumulated by the bank. As profit in excess of the
dividend requirements is made, it is practically all
written off ; in other words, it is going to replace
the non-existent assets —the froth that disappeared
after the boom burst. Reckoning the bank and the
Trust as one concern (which they really are, because
the bank is responsible for the debts of the Trust,
represented by old deposits), the position has
changed in recent years as follows: —
Pref. Writ- To
Dividend ten Reduce
Profit. 3 p.c. Off. Deficit. Deficit.

June, 1909.. £58,301 £31,760 £SOOO £21,541 1,058,850
Dec., 1909.. 66,711 31,760 5000 29,951 1,028,899
June, 1910.. 62,826 31,760 5000) 9 . „„„
Provident Fund .. 2000/ 24 ' 066
Dec., 1910.. 66,035 31,760 5000 29,275 975,558
June, 1911.. 63,239 31,760 5000 26,479 949,079
In two years the estimated deficiency has thus been
reduced by £109,771 ; and at that rate it would
take just upon 18 years to restore all the capital.
The question is whether it wouldn’t be far better
to have a cleaning up straight away. This could
be done with comparative ease by writing off some
of the present non-existent capital. Or, if it is
thought that there ought to be more share capital
in the business, some of the non-existent capital
could be written off and the balance could be made
up by getting in some new capital. It certainly
cannot do the bank any good to climb up to the
housetop once every six months and announce
that, though it professes to have so much capital, it
has lost nearly a million of it, and is busy screw-
ing and scraping to repair the damage. Moreover,
it can’t do any good to have it constantly before the
public that on some of its shares the bank pays no
dividend at all and on others only 3 p.c. No doubt
it can be argued that all these things haven’t ap-
parently affected it, seeing the progress it has been
making. Well, one never knows the progress that
it might have made. Also, it is just as well to re-
member that the country has been wonderfully pros-
perous during the past few years. It might be very
different if the public got the idea into its head
that the banks weren’t doing too well, and that their
position was none too strong. And the bank that
kept the public constantly reminded of past mis-
deeds would be likely to suffer most —and perhaps
most undeservedly.
Certainly the rights of the two classes of shares
would call for some adjustment. The old capital
of the bank is represented by 191,238 ordinary
shares fully paid to 10s. ( £95,619) ; the new
capital, £2,117,350, is in 211,735 pref. shares fully
paid to £lO. The ordinaries get no div. at pre-
sent ; the prefs. get 3 p.c. and cannot get more than
4 p.c. As the 4 p.c. would call for £84,694 and no
more, it looks as though the ordinaries might at
some future time get a very fat dividend. But the
ordinaries are outnumbered by the prefs., and each
share has the same voting power; so the prefs.,
with their overwhelming financial interest, are not
likely to let the ordinaries run away with big divi-
dends. Further, while restricted to 4 p.c., the pref.
shares will never reach par; and the public very
naturally has a prejudice—or is liable to be seized
with a prejudice—against a bank whose shares are
at a discount. And ordinaries will suffer from that
prejudice just as much as prefs. It should, there-
fore, be possible to arrange matters between the
two classes of shares to their mutual advantage—■
perhaps by putting all the shares into the one class,
giving the ordinaries one share for (say) seven of
the present denomination, and writing off as much
of the pref. capital as may be found necessary on a
revaluation of the assets. That, while cleaning up
the balance-sheet, would (1) give the ordinaries the
advantage of coming in on the same floor as the
prefs.; (2) allow the prefs. to get anything more
than 4 p.c. that the business can pay in the future;
(3) allow the ordinaries to get dividends forthwith
instead of having to wait indefinitely.
On recent profits the bank could pay 7 p.c. on
£1,400,000 ; and that could be made up as follows:
211,735 shares at £6 - £1,270,410; 191,238 shares
(ordinaries) ,< one £6 share for every seven 10s.
shares = £163,920; total, £1,424,330. The net
actual capital is now said to be £1,263,890, or
£170,440 less than the figures just suggested. The
deficit is merely a matter of estimate, however;
but if the estimate is correct —in other words, if
General-Manager Heron hasn’t got anything up his
sleeve—-it would be necessary to write off £5 in-
stead of £ 4 per pref. share; then, making the new
shares £5 instead of £6, there would be a total
reduction of £990,374 in the capital, and that
would leave a reserve to start with. The new capi-
tal would then be £1,200,000 in round figures; 7
p.c. would call for £84,000 a year (the profit for
last 12 months was £129,274) ; the holder of 10
present pref. shares, who now gets £3 a year,
would get £3 10s.; the holder of 100 ordinaries,
who now gets nothing, would'get £5.

months movement:—

thus amount to £7,087,607. The liquid assets are
equal to about 44 p.c. of that total—a very safe
holding. Including the Trust liabilities (oid de-
posits), the total liabilities amount to quite as
much as any bank should carry in proportion to its
shareholders’ funds (intact) ; and with an extension
of the business it may therefore be prudent before
long* to offer shareholders an opportunity to put up
more capital. But it will be advisable to have a
cleaning-up first.

The lay-by for the half-year is a little better than
the average, but it is still pretty wretched. It may
no doubt be that the bank is making more profit
than it shows, and is applying what it doesn’t show
to the further writing-down of assets or the building
up of an internal reserve or contingency fund. But
there is nothing at all to indicate that it is so. If
the bank is only making the profit it shows, then it
is doing mighty little to help itself along in the
dull days that are to follow the present extraordin-
ary prosperity. And it is quite possible that it may
need a good deal of help. It will be very generally
admitted that values of most securities —land, build-
ings, stock, etc.—are high at present; also commer-
cial profits are for the most part very good, and ac-
commodation that a bank wouldn’t have thought
about a few years ago is now being given quite
freely. The bank business taken during the past
few years may, therefore, be dangerous; and the
bank which has made the largest amount of advances
while the high values have prevailed, is in danger of
being hit the worst by a slump. Now, this bank
has made rather rapid strides of late years. Here is
a comparison

That is to say, it has added practically a third on
to its advances in the past 10 years, and the bulk
of it during the past three or four years. Undoubt-
edly this may be quite good business; but in times
of prosperity and high prices and profits like the
present, prudence demands that a substantial amount
of the profits should be put by to a contingency
account. The Bank of Victoria may be doing it;
but it is not doing it visibly.
The past 12 months have produced an unusually
rapid movement: —

This rapid increase in advances has made large in-
roads on the new deposits. The total liabilities are
£7,310,771; and the liquid assets are equal to less
than 29 p.c. of that amount. This is cutting things
very fine, unless a fairly large part of the advances
is of a very temporary nature. To work down to
284 p.c. may, of course, be good business; but there
are bankers who, in the present circumstances of
Australia, prefer 48 p.c. It may look a waste to
keep an extra 15 or 20 p.c. of money in cash or
liquid assets; but the bank with the big liquid re-
serve is the bank which is most likely to be able to
help its customers in time of stress (and, incident-
ally make exceptional profits for itself) ; and that is
the bank to which the cream of the business is likely
to go.
The earnings, calculated on capital and reserves
combined, have lately been as follows

The capital is £416,760 in pref. shares, and
£1,061,250 in ordinaries; total, £1,478,010. It
is a large amount for the size of the business; and
that no doubt is mainly why the profits are so
moderate. Thus, in spite of the recent rapid growth
of deposits, there is only about £4 ss. of the public’s
money in the business for every £1 of shareholders’
money. But that, while it tends to reduce profits,
also reduces the risks both of the depositors and
other creditors and of shareholders; for clearly the
shareholders’ £1 has more to do when it has to
protect £8 of deposit money than when it has to
do the same job for only £4.

Twenty-five years ago Queensland was beginning to
suffer from the same fever that it has a touch of to-
day. It had been enjoying some good times, and,
thinking the good times were going to last for ever,
it borrowed every penny it could lay its hands on,
and spent the money in a riot of extravagance. Of
course it didn’t call it extravagance; it called it
development.” The inevitable bad times came—
that is to say, drought and ticks and a few other
plagues came—and there was a terrible slump in
values. Sniffing trouble, depositors and other credi-
tors wanted their money; and when the banks and
other mortgage cos. asked their creditors to pay up
of course they couldn’t. Worse still, when the cos.
foreclosed, or thought about foreclosing, they saw
that they couldn’t get more than perhaps 50 p.c. of
the money they had advanced. The Government had
been hand-in-glove with the Q. N. Bank ; and to
help the lame dog over the stile the State borrowed
and dropped the proceeds into the till. Even that
didn’t save the dog; so the animal had at last to
face the situation and consent to have his tail cut
off. The arrangement made was that both State
and private depositors should forego interest on one-
foufth of the debt; that this one-fourth of debt to
the Government should be paid between 1917 and
1921; and that the private depositors’ one-fourth
should be paid only out of profits. That arrange-
ment is still in force, except that the debt to the
Government is now being expunged by annual instal-
ments (which may be paid in advance), the last
slice of the dead horse being due for removal in
1921.
Until the last of the one-fourth of the private de-
positors’ money is repaid, or until some other ar-
rangement is made, it seems that no dividend can
be declared; the directors have been so advised,
anyway. The shareholders (for, curiously enough,
the share capital was never wiped right out, as it
might well have been) are consequently very much
interested in the removal of the dead horse. This
shows what progress has been made with the job:—

The balance in hand is equal to the payment of
Is. 3d. in the £, which would make ss. 9d. paid
off altogether; and as it has taken 134 years to do
it, it doesn’t look like very rapid progress. But
there is another way of viewing the matter. Under
the arrangement which lasted down to seven years
ago, the Government got a bite out of the profits;
the last payment on that account is shown in the
first table. The repayments to the Government out
of profits then stopped. Further, large amounts
have been written off; apparently many of the old
assets were still over-valued. it wili be noticed
that, whereas in the two years 1905-6 only £36,000
in all was put aside to repay depositors, in the past
half-year alone £30,000 was put aside. Now, what
amount remains to be paid? The next table shows
that and some other things:—
Dec., Dec., June,

If £60,000 can be put aside per annum, it will
take approximately 124 years to pay off what is
owing. But as one year’s payment at that rate is
in hand, the period is reduced to 114 years. Whether
anything better than that can be done this paper
doesn’t know. Much depends on whether the man-
agement will think it necessary to go on writing off
profits. If the £10,275 written off in the past half-
year were applied to repaying the old creditors, the
disappearing trick would be accomplished in under
nine years, assuming present profits are maintained.
One feature of the present accounts calls for no-
tice. Hitherto there has been a line among the
assets- —“Private ledger accounts (debts in suspense
pending realisation of securities).” Originally these
presumably very doubtful debts stood at about
£750,000. No doubt they have bfeen written down;
very likely that is where a lot of the profit written
off has gone. Probably, also, a good deal of profit
not shown at all has gone into the same sink. Lately
values have so improved that, helped by the writing
down, these assets have been rapidly disposed of;
and now the item has disappeared altogether. This
may be taken to mean that one of the sinks into
which profits have hitherto been poured no longer
gapes for attention. Of course it doesn’t necessarily
mean that the bank’s assets are now all worth 20s.
in the £. The £770,977 owing to the depositors,
and payable out of profits, by the way, doesn’t
appear in the balance-sheet at all. If it did, the
assets would have to be inflated to that extent. This
very large amount written off (for that is what it
means) makes it more likely that the assets, in view
of recent rises in value, are worth something like
what they stand in the balance-sheet; but that
doesn’t alter the fact that, in order to really bring
them down to that value, the bank has to first earn
and then pay off £770,977. It may be that the
assets are worth more than the balance-sheet figures.
If so, a part of that £770,977 will, as it is paid
off, really become an internal reserve. It is ex-
ceedingly probable that this will turn out to be so.
As to current business: —

The new deposits have grown enormously. Advances
haven’t apparently increased much; but without a
doubt their character has changed. The cash posi-
tion is kept extraordinarily strong. It suggests
that the bank isn’t rushing advances. That is one
hopeful feature of the present situation; though
Governments are borrowing feverishly, and spending
money lavishly, to the great inconvenience in many
cases of other employers, the banks are going slow ;
and, in particular, they are not calling on John Bull
and Jamie Mcßull for assistance —“assistance” which
was the cause of the worst of the troubles in the
early ’9o’s.

June, 1910.. 16,482 10 ~ „ 2229 6283 7970 30,701
June, 1911.. 10,779 10 ~ „ 3973 5311 1495 32,196
Like most co-operative concerns, this one began
with a very limited capital, which presently, as
the operations grew, became much too small for re-
quiremcnts. Having 12 months ago acquired total
liabilities amounting to £16,130, and having further
outlay in view, the co. decided to get in more
capital. At that time, and for many years pre-
viously, it stood at £22,292. It has during the
year been increased to a level £40,000 ; and that is
none too much, seeing that the liabilities amount
now to £20,958. On the other side, however, there
ai 'c LPuid assets (cash and book debts) amounting
to £21,604. Practically all the new capital has
been put into the co.’s business premises, which are
now valued at £78,156. The past year’s profit
doesn t show up well when compared with the pre-
vious year’s; but the previous year’s was quite ab-
normal. For one thing, the poorer market for wool
in the past year hit the co. in two ways. (1) Had
the same prices ruled as in the previous year, the
commissions would have been £2826 more, accord-
ing to the management’s estimate. (2) With a dis-
appointing market, the co. shipped a larger quantity
of wool unsold. In all the circumstances, the re-
cord, m spite of the shrunk profit, may be regarded
as thoroughly good. Further, profits have been cut
into by increased expenses; and there is nothing to
grieve over in that. This is a co-operative concern ;
practically all the shareholders are clients; and,
while they are no more eager than other people to
pay big salaries and wages, they are certainly liable
to lose by a cheese-paring policy, which repels
good men. What the N.S.W. wool-growers, as a
class, owe to the co., can’t be put into figures; but
they can get some idea by comparing the charges
which have to be paid in Melbourne, where there is
no co-operative co., with those in force in Sydney.
Naturally the other brokers in Sydney owe this co.
something too; and just as naturally they are pre-
pared to pass the co. what is coming to it whenever
they get the chance.

The co. has had to spend £404 during the half-year
to meet Board of Health requirements, and another
£378 has gone in building additions; hence the
reduced profit. The premises are leasehold, with
something under 60 years to run; and nothing is
written off the value of this wasting asset, which is
a mistake. The real profit is, therefore, less than
the accounts show, as a rule. Some time ago the
co. was forced into making big alterations, which
plunged it deep into debt; but by dropping divi-
dends for a while, a credit balance has been
achieved. This little list shows the workmanlike
way in which the liabilities were wiped off: —
June, 1905.. ..£9745 Dec., 1908.. ..£3395
Dec., 1905.. .. 9078 June, 1909 .. .. 2185
June, 1906.. .. 7951 Dec., 1909.. .. 1684
Dec., 1906.. .. 7151 June, 1910.. .. 524
June, 1907.. .. 6015 Dec., 1910 (credit) 148
Dec., 1907.. .. 5083 June, 1911 .. ~ 766
June, 1908.. .. 4012
That last entry is, of course, before payment of the
current dividend. The co. has to pay a ground rent
of £IBOO, and that first charge, which has to be met
whether things are good or bad, largely accounts
for the poor return netted by the shareholders.
Still, under ordinary circumstances, a good deal
more than 21 p.c. has been earned lately, though it
has to be again pointed out that the profits are not
true profits until something is put aside to replace
the wasting capital represented by the leasehold
assets.

thus £52,500. There is nothing visible to explain
the shrinkage in the half-year’s profits; certainly
£750 put aside at the end of the previous half-year
may have been written off, but as all the reserves
are lumped, it is impossible to say whether this is
so or not. Gas sales have been steadily moving up.
In the half-year to the end of June, 1903, they
totalled £6965, or a shade over 18 p.c. p.a. on the
book value of the fixed assets (plant, mains, land,
buildings, etc.). In the past half-year they totalled
£12,336, or at the rate of over 26 p.c. p.a. on the
book value of the fixed assets. This means either
that the co. has been putting a good deal of undis-
closed profit into remunerative works, or that the
old works are more profitable than they were ; or it
may mean some of both. Those extensions of plant
out of profits have, however, to cover the depreciation
of the plant in general. In other words, instead of
putting by actual cash to renew works as they get
beyond repair, the co. puts that cash into new works
elsewhere, and trusts to being able to finance a.ny
big renewals as they fall due. What may be called
the cash position has varied as follows: —
Dec., Dec., June,
1903. 1910. 1911.
Liabilities.. .. £17,546 £17,053 £17,354
Liquid assets .. 3,859 13,412 12,922
Deficiency .. £l3 687 £3,641 £4,432
This means that the co. in 7-) years has (1) paid
the dividends; (2) paid for all repairs, renewals
and extensions; and (3) put by £9255. And, as
remarked just now, the extensions thus paid tor
have probably been pretty extensive. The co., there-
fore, has nothing to complain of in its past seven
and a-half years’ experience; it has indeed been re-
markably prosperous. Whether things are going to
continue like that is another matter; Queensland
will certainly have amazing luck if all future seasons
are like the past five or six have been. Neverthe-
less, the co. has so small a capital compared with its
turnover, and it has worked itself up into so strong
a position, that it would take a very bad knock to
put the dividends much below the present 10 p.c.
* * * *
Big Bill Baillieu has taken over that
lame dog in the manger, the V.D.L. Co.,_ paying
shareholders £39 a share, or £375,035, plus
£9565 for “commission, compensation and other ex-
penses.” Which probably means that the new co.
pays £375,035 and a few hundreds of the £9065
as “compensation.” The co. is to issue £460,000
4 p.c. debentures; £260,000 being first mortgage
and £200,000 seconds, both of 25 years’ currency.
There will be a sinking fund for redemption pro-
vided out of the profits of land sales, and .V.D.L.
shareholders may take, in lieu of cash, £25 m first
debentures, £2O in second, and £5 in fully paid-up
shares, or £SO of face value for each £39 per
share. Probably not a great deal of outside
money will have to be raised, as shareholders m the
V.D.L. have learned the lesson of sticking to a
leakv ship, and will probably elect to still stand by
it now that a live crew is pumping her out and
docking her. £IOO,OOO of working capital makes
the total money to be found £4B4,6oo—less, of
course, the amount that old V.D.L. shareholders
elect to take in debentures and shares.
Baillieu’s new co. may be expected to do quickly
what the idiotic old V.D.L. Cos. failed to do in a
century—hanging on to the proud job of being a
lord of the manor, and content to lose money in the
dignity. The new co.’s assets are not so big as the
acreage would suggest. The land was largely selected
in the manner Moses surveyed the Promised Land
from Pisgah. Button grass was taken because it
looked like the dear old English downs; and heavy
gum country was turned down because from a dis-
tance it lost its richness, and exaggerated only the
difficulties of clearing. In this way the V.D.L. Go.
missed 15,000 acres of excellent land at Marrawah,
and when a few young Tasmanian farmers discovered
and selected it, the V.D.L. Cos. vainly tried to steal
it all over again. Under the charter minerals are the
property of the co., and these rights may be valu-
able, because the V.D.L. Co.’s terms to the pro-
spector were impossible, and most of the _ country
has probably never been looked at, because in many
places the button grass would have to be stripped
to allow of it. It would have been better had Tas-
mania beaten its suscubus into decency or with-
drawal with a swinging land-tax; as.it is, the com-
monwealth tax has given to Tasmania the new pro-
vince it was afraid to take. Baillieu s Cos. is ener-
getic, and is out after profits, and to do that it
must cut up the land and sell it; so the new owner-
ship is a good thing. # #

Westralia’s June dividends amounted to
£110,460, making a total for the half-year of
£444 193 The July figures have so far reached
£76,719.
Bullfinches rose ss. on the official an-
nouncement of a bore-hole development at 210tt.,
but after it had dawned on the market that this rise
on a drill-hole assay, which indicated the possible
existence of Bft. of £4 ore ahead of the crosscut,
amounted to an increase of £125,000 in the mines
valuation, it drew in its horns a bit. According to
a local report, the co. will have to drive east to pick
up the continuation (if any) of the rich shoot
worked at and above the No. 1 level of the pros-
pecting workings.
’ Bull Ant (W.A.) is about to wind up,
which is about the best thing it can do. The cash
in hand is sufficient to pay shareholders 6s. in the
£ ; but if they wish it, Lionel Robinson and Dorrie
Doolette, who got the biggest cuts out of the flota-
tion, are willing to give them one Bullfinch Pro-
prietary for every two Bull Ants. In other words
these two, who have lately been buying up Bull Ants,
and who by this time must hold the bulk of the issued
shares, want to take the £63,000 cash in hand, and
give the other fellows Bullfinch scrip. By this
means they will get the money and pass on a large
parcel of Bullfinches without paying any brokers’
commission, and without running any risk of knock-
ing the market to smithereens.
* * * *
Tindals (W.A.) still exists, but how it
manages to do so is a mystery. Returns, giving
details of so many tons crushed for so many ounces,
are handed out every now and then; but, as usual,
there is absolutely nothing to guide the inquirer
as to what the output is worth. It may be fine gold,
or it may be only half-and-half, and in the absence
of this information, it is impossible to say how the
co. is doing. Judging by past results, however, it
can’t be having a very good time.
G. P. Doolette presided at the Boulder
No. 1 meeting, held in London on July 15, and did
his level best to cheer up shareholders. So far
neither of the mines worked by the co. in Groper-
land has earned its salt; but in speaking of the
future, Doolette held out great hopes of success,
and expressed what he called his honest belief that
when he again met shareholders he would be able
to report a better state of things and a bigger credit
balance.
* * * *
A comparison of two lengthy reports on
the Ida H. (W.A.) by the present managers (Hooper,
Speak and Feilding) and the late boss (Campbell
Shaw) shows that the former’s estimate of winze
values below 1000 ft. is 97dwt., against the latter’s
30dwt. Shaw accounts for the difference by stating
that the Hooper party’s samples were all taken from
the north side of the winze, where, owing to a
break in the country, the richest portion of the ore
is located. Shaw confirms The Bulletin’s assertion
that the co.’s reconstruction was unwarranted, and
has gone to London to ask the Board why it got rid
of him, and to present the other side of the picture
to shareholders. He estimates the gold in sight at
30190 z. (worth 795. 6d. per oz.) between the Nos.
6 and 8 levels, and the No. 9 level reserves at
64230 z. The profit for the last 15 months’ working
is given as £3789 ; but, with the new shoot to work
on, the monthly profits from the time crushing is
resumed in September are expected to average about
£1250.
* * * *
Having got a tiny reef and a few rich
assays at 100 ft., Greenfinch Prop. (W.A.) is telling
everyone that a battery is now a certainty. In other
words, the market is to be considered first. Next to
nothing has so far been done in the shape of de-
velopment, and with so much more to do, the pur-
chasing of a mill at the present time would be
simply farcical. Properly handled, the mine might
have some hopes of moderate success, but in anew
centre like Boodallin, it would be making the pro-
perty subservient to a lot of market gamblers to
order a plant on the resujts so far obtained. Sup-
posing the shoot dies early, as most of them do on
the Yilgarn field, what then?
Lord Nelson, St. Arnaud (Vie.), has de-
parted from old Zeb Lane’s plan of giving the height
of the stopes, and nowadays no one can quite tell
the extent of the ore reserves. Work is all in deep
ground. The 2225 ft. level yields three-fourths of the
stone, the rest being from the 2085 ft. stopes. There
has been an inversion of the usual order of things
at depth. The 1903 ft. level was abominably poor,
2085 ft. improved and 2225 ft. now gives the fair-
grade stone. So the question is, Is there a local
enrichment such as happened at the bottom of the
Long Tunnel Extended? At the bottom, 80ft. of
payable stone has been found south of the. crosscut,
where in the two upper levels there was little pay-
dirt. So here is a decided gain. North, in a drive
397 ft. long, there is good stone 175 ft. from the
crosscut. Beyond this small shoot is another 60ft. of
payable quartz, with the rest of the ore well min-
eralised, but poor. This invests the next sink with
the hope of longer pay shoots, though bonanzas are
rarely alighted upon in such tight country. Throw-
ing in old tailings, 14,939 tons last term returned
£20,524, won at a cost of £15,899; so that even
with the new slimes plant, there cannot be more
than a shilling to divide for the current term, see-
ing that work is now all in the bottom stopes. The
next lift is to be 175 ft., so it will be a hard race
between the sink and the battery.
* * * *
The world learns that control of Cock’s
Pioneer, Eldorado (Vic.), has passed from Jack
Dane to Hector Macdonald, of old North Lvell fame.
Dane has had plenty of time to option the claim,
but he hit London when, owing to busted Westralian
mines, J. Bull didn’t want much of his El Dorado.
Why go to J. Bull at all? Cock’s Pioneer is one of
the neatest little shows in the Cabbage Garden.
The barge is in a position to work, and there are
four floors in the face which prospect well. Past
records prove that payable gold exists in the wash,
so what is there to fear? The show won’t run
awav, and if shareholders dispossess themselves of
the "spirit of greed, they could let profit run up till
a second barge could be built. To chuck one of the
finest hydraulic sluicing shows in the State into the
hands of Bull, merely for a temporary gain, is mad-
ness. The thing to do is to also close down on Hec-
tor Macdonald’s power of attorney, and work the
mine for the company.

Bravo, Peter Mcßride! And it’s to be
hoped he will keep both feet firmly on Mother Earth
over the Napoleon’s float (Vic.). The story that
John Bull is to put up £500,000 to test 2500 acres
of alluvial ground at Golden Point, near Ballarat,
after he has spat out of his mouth the nasty taste
left by the Moorlort, Rutherglen, and Ararat
fiascos, is thin pap. If the £500,000 is up. Mcßride
might be justified in giving leases, provided they
are kept manned ; but if only nominal capital is up,
then Mcßride should at the most let the promoters
take the pick of 500 acres at Napoleon’s, and return
the balance for other cos. Half a mile of the Golden
Point gutter would be enough for anyone to bite off
if the old run of gold lives down.
New* Good Hope, Gippsland, looks a very
sick proposition these times. The mine manager s
report on July 10 was: “Crosscut from Tunnel A in
290 ft. ; hard sandstone.” At the beginning of Oc-
tober last year call-payers were exhorted thus: The
crosscut from the lower level should be kept go l ug
to intersect the Good Hope shoot of gold at depth.
Your mine-manager estimates the cost of driving at
40s. per foot. In conclusion, I am glad to say the
prospects of the mine never looked better. And
that was in the face of a crushing which yielded 3i
weights to the ton, and was said by the management
to be “disappointing.” #
Charters Towers mines for the first half
of this vear paid £43,100 in dividends Calls took
£32.686, leaving a surplus of £10,414. The gross
value produced for the six months waj £261,135.
The New Brilliant Freehold headed the list of divi-
dends with £ 22,500. The East Mexican gave
£12,350, Brilliant Extended £6250, and the Cum-
berland P.C. £2OOO. Brilliant Extended crushed
26,980 tons of stone, Mills’ United 12,631 tons, New
Brilliant Freehold 9328 tons, Brilliant Stockholm
6747 tons, and East Mexican 4382 tons. Mills’
United did a good deal of development, and is now
crosscutting between its shaft winze and another
winze sunk on the hanging-wall side of the forma-
tion, preparatory to opening a level 300 ft. below
No. 14. The level will be driven eastwards, in the
direction of No. 2 underlay shaft. This is the most
important prospecting work doing at the Towers.
The Brilliant Block hasn’t yet been able to locate the
Day Dawn formation at 2150 ft. vertical. At the old
Brilliant P.C. shaft they will soon be in a position
to again explore the 1437 ft. level, which is supposed
to have followed the Day Dawn reef.
* * * *
Mount Morgan tries hard to improve its
smelting practice, but the change over to more
economical conditions is slow. Last month’s smelt-
ing included 13,763 tons copper ore, 718 tons
silicious ore, 7097 tons pyritic copper ore from Many
Peaks, and a quantity of copper precipitate from
the mundic works. All these products returned
563 tons blister, containing copper 557 tons, gold
72460 z., and copper matte containing approximately
£BSOO worth of metals. This return doesn’t admit
of a close calculation of the average extraction per
ton of ore. It is, however, pretty certain that the
average extraction is less than 21 p.c. copper from
the mixed Mount Morgan and Many Peaks ore, which
are now being smelted in the proportion of three to
one. This extraction may appear low, but it is
helped by the high gold contents, which, in this re-
turn, amount to a shade over 13oz. per ton of copper.
A total of 21,578 tons were smelted, making the
fourth successive return with increased quantities of
pyritic fluxes and a larger output of matte. The ex-
cessive production of matte and its lower grade is
due to the large quantity of sulphur in the pyritic
ore ' *
The bears continue to tip Mount Elliotts
as good selling; but there is a risk in the game.
Obviously a mine that can show a gross profit of
£IOO,OOO for six months’ run isn’t one to take
liberties with, more particularly when the financial
position remains a strong one after the payment of
15s. per share in dividends. Then there are rich
surface shows to be picked up in a district that is
rich in the red metal. No less than 500 tons of
rich oxidised ore were sent away from this Cloncurry
country last month, and among the largest consign-
ments is some high-grade stuff from two new mines,
the Answer and the Limestone. The owners of the
Answer netted £ll per ton in Cloncurry, the ore
being high-grade oxidised stuff. A team of 47 camels
is carrying rich ore from the Limestone mine to the
railway. Perhaps Elliott is playing a waiting game;
but its idle smelting plant certainly suggests the
purchase of custom ore. #
At Gympie the most important recent
event has'been the cutting of the Inglewood reef
some 370-odd feet from the shaft in the No. 2 S.
Great Eastern. This crosscut is at a depth ot
1750 ft., but the shaft is now 1945 ft. deep, so it is
likelv that a deeper crosscut will be started when
the shaft is down below 2000 ft The dionte forma-
tion, now being prospected, carries several reefs, but,
as a rule, the stone is low-grade; so it may take
some time to prove whether the Inglewood reef at
1750 ft. is payable. South Glanmire and Monkland
next door, recently cleaned up a crushing of biz
tons of Inglewood stone for 2900 z. smelted gold.
Costs are kept down, and 9dwt. per ton is made to
return a small profit. At levels above 990 ft. the
Inglewood doesn’t carry the huge body of crushing
stuff worked by the Scottish Gympie below 2000 ft.
It should be remembered that Gympie formations
are greatlv influenced by dykes and crosscourses, and
a concentration of gold has occurred only when the
geological conditions are favorable. #
No. 3 North Columbia, Gympie, crushed
819 tons for 1019 oz. smelted gold. This crushing
was obtained from the 1656 ft. level, the workings
extending 262 ft. north and 116 ft. south from the
western crosscut. At the end of the north drive
there is 18in. of crushing stuff, showing a little
gold, in good country. _ Some of the stopes disclose
large and payable bodies of crushing stuff, from
which a small quantity of specimen stone can be
picked. No. 2 N. Columbia, which at one time pro-
duced many rich specimens, is now seldom heard
from At the 1468 ft. level they are driving south
from the end of the long eastern crosscut, there is
a small vein of stone, and it is intended to rise on
this. In this show, hard conglomerate or fossil
country is often found below the black slate that
carries the gold. The conditions change so frequently
that mining at Gympie is always canted on j^ nde |
highly speculative conditions. At present, No. and
North Columbia is in good heart, while No. 2—once
such a favorite —is putting up a hard struggle.
Bluff Colliery Cos. (Q.) held another meet-
ing the other day, making its twelfth. The output
of saleable coal for the half-year was only 5/26
tons, as against 8301 tons for the previous half ,
the shrinkage is ascribed to active competition.
The Admiralty has ordered 300 tons, to be delivered
as a test between October and December ; but the co
isn’t sure that it can deliver the goods, as it is
suffering from its chronic complaint, want of money.
The coke works that at one time had prominent
mention seem to have dropped right out. One little
item that pleases the co. is that at the Mammoth
three leases have been granted. For this the co. has to
thank the Denham people; when Mullie Kidston was
in power he had ideas of making a State coal mine
there. However, Willie and his ideas have now
a State job, and the co., which spent £ 10,000 in
development, has at least some ofthe
wanted. But the mere possession of the leases won t
make the co. fat. *

lnfp it has been a deal harder to make £4 or £5

Stanley Reward, like many another of
Tassv’s tin whistles, is very much on the flat side.
Little J. B. Lewis is the anchor just now. If he re-
ports adversely, the flag will go half-mast instanter,
as was the case after he sounded Cybele and found
her a iarle. The claim, on the north-west side of
the island, was to bring check-suits to many of the
faded aristocrats of Collins-street. Fourteen shafts
have however, cut a reported 25ft. of wash down
to 4ft Below is png, resting on the limestone
bottom. The show is hampered by the poverty of
flip dirt bv the heavy wash and by its shallowness.
As a standtov the co.has a lode, which the manager
wants prospected. Lewis will have his say on this,
and as P the calls have rolled in nicelv, a last round
can be fought on the lode. The price paid for the
claim was £IOOO cash and £12.500 in paid-up*
As things have gone, the vendors ought to have paid
a premium to get their show tested. #
FOR SALE Cheap, Second-Hand, 15 Break H.P.
Tangye Gas Engine, in good order. Apply Manager,
Bulletin Office, 214 George Street, Sydney.*
Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are

The BELL’S ASBESTOS CO., LTD., London, hav-

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Wilfley.” It is continuous, and
consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in a tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported on
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior to allow the liquid to be
withdrawn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the drum the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
means of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this filter.* [2

My Dear Aetni, —
On a drab afternoon last week Amelia J.
>upped up to a glass door that enclosed
the farmers and Settlers’ conference. Hav-
rng achieved a hurried glance at the length
and breadth of rural bone and beef inside,
she encountered a plump male Peri at the
?ate of the Hayseed Parliament. Amelia
has hayseeds and prize pigs in her own
family, so she understands the atmosphere.
Any women farmers, or lady silk-worms, or
females of any kind in there V” Amelia asked
in a whisper, and became more buried in her
hat. Mr Wetherspoon’s gun didn’t go off,
but the platform showed signs of weakening
interest m the rural worker, and stared at
the glass door and the feminine headgear
outside it. The body of the hall twisted its
neck, and turned round to find out what the
platform was gazing at. “Women farmers!”
'•aid the Pen. “No, there’s none of them,
an -j - no I women of an y kind in there.” He
said it slowly, as if they were taxes or ticks,
and wouldn tbe allowed in anyhow. Where-
upon Amelia J. fled into the friendly street
!>he had tipped up the deliberations of the
milk and butter department for nearly 60
seconds.

snapped by the Daily Mirror on the door-
M, e P of the Brompton christening party,
"he infant Margaret Sydney looked wise
enough to be a Home Secretary when the
camera caught her eye.

Pianist F. S. Kelly took charge of the
>aton when the orchestra tuned up one
afternoon last week in St. James’s Hall
His own composition, “Serenade in E
All nor, lor solo flute, with harp, horn and

distance, was lucky in having Lemmone to
pipe like Pan. The body of the hall had a

tails drooped and “wept” in the latest
node. She was sandwiched between two
young daughters with close-fitting skull caps
on their heads. Rachel Joy, the Morton in-
ant, was presumably tuning up her little

tions. . Mrs. Forbes Angus took her trim
figure into a front seat. Conductor Joseph

At the Dolores recitals in the Town Hall
the American pianist, Shrimplin, was on the
stool, with an occasional look-in by Edward
"• Sykes, most capable of accompanists. Mr.
bhrimplin has a worried expression and a
ock of front hair that gets lost in his fore-
head in acute crises. He needn’t worry about
his music—it flows along very nicely. A
small but appreciative audience, with an un-
quenchable thirst for encores, stamped the
floor at the first song-burst. There’s con-
siderable charm in the Dolores ballad sing-
,nf> because the back seats can hear every
syllable, a luxury they are not accustomed to
when the minor song-bird bites her English
between her teeth. The first-concert audi-
ence included Prof. Anderson Stuart, ac-
companied by the slim elegance of Mrs.
Stuart, with the plumpness of Artist Long-
staff to make a trio; Mrs. Ashburton Thomp-
son and a party; and a small sprinkle of
Society; and those who went once mostly
went twice.
Longstaff’s portrait of Prof. MacCallum,
with only a mild showing of University mil-
linery, is an intimate likeness of the genial
original. Probably it comes next to, or
even alongside of, the same artist’s “Lady in
Black, ’ in color and composition. Its tone
is moderate, and the subdued hint of red and
purple gown that hangs behind the coat of
every-day wear, is merely suggestive of
academic midnight oil. This picture does
everything but talk. Edmund Fosbery’s por-
trait, side-face, is painted in a cleverly-posed
attitude, with a characteristic “Discipline
must be maintained” expression in the tail
of the visible eye. There’s a small gaud—a
L.M.G.—just escaping from under the coat
collar. It is a fine likeness, and the original
is well known to the police.

links under water; nevertheless, golfer:

bottle up some of this superfluous energy,
hed be able to uncork a good brand of
water-proof militia for future service.

Half Sydney (or thereabouts) is nursing
its own or somebody else’s influenza. The
outbreak is serious enough to have de-
manded the earnest attention of managing
directors and such like. One bank magnate,
having heard that 17 members of a neigh-
bor s. staff were “down,” called science to
his aid, and next day every hand had a cake
ot camphor passed to him, with urgent in-
structions to sniff it earnestly.
At the Glaciarium the black velvet skirt
still waltzes to the tune of “The Blue
Danube. Miss Harris, who grew into lone-
?£ e ®f. es buite lately (she is a daughter of
Matthew Harris), is getting clever on her
skates, and achieves many twirls on the ice
patch in velvet upholstery. Mrs. Rich
glides round in the same rich pile at a speed
that passes most of this season’s traffic.
Mrs. Bonthorne puts her pace on in a snuff-
brown tailor-made. Miss Lamb is usually
sage green on skates. Allander. in black
tights with a short Norwegian jacket, shakes
one leg in _ the air and revolves on a few
crumbs of ice. The mere novice skates on
his head; and the girl who is collecting
ideas tor the August carnival, sits down be-
side a sledge and wonders how she’ll teach
it to collect a prize.
Alcide Capitaine, who has black velvet
eyes, an ivory complexion, and slender supple
i 1 cll ,“ bs tbe air in gymnastic twirls at
the Tivoli. She began life in Turin, and
carries with her the liquid notes of Northern
Italy. Coming before the public at the age
ot three, she has since then had time to un-
pack wardrobe all over the globe. She
looks 2d, and admits she’s more. She hasn’t
a line ip her smooth, oval face; she has
heard of female nerves, but doesn’t know
them by sight ; she has a digestion that can
absorb anything—she’d swallow the paper-
bag if that kind of cookery were put before
her, and not notice the parchment flavor—
she s never ill, and she’s only tired when she
takes a holiday. “I mean this —if I rest more
than one week, or perhaps two weeks, then
I begin to feel —how shall I say it? Not ill,
because I am never ill. But more dull in
my spirits. Never ill? Perhaps once or
twice; for I have had the influenza, but kept
on my work through it. So, though Igo to
Turin to see my mother and brothers every
year, I seldom take holidays.” Thus says
Alcide, whose small head, with its crisp
waves of black hair taken back into a classic
knot, seems to have got into the wrong cen-
tury when it retires under the huge black hat
ot to-day.
This is the era of muscle on female fore-
arms. "Physical culture for girls,” says the
prospectus of every hoarding school. “It is
good so far as it Will go, the movement of
the arms, the swinging club, the exercises
for girls—they are so pretty! And—oh, yes
—they are quite lady-like. But for real
strength to build up—l think the boy’s way
best. He goes over the parallel bars. He
gets, if he trains the right way, more lasting
benefit. JNo; the girls, as I have seen them
in other countries, do not exercise enough.
It they want to be really strong they must
go much farther in gymnastics and be—shall
I say it?—not so lady-like, but more like
A oy j S ’ use their legs for the high jump.”
And Alcide Capitaine, who coos to you like a
gentle dove, has many golden reasons to
prove she knows her subject.

Coronation capers on Thursday Island
were many. The numerous goats became a
frenzied mob when the guns banged a salute
or something unexpected, and the local milk
supply nearly churned itself into butter. Su-
rety—. * te> black and tan—gathered to-
gether in an open-air corroboree. The
Bishop of Carpentaria looked in at the
revelry. Malays, Japs, Chinese and white re-
sidents wore their best light-weight garb, and
squatted round while speeches were made
about George and Mary’s June party. Sports
for youngsters in the tropic afternoon, and a
“Coronation ball” at night, made Thursday
island perspire freely.

The Magyar brand of bodice is ahead of
competition. It is, like every other present
treak ot female adornment, designed for the
slim, and gives a champagne slope to the
shoulders of the shadowy feminine forms
that now haunt the drawing-rooms of the
Blest. The slender grey and pink brides-
maidens at the Willis-Osborne orange-blos-
soming wore Magyar in their sleeves and
shoulder lines. For the Fat it is anathema.
Magyar can be cursed in any language that
comes handy when it bulges' under the arm
and gives another billow to the too rotund.
Paris papers affirm that fashions for the
female form are where they were—quite
thin, admitting the demise of the hobble, and
allowing an extra half-yard in the width of
the skirt. Plump persons will continue to
blush for their waist-line.

Lady Dudley has an article in the August
Lone Hand on her pet hobby, “Bush Nurs-
ing”—which she remarks is “philanthropic”
rather than “charitable.” She starts on a
good wicket with —
Admiring the straightforward simplicity of the
Australian character, it seems to me that' I cannot
do better than put upon paper, in the simplest and
most direct way, a short record of my impressions
of bush nursing.
Having dropped her official mantle as Gov.-
Generalress, she writes freely in reply to the
criticism which got its teeth into the earlier
bush nursing scheme, and frankly admits
the failure of the first draft of the organisa-
tion. Her generous attitude towards ad-
verse criticism, and her effort to establish
the thing on a better footing before she
sailed away, reflect the charm of her late
Ex.’s sweet and candid nature.
The first N. S ; Wales bush nurse to carry

new scheme, hurried from Sydney for Jinda-
byne last week. She took with her the
euriously appropriate name of Miss Twelve-
trees.

“Oliver’s Kind Women” is Philip Gibbs's
just-to-hand novel, from D.vmock’s. Oliver
m a careless young spark, with a charming
appearance and a slack conscience about his
tailors bills. He has a large circle of
women friends, who lend him money and
give him good advice. Rut even a Greek
profile can t propitiate a landlady beyond a
reasonable period. Gibbs puts ‘together a
pleasant story, that hinges on journalistic
life in London. Lady Katherine Goldstein,
a one-time writer of social pars, is a vivid
character-sketch. She frankly quarrelled with
her gilded nest, and ached for the Bohemian
society of her fellows. “In our muddy
boots, said one of them, “we are able to
stroll upon her velvet pile carpets. In our
threadbare trousers we sit upon real Chip-
pendale. . The noble condescension of her
flunkeys is like a benediction.”
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORLAN.
From London :
Carrie Haase has hit upon a promising idea by
giving a recital of Australian ballads; but an Aus-
tralian male elocutionist, with bush knowledge and
sympathies, would work it to better advantage. Miss
Haase drew her selection mainly from The Bulle-
tin. with Gordon and Kendall as representatives of
the older school. The best notice appeared in the
Times, whose critic hailed the recognition of the
younger Australian singers as “refreshing,” and re-
joiced that the age which regarded Adam Lindsay
Gordon, with his “mawkish sentimentality,” as the
representative Australian poet had passed away. The
Times’ critic considers that Australian sentiment is
best reflected in the verses of Victor Daley—“a poet
