MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 24, 1911.
My Deab Yabkie, —
. The tall Linlithgow is visiting at Wid-
rigal, one of the Manifold stations near
Camperdown. It was one of the country
places that often saw his father. The son,
however, is staying with Edward Manifold ;
his father usually put up at the old home-
stead with Chester of the clan. The original
station was cut up into runs for the several
sons. On the death of Manifold, sen.,
Chester took up his abode at the home-
stead ; lut recently he built himself a man-
sion on the hill. The new house has several
fine panels by Australian artists in the hall,
rnese depict incidents in the station his-
tory of the pioneer Manifold, who came
from Tasmania. The site of the present
station was then a swamp fringe, but was
afterwards drained and transformed into
marvellous dairying country. Manifold, in
his search for solid land, waded for miles
through this slushy area. His sons saw no
good in the damp expedition ; but the father
waded on. At last he climbed a hill, from
the top of which he saw a small area of dry
earth. That with its well-drained surround-
ings is now the station. The cold-footed
swamp-searching is the subject of the first
panel. The second pictures the attempts to
hold the place against the neighboring
blacks, who were actively hostile. The
other panels continue the yarn up to the
wealth and plenty of smiling acres, with
Cow as the goddess thereof.
The wonder is that Camperdown has
escaped being called Manifoldville. The
whole place is Manifold. There is a Mani-
fold Park, and Manifold Gardens blossom
bravely. Manifold-street runs at a great
pace through the town, and, in the middle
of it, a great clock with Manifold printed
on it has been ineffectually trying to tell
the awed inhabitants that the time is not
yet ripe for a land tax.
The death of Clerk of Parliaments Jen-
kins revives many recollections. Nothing
was more typical of the man than his much-
quoted “Do the thing properly!” when the
Exhibition Trustees asked what expense
tney should go to for the Duke of York’s re-
ception. When he had secured his first
little tin tag, “Jenks” was the proudest man
in Melbourne. About that time, a Parlia-
mentary party visited the wilds of Apollo
Bay on some mission. The accommodation
was rough and limited, and the only bed
was duly claimed by Jenkins. John Woods,
the Minister for Railways, who was a
martyr to rheumatism, was offered a shake-
down. Woods electrified the air for a few
minutes, and finally announced that if there
was any member of the party in worse
health than himself he might have the bed ;
but he would be scorched before he would
be C.M.Geed out of it.
The late Allan McLean’s reputation in
the House as a teller of good stories was
helped by his drawl. One of his brightest
was about the time he drove some cattle
across a creek with the assistance of a
farmer and the unassistance of a boy. After
the boy had done his worst, the farmer
offered up a prayer which, according to
McLean, ran this way: “Lor-rd, You know
1 don’t tr-r-rouble You often! Please droou
that boy.” A description of the farmer
was always appended: “This gen-tle-man
was a fr-r-riend of mine at Maa-a-fra. He
was not wliat you would call a ree-lee-gious
man; that is to say, he was not de-monstra-
tive in his ree-lee-gion. But he was a goo-d
man; he always voted for me, whether I
was r-right or wr-rong.” Allan’s stock
anecdote, assisted by the accent, was always
accepted as very humorous in pawky circles.
The management of the Sheffield Choir,
whilst in Melbourne, showed no particular
regard for the public that pays its money
and looks for punctuality in business. There
was, as a rule, a leisurely slowness in start-
ing the long concert; and on the Wednesday
night, when “Elijah” was the job in hand,
the multitude suffered a wearisome wait for
the arrival of Lord Dudley, as though his
Ex. were the Lord of Heaven, and “Elijah”
couldn’t begin without him. On the pre-
vious night a miscellaneous programme was
interrupted by the ceremony of presenting
souvenirs to the learned musical doctors,
Coward and Harriss. This was described
in next morning’s paper as “a pleasant inci-
dent in the evening” ; but it didn’t seem
pleasant to the honest citizens in ’way-back
seats who wanted entertainment. They
grew very restive under the infliction of in-
audible speeches concerning matters in
which they were uninterested.
On Saturday night the Sheffield Choir
raised its joint and several voice to express
miscellaneous views on God and Empire—
or, perhaps, Empire and God. There was a
big congregation, which sat through the “pa-
triotic” programme with a carefully-schooled
demeanor of interest and importance. The
best item of the evening was “God of Our
Fathers,” chorally thundered as if it were a
kind of personal reminiscence. But the
audience reserved its attention for' the ap-
pearance of Lady Norah Noel. There was
a hush as she came forward, wrapped
in pink silk and a pained expression; then
she proceeded to turn over a small, gilt-
edged volume, from which she sang a well-
known Hibernian air, and contrived to make
the melody another injustice to Ireland.
Afterwards she warbled a familiar foreign
piece, and showed that she is privately con-
vinced she has a voice, but is determined
never to divulge her awful secret.
The Sheffielders probably enjoyed their
party at Mrs. Albert Miller’s more than any
other function they were roped into. For
one thing the run out along Toorak-road is
almost like a country trip. Melbourne,
rather flat in other parts, indulges there in a
few undulations; and when the air is clear
there is a distant background of mountains.
For another thing, the house was filled with
early “Cootamundra” wattle, whose gorgeous
golden blossoms drew a chorus of admiration
from the hostess’s North Country compa-
triots. In the music room, Rita Hope at the
piano, and Amadio, with his flute, made
melody, and Gertrude Winkelmann and
Gregor Wood sang. The Choir, not to be
out of it, added two numbers to the pro-
gramme.
Rupert Bunny was to open his picture
show at the Athenaeum on Monday after-
noon, when Governor Fuller was booked to
make remarks suitable to the occasion.
Bunny, since his arrival, has been strug-
gling to get to work; but he finds himself
handicapped by the severely business-like
atmosphere of his new studio. It makes
him feel “like a dog on a racecourse,” he
says.
Squatter Sam McCulloch, of the Grecian
profile, came to Melbourne at steeplechase
time, and has been tooting around in a
mustard-tinted motor. He appears to have
preserved his fondness for dancing, and pre-
sumably his poetic fancy for Melba will
bloom again in the spring when the Diva
arrives. Sam McCulloch was a picturesque
equerry to the Queen of Song last time she
was in Melbourne. They were much about
together for months and months. But even-
tually long Dick Nevill resumed his former
position of Lord-in-waiting, and gossip
changed its tune again. As regards the
cheery and tempestuous Nellie Melba, gossip
must have formed a large repertoire of
tunes.
Leila Doubleday, according to a judicial
hearer who knows all about her, played well
enough at her debut concert in London, but
was not quite up to her best form. Ner-
vousness prevented her from doing as well
as she will do next time. The big news-
paper criticisms were reckoned fair. All of
them spoke of her artistic talent and musi-
cal instinct; moreover, the London D.T.
said: “She plays more perfectly in tune
than almost any violinist we have ever
heard. ’ And to say that is to credit the
girleen with positive genius ; for to be always
in tune with vocal or fiddle notes is to be
wonderful.
Tall Talbot, who acted as manager to the
intractable Calvd in some of her Australian
touring, went off to Capetown last week—
tor the Taits—to meet Clara Butt. He will
prepare the way for the rich-toned giantess,
and leave paragraphic bouquets for her at
editorial sanctums what time she is making
a triumphal march through the land of
gold, doppers and diamonds. Subsequently
Mrs. Butt is to invade Canada, and the
iaits would have their Talbot follow her
there and eventually bring her back to Aus-
tralia. But he would prefer to bring himself
back in the meantime, reckoning that this is
a very good country not to be out of for more
than a year at a stretch.
On Friday night, for their farewell, the
i Jmimer-Denniston Co. revived “The Second
Mrs. Tanqueray,” with Mrs. Brough in her
old part of Paula. Nearly every old Brough
lover was packed away somewhere in the
crowded house. At curtain-time, Mrs.
.Brough was overwhelmed with ornamental
vegetables, and Plimmer spoke a fe'v part-
ing words. “We will be back again in a
year with a fresh lot of good plays,” he
said ; and the house said “Hooray!”
The suburban mayoral dance is no cheese-
paring function nowanights. His Washup lays
every red cent he can spare, for he knows
that the chained man next door is watching
him closely, and will endeavor to go one
better when his turn comes. The district nobil-
ity and gentry eagerly accept his Worship’s
invitation, and watch closely to see that none
ot the acceptors dodge the “return” (at half
a guinea per head) towards the end of the
season. Those who neglect the second affair
are promptly labelled Mud or Dust—and so
the round game of Suburban Sassiety has
gone on. This year, however, Mayor and
Mayoress Hughes, of St. Kilda, whose dance
was an 18-carat one, firmly refused the cus-.
tomary return.” Prahran followed suit, and
other places where mayors flourish are ex-
pected to do likewise.
Caulfield, the place where horses run
dead and alive, lately had a mayoral fling
ror the first time on record. Mayor and
Mayoress “Sinjin” Hall were the respon-
sible parties, and they were aided and
abetted by 500 of the Elect. Brighton
hurried across country and joined in. The
hostess led the revels in a gold and white
tunic over an oriental satin gown, and came
in an easy first.
A very natural, but hitherto unconsidered,
danger attends the Dudley bush nursing
scheme. Miss Mary Thompson was the first
nurse appointed. She was stationed at
Beech Forest. Her first patient was Syd-
ney C. E. Beeston, a railway construction
officer, who was thrown from a trolley. He
was also the keenest supporter of the bush
nursing scheme in the district. The wed-
ding has been fixed for September 5.
The Beech Forest nurses are accumulating
way-back experiences. One clever girl,
wffiose path of duty had hitherto been as-
plialted and carpeted, had to ride 10 miles on
a. rough horse to an operation. Says the
ministering one: “I couldn’t sit when I got
there, and the. horse couldn’t stand.” The
patient was discovered lying in a lean-to
which she had been helping to fix up when
she got ill. The unfortunate husband peered
m through the tiny unglazed window until
the doctor shooed him off.
Ballarat is almost ready financially for a
District Nurse. Money is coming in almost
without begging. Even the Tram Co. is
offering the nurse free rides, and when the
heart of a Tram Co. softens anything mav
happen. And the local cabby promises to
be as good as his rival. “Only put up your
hand, Miss,” is the cheerful invitation ex-
tended to the expected angel whose lack of
wings makes quick transport a necessity.
Father Tom Ewing, one of the best yarn-
spmners the House of Reps, ever knew, has
given a parental blessing to son Tom, who
is engaged to Leila McDougall, of Richmond
River.
B. Jim Bennie, son of Dr. Bennie, of
Collins-street, has won a promise from
Muriel Handffiy. The lady is the daughter of
Mrs. Hamilton, of Culverthorpe, Queens-
land, and of the late Captain Handley, of
Lincolnshire.
Melbourne gossipers are interested in the
news that Ida Minnett was lately married,
in ’Murka, to Mr. Levien, son of the
Sydney political identity. Ida, of yore,
affected a profoundly stage-struck mood,
and was always understood to be intending
herself for one or other “star” actor who
didn’t already bear the brand of matrimony.
Miss Nell Robinson, the interesting
daughter of Brighton importants, has an
appointment with T. A. Moyle, of the Mel-
bourne branch of the Guardian Assurance
Co., at St. Paul’s, at the ultra-fashionable
hour of 7 p.m., on August 15.
Ethel Leon, daughter of Crown Prosecu-
tor Leon, has just blossomed into a fully-
qualified chemist. This city is gradually
accumulating a strong contingent of profes-
sional and scientific women, and the ladies
stress the fact that they have to pass the
same examinations and do the same work
as the superior male, who, whether he wants
to or not, is forced to consider the vexed
question of whether the woman sex doesn’t
fully deserve all the rights it hasn’t got.
Mrs. Marie E. J. Pitt, who has recently
issued a book of verses, with a loud demo-
cratic ring in them, was given a dinner in
Melbourne last week. Several literary per-
sons sampled the feast, and, between attacks
on the edibles present, took it in turns to
applaud the energetic lady. Bernard O’Dowd
led the chorus of praise, and was supported
by C. R. Long, Miss Kenny and H. H.
Champion.
Someone should propose the waters of
Port Phillip Bay as an honorary but
hard-working member of the Child Study
Association. Some time ago, councillors
conspired to “improve” the foreshore
at St. Ivilda, by doing away with a
beach which had been the playground of
thousands of youngsters. The waters of the
bay waited for a suitable wind; when they
got it, they started to improve the place back
again. They scooped up sand, and packed
it into an excellent playing beach; they
made another sand-castle paradise along by
Middle Brighton where before there had
been nothing but rocks and stones; then,
just to “nark” the municipal meddlers, they
leapt at the parade which connects St. Ivilda
and Port Melbourne, chewed up some of the
beach the municipals wanted, put water over
the spot, and ate up a mile or more of
asphalt footpath.
Frank Bottrill, an Australian engineer,
lias made heavy traction engines walk over
loose sand. Whilst directing big contracts
for the Government near the dotted line
which divides us from South Australia, he
was faced with the problem of getting two
heavy engines across a patch that looked like
pepper. Thousands of strong men have used
stronger language and walked round that
problem without being able to solve it. Bot-
trill went away to a quiet spot and thought
deeply. Then he came back, and in some
ingenious way fitted flat boards to the great
wheels, so that, as they revolved, a firm flat
roadway of wood was always beneath them.
I don’t know whether it has anything to do
with the case, out the inventor is a strict
vegetarian. He lives on small portions of
crushed nuts, milk and cold water: never
takes stimulants of any kind; avoids tea
and coffee; and does without a campfire in
all but the very coldest weather. Withal he
is a mighty man of muscle and a Latter
Day Saint, into which sect he lately married.
A reception to meet Vida Goldstein was
held at one of the London clubs for women,
on June 9. t Andrew Fisher attended, and
made some remarks which did not interest
W. T. Stead or the British Empire League:
so they were not cabled. The dark-eyed
political lady is having a busy time in Lon-
don, and Melbourne people, playing truant
thereabouts, report that she has become a
social lioness.
Gerald Blackbourn, a young Melbourne
lawyer of promise, died suddenly the other
day at Euroa, where he had settled with his
young wife and child. He was a son of
civil engineer Blackbourn, who died re-
cently.
Harry Busteed, who used to motor-cycle
round the forgotten saucer track at hair-
rammg speed and wring shrieks of admira-
tion from terrified girls, has won his pilot’s
certificate at the Bristol aviation school.
He went to England last March in pursuit
of the lark at Heaven’s high gate. The bird
is still at large, but the certificate is safely
m hand.
The most recent English papers spread
themselves, carefully over the bowling green,
which is already a woman’s sphere and
threatens to become fashionable, for Queen
Mary isr a player. The greens at White
Lodge, Richmond, are her favorites. Any
w-oman who can curtsey should be able to
pa / sedate game. The graceful bend
and the swiiip* of the arm make even round
old gentlemen look almost stately. The im-
portant thing for a woman is to have a
neat foot and ankle. This virtue English
snapshotters deny Queen Mary, and some
disloyal female suggests that this fact may
be the cause of the short-skirt edict. The
treasonabJe statement goes uncorroborated.
_ ±he tehelfleld Choristers are so numerous
that, until they sailed into Melbourne, they
had never found one hotel big enough to hold
them all. However, the Grand swallowed
them, bag and baggage; and then the hall
porter went out on the step to look for
more. There promises to be a large crop of
orange blossoms as a result of the melo-
dious pilgrimage; several engagements have
been announced, and the baritones, sopranos
and tenors say there are likely to be others.
J-he basses and contraltos confirm the iov-
ful rumor.
h rom my Adelaide correspondent:—
J h | an n ua l football match between Prince Alfred
and bt. Peter s Colleges occurred on Saturday, and
the weather was grim. The chief side-show air these
affairs is provided by the barraekers in the boys’
stand, who, in the intervals of waving rival flags of
red or blue, and yelling lustily, fill in spare moments
with whole-hearted free-fights, in which the interfer-
ing policeman usually fares badly. Government
House was there, looking as though it came of
choice —not of necessity.
Borwick is here, and music-lovers (and those who
would fain be thought so) are in a seventh heaven.
He is reserved, both as regards himself and his play-
ing. Gushers are gently snubbed, and encores at the
C ?!vi r^s .5 re ® rm C' refused. As the programme is
written it stands, and no amount of stamping can
alter that rule. The Lyric Club donned its glad
rags to welcome him and h:s sister, and incidentally
supplied a musical programme which spoke well for
Adelaide amateurs. Miss Nora Kyffin Thomas is a
host m herself, and plays with rare charm and
delicacy.
Eocnen, the Arthur Waterhouses’ home on East
terrace, was festive on Wednesday evening, with the
music and cheerful people which go to make up a
good dance.
Medical men who, some years back, settled their
homes and their consulting rooms in the city are
repenting of the former part of the deed, so that many
one-time social centres are now mere “professional
apartments. The last to evict themselves are the
bwifts, who are forsaking Victoria Square for Br»-
ninr, in North Adelaide—a house for many years the
home of old Henry Salom.
The Literary Theatre continues on its successful
it? 7- ,, . late . st ve ntnre, Ashley Dukes’s “Civil
v\ ar, is not light fare, but the audience took it
smilingly, and interspersed attentive smiles with
sincere applause.
A comprehensive bill has been introduced into the
Assembly demanding that shop-assistants shall be
allowed to sit down in the intervals of measuring
lace and chiffon. Also, the Adelaide branch of the
Australian Natives’ Association called a special meet-
ing to consider the question of extending its privi-
leges to lady members. The motion was carried with
no dissentient voice, and several female-membership
applications were immediately lodged.
But woman is born, apparently, to be shackled
and, as her citizenship advances, her domestic w’
+w S grOW n ea Tl e v’ The elusive M ary Ann is wow
that any will-o -the-wisp, and housekeepers clutch at
any sttaw that may help them. The latest project is one
for importing orphans from India. The girls in
question are trained with a view to subsequent do-
mestic work and, as it is specially stipulated that
they must be of white parentage, some good mav
come of the scheme.
Among our recent brides we number an ancient
princess, the last of her race. She is known famil-
larly to southern tourists as “Jennie,” and is a
desolate-looking old lubra, well smitten in years and
L e ° en «y widowed. Her new husband is considerably
less than half her age, and is reported to have sown
his wild oats at the Point Macieay Mission Station
Adelaide is grieved at the illness of Miss Winifred
bcott, who for years has been connected with the
Reg ist er and its associated papers. Some of her
form Pie sketches have been published in booK
Douglas Thorn, of Mitcham, is to get the Roval
Humane Society s silver medal. Towards the end' of
May he literally dragged a girl from death under
the nose of a train at the Mitcham station ; his action
is regarded by railway men as being one of the most
recklessly daring rescues on record.

UNCONSCIOUS FROM GRIPING PAINS.
“For ten years I was a constant sufferer from
attacks of colic, and at times unconscious from the
griping pains,” says Mr. Alex. J. Cresswell, of Bir-
regurra, Vic. “The only thing that gave me relief
was Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea
Remedy. It never failed. I have recommended it
to many friends and customers, who have always
met with the same results as myself. My advice is
to always keep a bottle in the house. It is the most
effective relief and cure for severe griping pains in
the stomach and for Diarrhoea ”*
A Good Square Meal
What the dyspeptic subject
mostly longs for*
Treatment for Indigestion that pots
the stomach into condition to
digest ordinary food.
The Dyspeptic who wants a good square
meal, and knows that he will suffer if he
eats one, finds poor consoluation in picking
and choosing a diet. The only real cure tor
Indigestion is treatment that will enable
the stomach to digest, good solid lood a
steak and vegetables without trouble.
The tonic treatment for Indigestion is
based on the contention that Indigestion
occurs when the organs of the stomach
have become too weak to digest ordinary
food, and that only the restoration of the
stomach to a nroper state of tone will cure
the trouble. From their blood making and
general tonic qualities, the use of Dr. Wil-
liams' Pink Pills have been found of great
value in Indigestion. Amongst many suc-
cessful cures we might mention that ol
Mrs. Phillip Higginbottom, of 59 Lennox-
street, Richmond, Melbourne. Mrs. Hig-
ginbottom stated: —
“Several months back my health wim
very unsatisfactory. My appetite went oft,
and I would get Indigestion as soon as I
swallowed anything, so that at last I was
afraid to sit down to a meal. I would teel
so sick that I would turn at the sight of
food, even if it were just an egg. I would
get thumping bilious headaches that made
me think my temples were bursting, and
pressed down on my eyes the live long day,
till I could hardly see or hold my head up.
After a bit of food my mouth would fill
with a hot, stinging, bitter fluid, and my
mouth would have such a. nasty taste. My
tongue was always dried and thickly
coated. I lost flesh greatly, and my skin
turned as sallow as possible. I would get
such dizzy turns, and my head would teel
as heavy as lead. A mist would come be-
fore my eyes and black specks that made
every line that I might be reading waver
before me. I could not be bothered to
speak to anyone, I felt so wretchedly low
in spirits. I noticed my heart beating
furiously if I had any exertion, so that 1
hated to go out anywhere. I would have
to loosen my clothes every now and then
with the wind in my system. I would want
to sit down all day, I would feel so drowsy
and heavy, and too tired to do much work.
I can, however, speak most highly of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. My health has been
splendid since I took them. My appetite
is good, and all the heavy weighty feeling
lias gone. I heard of them through a friend
when I was living in Clifton Hill, and the
first few doses relieved me a little and en-
couraged me to keep on. Two boxes com-
pletely cured me.” .
Don’t let any dealer talk you into taking
something which he says is “just as good.
If you are pestered to take a substitute,
send 2/9 for one box, or 15/3 for six
boxes to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.
of Australasia, Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
C. G. Wade urged McGowen to go to the
Coronation; and, in McGowen’s absence, the
same Wade launched two no-confidence or
censure motions within a month. It is un-
derstood that C. G. stands for Confidence
Game.
Charles Gregory Wade’s thick hide lately
allowed him to introduce a deputation .to
Holman, asking for the prompt construction
of a tunnel or bridge to North Sydney—the
job that he talked about for years, and did not
do one thing to put in hand. Still, he felt a
spur on his own dunghill over the matter.
The Willoughby Council —formed of his sup-
porters —refused to have anything t<p do with
the deputation, and practically intimated
that it was like Wade’s cheek to show in the
matter at all, considering how his Govern-
ment had left it hanging in the air until just
before the elections, when the usual com-
mission was given a billet. Wade remarked
that he “was not aware of the particular
reason for the absence of the Willoughby
Council” on the deputation. But it was
quite an unnecessary explanation—Wade is
unaware of so many things.
Victoria finds, after studying the follow-
ing figures—
Sunshine claims paid .. ..£125,623
Richmond claims paid .. . * 126,/01
Total £252,324
that special arrangements will have to be
made to renew the Railway Accident I und.
The State couldn’t have afforded Heaven-
sent railway management for another five
years.
The N. S. Wales Cold Tea Organisation
has decided to get a Bill introduced by a
private member “to bring liquor bars under
the provisions of the law for the early
closing of shops.” Bung, faced with the
prospect of closing at 6 p.m., will, it is under-
stood, reply with an amendment that
churches and church bazaars shall also be
included.
The most brazenly impudent part of Wade’s
censure motion in the N.S.W. Assembly
deplored “the proposal under the Workers’
Dwellings Bill to deprive workers of the
right to acquire titles to land in connection
with their homes.” The Labor Government
no more deprives “workers of the right to
acquire titles to land in connection with
their homes” than the Wade Government
did. Wade didn’t even assist them to acquire
homes —he simply left them to be wolfed bv
the rentlord.
Minister Trefle (N.S.W.) opened out
again a few days ago, and said he had cer-
tain information as to the hold the bie Beef
Trust was getting on the Australian trade.
Of course he is going to do all sorts of
things to block the octopus. Unfortunately,
the big thing that might have been done was
neglected; in fact, when the Government to
which Treflfi belongs turned traitor to Aus-
tralia and the Australian Labor party, it
gave invaluable help to the Trust to get a
comfortable footing in this country. The
only hope that Australia has of fighting any
such Trust is with the Federal machinery,
and Ma’s Labor Government, in giv-
ing way to its petty State Spites feeling,
effectually clogged that machinery for the
time being. Against that big, potent, shame-
ful fact, all the talk about what the State
Government is going to do is mere piffle.

On a recent Friday in Sydney live shir-
masteis, captains of merchant steamships
with alien crews, were fined £7OO for
allowing prohibited immigrants to enter the
Commonwealth. The net result. is that the
Commonwealth received six Chinamen and
one Lascar, and the £7OO which went with
them is poor consolation. The downpour
raises the question whether the penalty for
allowing discolored aliens to abscond from
vessels is severe enough. The £lOO fine im-
posed on the master comes out oj. the ship s
profits —less the amount due to the absconder
in wages, or paid by him as a bonus and
the frequency of bolting Chow suggests that
the shipping cos. are not being unduly irri-
tated in their most vulnerable spot.
The Victorian Council of Progress Asso-
ciations may not go forward with the speed
of a “joy”-riding chauffeur, but still it moves
at times. The other day it resolved that it
is eminently desirable that in tuture sub-
divisions of residential land in the suburban
area the municipal councils should insist on
a minimum area of one-sixth of an acre.
The council might have gone a good deal
further without doing any harm. 11ns
country 7 can hardly be described as crowded
yet, and ir the population were to spread
itself out more it would look larger, even ir
it wasn’t. And it would certainly last
longer.
Sydney D.T. put Wade on the spit over
his censure motion, and blamed him for not
“allowing the Labor party 7 to fry in its own
fat a little longer.” But the worst of these
Labor schemes is that, the longer they try,
the better the public likes the dish. Every
good thing the “Liberals” have done in the
past year or two they cursed 20 years ago.
Wade throws bricks at the Labontes for
stopping the resumption of estates; yet only
10 years ago Wade’s crowd was denouncing
the resumption of estates as a monstrous
interference with private property. There
are invariably three stages in the business.
First, the “Liberals” say that the reform
will mean ruin, and they will fight it to the
last ditch. Then, when the reform has been
accomplished, they say: “Wait awhile, and
the disaster will turn up all right.” And
finally: “The scheme has been a great suc-
cess, and the country 7 ought to be grateful
to the ‘Liberal’ party which carried it
through in the face of bitter opposition.’
The latest freak effort of
Geelong (Vic.) is to borrow
£50,000 or £60,000 for a new
municipal hall, which the
aldermen and councillors de-
clare would be a “credit” to
the place. They recently
asked Watt, the Acting-
Premier, for help; but even
Willy wasn’t sympathetic.
What he should have done
was to submit the lot to the
indignity of making them
J’s.P. Any indignity is jus-
tified in the case of men who
are willing to go to Cohen
for £60,000 to build a hall for
their own aggrandisement,
after they have refused, on
account of their poverty, to
instal a municipal tramway
service. As it is, the Council
recently handed its roads
over to the usual syndicate
for 30 years, though there
isn’t one single bargain of
the sort made in Australia
that hasn’t produced
trouble.
From S.A. Treasurer Crawford Vaughan:
The Bulletin’s Wild Cat criticises the S.A. Gov-
ernment for treating the amount received from sale
of Crown lands as ordinary revenue, and concludes
that the sooner S.A. either knocks off selling land
altogether or uses the proceeds from the sale of one
lot of assets to buy another lot of assets,
the better for the Labor party’s reputation
as well as for the State’s record for honesty. Last
session the Government submitted an amendment of
the Taxation Act, providing that the amount re-
ceived from sale of Crown lands should be devoted to
the reduction of the public debt, instead of being
treated as revenue. That provision was thrown out
by the Leg. Council. In an amending Crown Lands
Bill we are seeking to provide for the substitution of
perpetual leasing for agreement or covenant to pur-
chase, and we are curious to know what its fate will
be in the Abattoirs Dept, of the Legislature.

Picture of a part of the Coronation panto-
mime :
. . . Aloft in the South Triforium, in a badly-
lighted space, filthy with the dust of centuries, sat the
journalists and others who were to give the story of
the Coronation to the world.
The press is supposed to represent the intelli-
gence of the people; and, appropriately,
while this pagan flummery was in progress,
the intelligence was thrown into a dust-box.
BREATHING-TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I hope that something else will fade into oblivion
—that is the particularly venomous spirit of a
section of Australian journalism which has made
characteristic efforts to stir up international hostility
between ourselves and the Japanese.— Acting-Premier
Holman, on the renewal of the Anglo-Jap Treaty.
Peace, perfect peace Come, .lay aside your gun.
The danger zone is past; the gauntlet run.
The bark of Scylla ceases on her shore,
And grim Charybdis threatens us no more.
Respite, Nepenthe, leaning-posts and beer!
Football and horses! Breathing time is here!
O witless fools, who, with your cry, “To Arms!”
Your warnings venomous, and false alarms,
Sought to estrange us from our yellow friends,
Thus all your potter and your bunkum ends!
We are secure once more; we breathe again.
No further need is there for ships or men.
“The Treaty is renewed!” Hip, Hip, Hooray! . . .
Now let us dream the happy hours away.
One pen-stroke! and our liberty appears
Secure again, for ten long, blissful years.
A diplomat or two, a little ink,
Some paper, and, Hi Presto ! in a wink,
The Yellow Peril vanishes from sight,
Like vague dream shadows of a restless night.
Let gentleness and peace o’erspread the land ;
And bid our infant warriors disband.
The War-god broods o’er Europe even yet?
What matter? We’ve a decade to forget
That e’er we dreamed we heard the grim dogs bark.
What child at noon is fearful of the dark?
The forges of the nations still are lit?
Their anvils ring? What do we reck of it?
With ten long years of peace and joy and light,
We laugh at our vague terrors of the night.
Are truces ever broken? Treaties scorned?
Statesmen corrupted ? Diplomats suborned ?
Perish the thought! What if, in some far day,
Some foresworn nation flung its bond away ?
Shall we, for such as that, forego our joy,
And start at shadows, like a frightened boy?
Shall croaking pessimists, with mild alarms,
Force us, all needlessly, to fly to arms?
Down with the dolts who prate of ships and guns!
Stern Mars shall not enslave Australia’s sons.
Come, gag the fools who urge us to defend
Our ports against our harmless yellow friend !
Their words are insults; their aggressiveness
May give him pain, and cause us much distress.
Ah, gaze on him! as he steps forth to sign—
Say, is his smile not peaceful and benign?
Ten years to hoard the gold in shop and mart;
Ten peaceful years to play the trader’s part;
To tend the sheep ; to watch the green corn sprout;
To cheer the race; to gaily clap and shout
At sports of children, played by heedless men.
Ten years of sweet Arcadia—and then? ... ,
Heed not the voice that thunders the alarm:
“Ten years to play the man! Ten years to arm !”
(O God of Battles, who, thus long, hath spared
A heedless nation, grant we be prepared!
Ten pregnant years! Ten scanty years of grace,
To make or mar the fortune of a race.
Grim years of strenuous and unceasing toil,
That all may not become a foeman’s spoil—
That it may not be told, some fateful day:
“Ten years they had; ten years they fooled away.”)
Peace, perfect peace. . . . Ho, let the fun begin,
And split the welkin with a joyous din!
Charybdis grim has ceased to roar and rave,
And Scylla sits demurely in her cave.
Ho! clash the cymbals, and begin the race!
And thank the gods we have a breathing-space.
VICTORIA.
C.J.D.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Arnst-Pearce match for the Cham-
pionship, and £5OO a side, takes place on this com-
ing Saturday, the 29th. At 4 p.m. sharp the scullers
will start from Ryde Bridge, and the finish will be
at Searl’s monument, the distance being 3 miles
330 yards. Arnst will be 28 in November; he will
scale 13st. 31b., and stands 6ft. iin. in his socks.
Pearce was 32 on Saturday last, and by the book
should weigh 12st. 101 b. on the day of the race. He
is exactly 6ft. long. If they row at these weights
there will only be 71b. difference in their tonnage;
but, looking at the two men, it does not seem possible
to get them so close without over-training the one
and under-training the other. The winner takes the
whole £lOOO, passes £5OO to his backers, pays his
expenses out of the other £5OO. and banks the
balance; the exes, will probably amount to £lOO per
man. Then there should be a few hundred pounds
from the steamers and the cinematograph pictures.
The winner stands to make £6OO or £7OO clear
profit; but the loser comes out rather badly.
* * * *
The fact that Pearce’s backers are now ask-
ing longer odds indicates that the curious and
unjustifiable confidence of the last few weeks is on
the wane. Of course, with almost a week to go,
Arnst may possibly go off, or maybe Pearce is not
displaying his prowess to full extent; but it is cer-
tain that, on the public work of the two men, Pearce
is not a match for Arnst. He does not get the
same weight on his sculls; neither is his sweep as
long. Moreover, Arnst is roughly a stone heavier
than Pearce, and he puts his weight to much more
use. So this writer’s expectations still go in Arnst’s
ark.
League Rugby in Sydney on Saturday
was of the strenuous type. Players had had a week’s
spell, and were neighing like young war-horses for
scrap. And naturally in those colliding masses of
valor and energy the earnest seeker could be sure of
finding scrap. At Wentworth Park, where Glebe
stamped North Sydney flat, the crowd rushed the
ground, and there was scrap galore, and fragments
of barrackers were plentiful. At the Agricultural
Ground the scrapping took place among the players.
While Messenger was on the broad of his back a
gentleman from Western Suburbs started perform-
ing on him the operation known in the vernacular
as “getting into his neck.” A rescuer arrived, and
then an anti-rescuer, and the mill became general.
The referee arrived, and Messenger and the gentle-
man who was getting into his neck, and the other
gent who was trying to prevent it, were ordered off
the ground altogether. Why he ordered Messenger
off, unless it was for medical attention, is hard to
understand. When the row became general, the
referee blew his whistle and ordered both teams off
the ground. Then gentlemen rolled up their sleeves
and ladies drew hatpins, but 14 “Johns” appeared
from nowhere and assumed the offensive, and the
crowd that was waiting to deal with the referee was
shooed off the ground.
* * * *
Union Rugby is now split into two divi-
sions, and University (18 points), Newtown (16),
Western Suburbs and Manly (13 each) compete for
the premiership. The other six—Glebe, Eastern
Suburbs, Balmain, South Sydney, North Sydney, and
St. George—are out of the hunt. University caught
Western Suburbs on the ’Varsity Oval, and scrunched
it by 18 points to 3. Newtown crossed over to
Manly, and fell on the Villagers in great style.
Manly couldn’t respond worth tuppence, and literally
went to pieces just before half-time, so Newtown re-
turned home with 13 points to nil. In the second
division, South Sydney downed Balmain by 12 to 8 ;
St. George killed the Eastern Suburbs dragon by 28
to 6, and Glebe kicked up 22 points against a miser-
able three by North Sydney.
* * * *
A curious feature of Sydney football on
Saturday was that the League and Union teams of
Glebe beat the League and Union teams of North
Sydney by almost identical scores. In the League
tussle'Glebe won by 24 points to six; in the Union,
by 22 to three.
In neither form of Rugby is Balmain
doing very well. In fact, in the League competition
it is bottom of the list. But that does not mean
that Balmain can’t play football. It means that
they play Soccer there. On Saturday a Balmain
team smashed up Newtown, and won the Associa-
tion Cup (there is one match to go with Garden
Island, but its result can’t affect the destination of
the utensil), while another team was stamping New-
town flat in the struggle for the Association Cup,
and a third was pounding the Navy to pieces in the
fight for the Rawson trophy. It won easily with 5
goals to 1, and leads in the competition for the Raw-
son Cup by two points.
* * * *
By downing Annandale (eight to nil),
South Sydney keeps top of the Rugby League com-
petition with 16 points. Glebe managed to turn the
tables on North Sydney, dishing the Hillmen by 24
to 6 ; and so it is second on the list with 14 points.
Newtown, which ran over Balmain (16 to 7), is now
only third, with 12 points. Eastern Suburbs, through
scoring only a draw with Western Suburbs, is fourth
with 11 points. Below these are only single figures.
None of Saturday’s play was startling. Glebe started
from the jump to chop North Sydney to pieces, and
did it. Perhaps the best match of the day was the
stubborn stand made by the plucky Annandale team
against South Sydney. It was not till the last 10
minutes that the game was lost, and then more by
luck than by play. The struggle between Eastern
and Western Suburbs was somewhat humorous. Easts
were there to score a walk-over victory, and found
something like a conquering team; and but for the
riot bringing the game to an early end, the chances
are Wests would have won. The Eastern Suburbs
crowd had quite lost its temper, and was rapidly
losing the game.
* » » *
Those Sydney people who have the hal-
lucination that there is nothing in the Australian
football game should have been at Erskineville on
Saturday, when the Ivalgoorlie (W.A.) Railway team
met a Sydney XVIII. Although conditions were all
against them, the vis’tors gave a fine di«plav of the
scientific side of the game. Perfectly disciplined, cool
as frozen cucumbers, fast as buttered lightning, the
Westralians showed a combination that has rarely
been equalled in Sydney. It was human chess —
and nothing less. Against that beautiful order and
splendid oomb'nntion the Sydney XVIIT. was helpless.
Still, Kalgoorlie was merciful. It might have won
by what it liked, but apparently saw nothing in
rubbing it in. It was here to educate, and was con-
tent to walk through with 89 points to 66.
* * * *
Saturday’s Melbourne ’oofball didn’t
settle the momentous question whether ’Roy or ’Wood
would miss being in the “final four.” The Magpies
collided with South. Victory to either side meant
a lot; defeat meant much or little, according to
circumstances. The premiers went off with the lead,
and at half-time had collared a three-goal advantage.
But the tremendous effort had told, and by lemon-
time Skinner’s hirelings had reduced the difference
to 12 points. The last term saw the Southerners
slowly overhauling the ’Woodsmen. The tired de-
fenders kept the attackers off, with five points still
in hand, until, as the time-keeper stretched out his
hand to the bell, an unguarded Southerner marked the
ball right in front, and swiftly booted it through,
and the game went to the Red and Whites —37 points
to ’Wood’s 36. It was a considerable feat for the
winners; but, when the two meet again, as they are
pretty sure to do, the writer’s cash will go on the
Magpies. Less eagerness and more assurance would
have gained them the day. Down at Geelong ’Roy
was fighting for its life. It has a tougher pro-
gramme ahead than ’Wood, and victory meant every-
thing. But the Seasiders played a game that para-
lysed ’Rov. There was little to choose between them
at lemon-time, but the terrible last quarter—the one
that tests the untrained, the half-hearted and the
novices—saw Geelong carrying all before it, piling
up 27 points to ’Roy’s 9, and winning by 74 to 39.
Of the others, Carlton walloped Richmond by 76 to
41 : Essendon smashed ’Varsity by 80 to 42, and
Melbourne pulverised St. Kilda by 77 to 39. But
these were merely exhibition games, and in no way
affect the premiership.
The most considerable episode of the day occurred
out on the bleak Footscray ground, where the locals
beat the hitherto unbeaten Essendon (Ass.) team by
5 goals 10 behinds to 5 goals 5 behinds. It
was a narrow victory, but it gave tremendous joy
to ’Scray, and left ’Don disgruntled. The latter was
still ahead of all competitors, with a mortgage on
the premiership, but its chance of accomplishing the
rare feat of going through the season unbeaten was
gone—and all for the lack of one measly goal. On
the adjacent ground, at Williamstown, Brunswick
lost much of its grip of “final” chances, by totting
up the same old five goals, but neglecting to acquire
as many behinds as ’Town —45 to 39. A goal here
would also have meant much. Port made a game
effort to keep its end up against P’ran, but failed
by eight points—4B to P’ran’s 56. But as Bruns-
wick also met disaster, there is still corn in Egypt
for the Portonians, though it begins to look rather
shrivelled. North castigated Northcote by 73 to 27,
and Brighton and Preston indulged in a disturbance
that mattered to nobody but themselves.
* * * *
The news that “Dally” Messenger would
not go with the “Kangaroos” is a heavy smack at the
drawing power of the League team. As a gate asset Mes-
senger is unrivalled; and the news of his coming had
made the Englishmen look forward to a mighty treat.
Last time he was in the Cold Country the stolid
crowds were fascinated by the comet from Eastern
Suburbs. Now a rather charming young widow has
put two dainty feet through the whole arrangement,
smashed half the hopes of the Kangaroo team, cut off
vast supplies of coin from its pouch, and disap-
pointed England. Truly lovely woman can make a
wreck of things.
* * * *
Adelaide’s big annual college football con-
flict —-Princes v. Saints —sent the latter home with
their halos awry, the scores being 57 against 33.
Princes have won 19 of the last 29 matches. In
1883 they won by 81 points to nil, and, if a record
was kept previous to that year, the Saints hope the
moths have eaten it.
* * * *
Maoriland ’oofball has this season already
killed two players and injured a number of others.
All branches of the sport of “muddied oafs” have
contributed to the accident list. And there have
been other casualties. Two of the Otago team which
lost the British Association match with Christchurch
for the Brown Shield have been passed out for bad
conduct; and in Auckland a whole team has been
suspended for rough play and rougher language.
* * * *
Hugbie Mehegan, light-weight champion
of the Six States, was never exposed to any particular
danger in the course of Mark Higgins’s attack at the
Melbourne Athletic Club’s punch-bowl. Higgins
coveted Mehegan’s eminence and the butt-end of a
corpulent purse, and tried hard for both; but the
harder he tried the more apparent it became that he
was not the man for the job. One may safely affirm
that Hughie knows more than Mark can ever learn,
in addition to which he is the better machine. In
the earlier rounds the champion rammed his op-
ponent about the body, and extinguished his fire in
three rounds. Higgins was down in the third, re-
flecting. He made a bit of a flutter in No. 4, and
succeeded in landing one fairly effective cross in the
vicinity of Mehegan’s chin in the fifth, which latter
so disgusted Hughie that he woodened Mark with the
right. Mark sat it out, only reaching perpendicular
just in time. The fight was already won, but Higgins
remained in action till the eighth, when his corner
lowered its flag on humanitarian principles.
* * * *
When the Mclnstoush arranged the fight
between Jack Johnson and Bombardier Wells he
offered the champion £6OOO, win, lose or draw. It
was a big price to offer for such a fight, but pre-
sumably the Mac. knows what he is about. Johnson,
however, thinks he does not, and has demanded £3OOO
of the money “right now,” and the balance before
entering the ring. It would have been a treat to
hear the McTnstoush in reply to this impudent de-
mand from the gentleman who so flagrantly broke
faith with the Sporting Club over his fight with
Langford ; but no cable or mail would carry the in-
candescent words. The chances are that Johnson is
still feeling scorched where they struck him. If
Johnson sticks out the chances are that there will be
no fight with Bombardier Wells, and his pugilistic
career, as far as England is concerned, will end sud-
denlv. The vagaries of Johnson are becoming a
little wearisome.
That Mclntosh is sending Sammy Lang-
ford and Jim Flynn to Sydney to fight Lang, Lester,
Squires and Peter Felix is about the most astound-
ing fistic news that has struck Sydney for a while.
Lang might make a decent fight, and Lester is quite
an unknown quantity—but Squires and Peter Felix!
Peter Felix and Squires! What about old Dan
Oreedon, Peter McColl and Terrible Tiny ?
* * * *
“Akrakadabra”: I see J. Johnson re-
ferred to as “the only black gladiator” left, now that
Langford has been knocked out. The man who wrote
that word had better be careful. When Ike Stewart
and Starlight met for the second time, and as they
sat in their corners, the referee announced the
“second meeting of these two great black gladia-
tors.” Starlight sprang up and addressed the crowd.
He had fought many times and in many places —had
appeared at the National Sporting Club, London —
and had never before been insulted by a referee
calling him a black alligator, and he was going to
see about it afterwards.
* * * *
H. W. Stevenson arrived in Melbourne
last week, and is just now playing a 12,000-up with
Reece at Alcock’s Room. Reece, with 2000 start,
was as good as his master at the outset of the game,
but the “champion” obviously wanted time to warm
up after his travels. When the argument with
Reece is finished, Stevenson will take on Lindrum,
giving him a start, as a matter of championship
etiquette. Both will be limited to 25 consecutive
hazards off the red.
* * * *
The tournament of Victorian amateur
billiardists ended last week in the triumph of U. Von
der Luft, who beat C. M. Cock (the best stylist of
them all) by 1000 to 823 in the final. Arthur Tricks,
the established champion of previous seasons, refused
to defend his title this time, on the ground that the
V.A.B.A. rule of nowadays puts the old champion at
a disadvantage. Both Von der Luft and Cock have
been several times beaten in tournaments, where
Tricks was either the winner or runner-up, so it
would seem that amateur billiard form in Mel-
bourne has lost weight by the retirement of the old
hand. The best breaks that the winner made in this
year’s final were 93, 76 and 57, against Cock’s 109,
55 and 43, neither man showing much strength at
the “red loser” game which George Gray has so
mightily developed.
* * * *
Saturday was a great day among the
baseballers in Sydney, for there were two parties of
invaders wearing meat-covers over their faces, fry-
ing-pans on their hands, and liver-pads over their
vitals. The visitors represent Tasmania and Vic-
toria, and the campaign is to extend over three
days. The Tassies were novices at the game; from
start to finish they had no show against the well-
trained, easy-playing Cornstalks, and were disposed
of by 30 to nil. The Victorians were a tougher
problem ; but again the Ma Staters proved too good,
and won with a margin of 10 runs. The 2000 who
looked on at the glorified rounders followed the
details with as much interest as if they were a
Shakspearian society watching Irving’s first per-
formance. Evidently, it is a game to be studied.
* * * *
W. G. Ingleton, who captained Victorian
baseballers on Saturday, has the tall record of having
played in 46 inter-State matches, and also had a
seat in the Australian team which worried Uncle
Sam’s players a few years ago.
* * * •*
N.S.W. Cricket Association is awakening
out of winter sleep in preparation for a heavy sea-
son. The coming of the English XL will disorganise
premiership matches considerably; and an early
start will be made to get headway on before the
invasion takes place. Thanks to the Springboks’ ramble
last year, the Association has £1873 in the cotters to
begin the season —out of which £5OO will be do-
nated to clubs to put a level surface on the grounds
after their rooting by the muddied oafs.
* » * *
Dorliam Doolette, the father of the awful
Bullfinch fizzle, has had still worse luck with his
start on the turf. The first two jockeys he engaged
in W.A. were immediately wiped out by the racing
clubs.
* * * *
Though the Auckland Club (M.L.) loses
two days through the “adjustments” made by the
Government Commission, it will give away in the 11
remaining days £28,915 in stakes, or only £9OO
less than last year. The racing clubs’ howl over the
general reduction in the number of race-days is still
in good working order.
* * * *
The professional athletic atmosphere is
choking with challenge and bluff. Yankee Ilolway,
who gathered in the recent three-cornered sprint, has
got the better of his desire to rush home to Uncle
Sam, and has fixed up matches with Trembath
(400yds.) and Donaldson (100yds., 130yds. and
150yds.) ; and Postle and the other runners are pro-
mised consideration later on.
* * * *
Although the winter is well advanced,
there does not seem much chance of a decent track
being laid down on the Victorian Amateur Sports’
Ground (late Friendly Societies’ Gardens) in time
for the track season. The trustees are catering for
amateur wheelers and motorists, but, so far, the
peds. are out of the swim. Yet a cinder track is
badly needed. “Dad” Wheatley, who finished second
in the half-mile at the London Festival of Empire
Games, writes out, saying that he has been greatly
handicapped, owing to want of practice on the cinder
path; and it seems unfair to send representatives to
the Olympic Games and other big contests without
affording them the necessary facilities to compete
with the world’s best under favorable conditions.
* * * *
R. T. O. Rennick put up a slashing per-
formance when he won the Dave Scott Memorial Race
(130vds.) at the Queensland A.A.A. championship
meeting the other day. The going was very heavy—
about an inch and a-half of rain had fallen in the
previous 24 hours —but Rennick, starting from
scratch, got over the distance, which was subse-
quently measured, and found to be half a yard short
of 130yds., in 13sec.
* * * *
The battle for the Sydney Lacrosse pre-
miership is very strenuous this year. Last week’s
dust-up left Petersham on top with 20 points, Stan-
more 19, Sydney 18, Mosman 15, and the balance
very drawn-out. The visit of teams from Queens-
land and Victoria, next month, and the rush for
seats in the two N.S.W. teams, is responsible for
much of the new vim that marks the game.
* * *
Australian champion golfess, Miss Par-
bury, added the N.S.W. State championship to her
jersey, for the second time, last week, after a tourna-
ment in which the champion outshone all others.
She defeated Mrs. Lethbridge in the final by 6 up and
4 to play—which is almost equal to annihilation. A
sorry part about the championship was the notice:
“Players are advised to bring their caddies,” and,
though it didn’t debar the small boy from parading
the links, it left the matter open for each lady to
choose her own most-favored size of “boy.”
* * * *
The S. Aus. Waterloo Cup—the State’s
big coursing event —had an odd finish this year. It
was taken down into the Strathalbvn district, where
hares seem to be more plentiful than at Buckland
Park or Hill River, the old coursing places.
There are a few prickly-pear hedges about —so far
south the pear does not get out of hand and become
a pest—and, in the final. Chorus picked up a thorn,
and stopped ; yet she had scored so many points that
Ring On, chasing the hare alone, could not wipe
them out. Chorus comes from Nhill, and is the first
Victorian to win the S.A. Waterloo.
* * * *
Terrible news from England last week to
the effect that Anthony Wilding does not think he
will be able to get back in time to play for Aus-
tralasia in defence of the Davis Cup! Desperate
endeavors are to be made to secure his services, and
the lawn tennis people hope that the fact that
Christchurch, his birthplace, is to be the battle-
ground, may prove the last straw to break the back
of his unwillingness; but the chances are there is
no unwillingness about it —it is probably an
affair of business. It will be an irreparable loss if
he cannot come. That is the trouble with Aus-
tralasian tennis —we have no young giants in view
waiting to take the places of those who are growing
old.
* * * *
The Cyclists’ Union ran off two heats of
the Dunlop Team Premiership on Saturday. The
first was between Eastern and Western Suburbs. It
was an easy victory for the Western team, which
lost onlv 14 points in the 20 miles run, while
Eastern 'Suburbs lost 22. The victors ran through
in 54min. ssec., which, considering the wind, was
excellent going. The second heat was between the
“B. and B.” and Hurstville clubs, and the result
was exactly the same as between the first two.
“B. and B*” won, with a loss of 14 points to the
Hurstville’s 22; but the winning team took a trifle
longer over the journey—s7tnin. 59sec. The third
and fourth heats will be run over the same course
(Homebush) next Saturday. The team that wins
the competition carries off the Dunlop Shield, which
is the outward and visible sign of the road cham-
pionship of N. S. Wales. Two shields have so far
been won outright—by Balmain in 1904 and Enfield
in 1906. The third is now being contested for.
Western Suburbs has got temporary possession of it
twice, in 1907 and 1909. Last year it was carried
off by Marrickville. Western Suburbs has great
hopes of annexing it permanently this time.
The long arm of coincidence again. Twelve
years ago the Dunlop Rubber 00. organised an Aus-
tralasian 100 miles inter-club road relay race. Each
club supplied 10 riders, and each rider had to cover
a 10-mile section with a despatch. After a very
close contest the trophy was won by the Christchurch
Cycling Club (M.L.), in 4hr. 29min. 80sec. In May
last a similar tussle was held in the United States
and the winning team took the same time—even to
the seconds. . .
* * * *
The cable says that George Westing-
house, of brake fame, has perfected an “air-spring
wheel” which will do away with pneumatic tyres.
The fly in the ointment is that Westinghouse, like
Edison, is always announcing wonderful discoveries
that come to nothing. Little more than a year ago
Mr. Westing-house informed the world that he had
invented an air spring which would do away with
pneumatic tyres. The air spring consisted of a series
of brass tubes which worked into each other like a
cylinder and a piston ; one set of four tubes was to
be fixed to the frame, while those that worked into
them were fastened to the axles i so the whole weight
of the chassis rested on four columns of compressed
air The invention was said to have proved a success
in a series of tests, but it was never put on the
market. It is quite possible that the recent cable
referred to the old air spring, for what an air-spring
wheel” can be Heaven only knows.

# * *
Motorists are painting their tyres white at
brief intervals, and it makes for smartness. But
smartness is not the object; it is the. by-produ -
The real object is to prevent the sunlight striking
on the vulcanised rubber. Direct sunlight is more
injurious to vulcanised rubber than tin tacks, and
in Australia tyres get much sunlight. The pamt
becomes a protecting case, and by excludingthe
light prolongs the life of the tyre. But beware.
Oh, my brother! Use only the correct kind oi
paint—a special preparation in which there is no
oil. Oil is as bad as light, if not worse.
A 40-year-old advt. from a pious Scawtch
newspaper. It was the tourist season among the bon-
nie heather, and the ad. referred to a four-horse
coach and a Hieland roadside inn: The Duchess of
Montrose leaves the Duke’s Arms at 7.15 every morn-
ing except Sunday.” The hard sanctity of the
Scotchbyterian Sawbath was never more tenderly ex-
pressed than in that little ad.
Captain Grant, of the Bth Gurkhas, who
has been appointed to help Maoriland get
together a better defence than a Dread-
nought stationed alongside China, won a
V C." in 1905. Up Thibet way they still
curse in 10 dialects when they recollect how
Grant led his crowd up a hill against a
storm of bullets, rocks, whirling knives and
scandalous language, leaped with one Ghurka
into the native fort, and Jack Johnsoned
everybody in the place.
WELL MEANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One Moriarty, a ’Hoy delegate, at a recent meet-
ing of the Vic. Pootbrawl League, cried aloud against
police interference at matches. “A lan likes a
punch or two to see if he can stand it,” said Mor-
iarty. “A knock-down now and then doesn’t matter,
so long as it is well meant.”
The fightball warrior who smites,
And darkens his opponent’s lights,
A generous respect invites.
He does not stoush the other gent
With malice, or with foul intent—
His biff’s invariably well meant.
The lad who takes the jabs and chips,
May have an ear that hangs in strips,
And wear his teeth outside his lips,
But he is grateful, and will give
His hand, for now he’s positive
What punching he can take, and live.
The player who elects to croak,
He’s just a poor, unlucky bloke
Who didn’t live to see the joke.
Upon his monument indent
This simple football sentiment,
In letters large: “It was well meant!”
VICTORIA.

J. D. WILLIAMS’ ANSWER
TO A THOUSAND LIES, AND WITH HEARTFELT THANKS AND APPRE=
CIATION FOR THE GREAT AND LOYAL SUPPORT OF NUMEROUS
FRIENDS, PATRONS AND THE PUBLIC IN GENERAL OF AUSTRALIA.
MY advent in Australia was to pronnote various amusement enterprises, but under Modern Enterprise conditions, which
meant
PLACING AUSTRALIA ON A PAR WITH NEW YORK AND LONDON.
My first endeavor was the Colonial Theatre No. I, where I originated and introduced to the Sydney Public
CONTINUOUS PHOTO PERFORMANCES.
The preliminary announcements in reference to this enterprise Were greeted with ridicule and prognostications of dismal failure.
The theatre, the style of performance, and everything Connected with same were maligned right and left. My Company were called
foreigners and butters-in. The theatre was termed “a penny peep show/' and generally referred to by certain persons in con-
temptible terms. Nothing daunted, I had seen this battle of Modem Enterprise against Monopoly fought out in other countries.
1 sawed wood and advertised, but in my advertisements 1 told the truth. 1 raised capital and equipped my theatre with up-to-
date and modern upholstered seats. 1 arranged for ladies’ rest rooms with lady attendants, and also gentlemen’s lavatories. 1
had representatives abroad, who were told to buy, without fear or favor, the best photo play productions the world’s most famous
makers produced. Through the press of Australia I told what 1 was doing, and what 1 proposed to do for the public. I kept
my word. The public responded—a few hundred came at first out of curiosity. They were agreeably surprised. What 1 claimed
for my theatres they found to be true. They spread the news, and business increased beyond all comprehension.
MODERN ENTERPRISE HAD CONQUERED.
1 increased the capital of my Companies. 1 built other theatres —each one an improvement on its predecessor. My last venture,
the Melba Theatre in Melbourne, is undoubtedly the finest Moving Picture Theatre in the world, and 1 can truthfully state that
the receipts of this Photo Play Theatre are more than any other photo-play theatre in the world. During all this time 1 met
opposition on every side—some secret, some open. The open and legitimate opposition 1 make no comments about. This is the
spice of trade. It puts every man on his mettle, and also goes to benefit the public. The secret and underhand opposition was
a bird of another feather. It has been to the imaginary advantage of certain persons to spread various rumors concerning my
Companies and myself. One rumor lately circulated is to the effect that lam severing my connection with the Greater J. D.
Williams Amusement Co., Ltd., and leaving for America for good. This report was spread about by some person or persons
unknown. This I wish to refute absolutely, and to state once and for all that I have no such intentions. My enterprises are
too big a success for any man in his sane senses to even dream of severing from. I am leaving for America on the 31st instant,
AND AM RETURNING ABOUT DECEMBER.
Each and every one of my theatres are now fitted and furnished in the most up-to-date and modern style, and I am going to
America, England and the Continent to get even better improvements, and to bring back new ideas in the amusement line. My
Companies will build new theatres in every city of importance in Australasia, and in each and every instance the prices of admis-
sion to any enterprise promoted or wherein I am interested will be the same as charged in America and England. Modern
Enterprise Prices of admission were laughed at when 1 first introduced same in a first-class theatre in Australia, but I showed
and proved to the public that an up-to-date, clean, amusing and educating photo-play performance could be given at these
theatres, and also that these prices would show the promoters a good profit. When I state that the theatres under my direc-
tion in Australia are now showing to over 80,000 patrons weekly, I am telling the truth without exaggeration. I stand prepared
to personally donate to any public charity £lOO if this statement cannot be verified by the books of the said Company. So
when I state that
WE HAVE CONQUERED THE ENEMY,
and that they are ours, lam making no idle boast. 1 bear malice toward no man, woman or child, or opposition concern.
May the best man win. Competition is the life of trade, for Modern Enterprise was, is, and always will be for the benefit of
the public, and my trade mark was, is, and always will be
“ADVANCE MODERN ENTERPRISE. NOTHING TOO GOOD FOR
AUSTRALIA.”
Believe me, sincerely and appreciatingly,
A servant of the Public, •
J. D. WILLIAMS.
P.S.—ln appreciation of the success attending my enterprises in Australia, it is my intention to personally spend £2OOO
while In America and England advertising this great, grand and glorious country, and 1 can furthermore assure the public that
Australia is good enough for me. The money that I have made, and am making, in this country is being re-invested in
Australia. The Companies which I have promoted now give employment to over 1000 persons of t both sexes, and the staff will
certainly be doubled in less than 18 months.
Furthermore, the Greater J, D. Williams Amusement Co., Ltd., and other Companies which 1 promoted are owned by
Australians and Australian capital;

ONE HONORED AND TWENTY-SIXTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT
OP
The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney
LIMITED.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting held at the Head Banking House, George-
street, Sydney, on Friday, July 21, 1911.
«
The Directors subnlit to the Shareholders a Balance Sheet showing the Liabilities and Assets of the
Bank on June 30, 1911, and present the following report:—
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on current bills and
accrued interest on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying State note tax, and providing for
Australian Commonwealth land tax and State Government land and income taxes and bad and doubtful
debts, as well as granting to the Officers a special bonus on their salaries, amounts to . . £155,659 19 0
out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations:—

This Meeting is called upon to elect a Director, consequent on the retirement, in terms of the Articles
of Association, of the Honorable H. E. Kater, M.L.C., who is eligible for re-election, and has given the
requisite notice that he is a candidate for the office. It also rests with the meeting to appoint Auditors
for the ensuing year.
During the half-year Branches have been established at Aberdeen, Boree Creek, Humula, Manildra, and
Tumbarumba, in this State.
The Dividend, free of Income Tax, is now payable.
GEORGE J. COHEN, Chairman.

Sydney, July 12, 1911.
We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances and counted the coin in the
Head Office of The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, Limited, and have compared the returns of
the Branches, and have found the same as specified in the foregoing Balance Sheet.
E. S. CAPE,
F. W. HIXSON,
Auditors.
The following resolutions were carried unanimously:—
“That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated
among the Proprietors.”
“That the Honorable H. E. Kater, M.L.C., be re-elected a Director of the Bank.”
“That F. W. Hixson, Esq., and W. L. Docker, Esq., be appointed Auditors for the ensuing year.”
“That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and Officers of
the Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the institution.”
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chairman, Mr. G. J. Cohen, said :
I have pleasure in stating that the business of the Bank still shows satisfactory progress, and the
Balance Sheet presented to you to-day is a strong one.
In making the usual comparison with the items in the Balance Sheet of twelve months ago, it is seen
that the total figures have increased by £1,826,000.
On the debit side there is an addition of £BO,OOO to the Reserve Funds, which now exceed £1,500,000.
Bills in circulation are larger by £229,000, and the Deposit and other Liabilities have increased by
£1,769,000.
On the credit side there is an addition of £358,000 to the Coin and Bullion. The new item, Austra-
lian Commonwealth Notes, accounts for £405,000. Additional investments in Government securities repre-
sent £361,000
Bills Receivable in London, etc., and Notes and Bills of other Banks are larger by £160,000
Bills Discounted and all debts due to the Bank have increased by £1,131,000
The total of the Liquid Assets, £11,539,000, is £668,000 higher, and represents about 54 per cent,
of our Total Liabilities to the public.
The Board has granted a Special Bonus to the Staff, which they consider is well deserved.
The expanding business of the Bank has necessitated increased expenditure for buildings and sites, but
the Capital Cost is being steadily written down.
On the Ist instant we ceased to issue our own notes, which have been in circulation for nearly 77 years,
and you will notice we have been redeeming them, the total having diminished £314,000 since December
last, and we anticipate a further reduction of at least £250,000 before the end of this year.
The adoption of a Government Note in place of a Bank Note circulation is necessitating on the part of
th : s Bank the retirement of its own notes, of which there were in the hands of the public on December 31
last . .. . . .. .. .. . . .. . . . . . . £631,000
Payment to the Commonwealth for their notes to replace our own, say .. . .
Payment for Commonwealth Notes for till money, say .. •• •• £500,000
A total demand on our coin holding of .. •• ..£1,762,000
Against this we shall hold the £500,000 in our tills, and we shall be relieved of the liability in respect
of our own notes.
The charge has not, however, so far greatly affected our holding of Gold. The Commonwealth Govern-
ment having lent to various States a large proportion of the Funds derived from the sale of Australian
Notes, the Gold automatically flows back to the coffers of the Banks to the extent of the local State
Government expenditure.
It may interest the Shareholders to know that for the five years to December 31 last, the mutilated
notes of this Bank destroyed have averaged £241,000 per annum. Our Circulation has been about one-
seventh of that of all the Banks of the Commonwealth, so that an annual provision ot note forms of the face
value approximately of £1,689,000 is required to replace the mutilated Notes of the Commonwealth which
will have to be destroyed. . . , .
T now move that the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and circulated among
the Proprietors. . .. ■ .

HOW A HYPNOTIST
MADE A FORTUNE.
The Secret Methods by which Dr. X. La Motte Sage,
the Greatest Hypnotic Scientist of the Age,
Created a Tremendous Sensation.
He Firmly Believes Hypnotism a Public Benefit,
Has Donated £5OOO Toward the Free Distribu-
tion of a Handsomely Illustrated Book con-
taining His Views and Suggestions on
How to Acquire this Mysterious
Power and Use it in Business, in
Society, and in the Home.
While the Special Edition Lasts a Copy of This
Remarkable Book Will be Sent Free to
Any Interested Person.
Dr. X. La Motte Sage made a fortune
out of Hypnotism. He probably knows
more about the subject than any living
man. His methods are radically different
from any ever before presented. By his
new system he hypnotises people instan-
taneously. He tells you how to exert
tremendous silent influence without making
a gesture or saying a single word. He gives
the only real, practical methods for the
development of the power of Personal
Magnetism that have ever been published.
During all the time that Dr. Sage was be-
fore the public he made it his business to
note carefully the effect of hypnotism upon
the human mind. He became convinced
that this mighty mysterious power could be
utilised to the advantage of ambitious men
and women who wished to better their con-
dition in life. To demonstrate the correct-
ness of his ideas, when he retired from pub-
lic life he founded a college where Hypno-
tism, Personal Magnetism, Magnetic Heal-
ing, etc., might be taught along the de-
finite lines he had laid down. The result is
that the college has grown to be the largest
of its kind in the world. Thousands of suc-
cessful students in all parts of the globe
are living witnesses to the wonderful power
and the great practical benefits to be derived
from Dr. Sage’s methods. The Doctor has
recently written a book entitled “The Phil-
osophy of Personal Influence,” in which he
tells in plain, simple language just how to
acquire hypnotic power and the various
uses to which it may be put. Among the
many interesting things upon which the
book treats are: How to develop magnetic
power and influence people without their
knowledge; how to cure bad habits and ob-
stinate chronic diseases when medicine and
everything else in this line fails; how to im-
plant a command in a subject’s mind that
he will carry out in every detail a month or
a year hence, whether the hypnotist is pre-
sent or not; how to hypnotise people at a
distance; its value in business; marvellous
scientific tests how to prevent people from
influencing you; hypnotic power more fas-
cinating than beauty; the use of hypnotism
in the development of the mental faculties;
controlling children, removing domestic-
troubles, etc.
The college which Dr. Sage has founded
proposes to give away £5OOO worth of the
above books absolutely free so long as the
special edition lasts. Any person who is in
earnest can get a copy merely by writing
for it. This book is handsomely illustrated
by the finest hair-tone engravings. It tells
you how the marvellous power of hypno-
tism has been used to cast a secret mystic
spell over people without their knowledge,
and how they have been for months, and in
some cases even years, obeying the royal
will of another. It gives you the secret of
the development of what Senator Chauncey
M. Depew calls the money-making microbe.
Don’t think because you lack a fine educa-
tion and are working for a small salary that
you cannot better your condition; do not
think because you are now successful in
life that you cannot be more successful. Dr.
Sage’s book has been read, and his methods
are to-day being used by many of the richest
men in the world. They know the value of
personal influence, or hypnotic power. If you
are interested, address a Id. postcard to-
day to the New York Institute of Science,
Dept. 22M., Rochester, New York, U.S.A.,
and a copy of Dr. Sage’s book will be sent
you by return mail absolutely free. This
is a rare opportunity to learn the uses and
possibilities of the most wonderful, marvel-
lous and mysterious power known to man.
The book is enthusiastically endorsed by the
most prominent business men, ministers of
the Gospel, lawyers and doctors. Tt should
be in every home; it should be read by all
men and women in this country who want
to better their condition in life, who want
to achieve greater financial success, win
friends, gratify their ambitions, and get out
of life the pleasure and happiness which
the Creator intended they should enjoy.
