824
The Inky Way.
Westralian Worker, hitherto a goldfields weekly,
has at last definitely arranged to turn itself into a
daily; and it hopes to be ready for battle during the
coming W.A. election campaign. The co. is not over-
burdened with money, but there’s heaps of enthusiasm
in the “Yes, Yes” State, and editor Bodely is a
glutton for hard work.
* * * *
A Groperland pressman was sent to do a church
meeting. As he entered the hall they were intoning
the old familiar yearn :
“Oh, for a thousand tongues to sing!”
The scribe was a statistical fiend, and the report of
the show appeared under the heading: “Wanted —
999 Tongues.”
* * * *
“Chris”: The most scornful thing I ever heard
about our star in the Inky Way floated into my ear
the other morning. “Buy a Dingo Watchman, sir?”
asked the boy. “Buy a Dingo Watchman, is it?”
said the half-beered. “No, I won’t. I’d rather buy
a tin of sardines for the sixpence and read the label.
I’d get as much information and a bellyful as well.”
“Mulwa”: I came across this gem in the hut of a
boundary-rider in the Riverina. It was part of an
Obituary notice which appeared in the Farmer and
Grazier of June 10 last: “He used to say that he
had read the Sydney Morning Herald every issue
since 1841, and his faculties still remained unim-
paired enough to enable him to read it till within a
few days of his death.”
* * * *
One of the queerest sign-posts of the Inky Way
hangs beside the advertising counter in the Age
office. It is a collection of In Memoriam verses —
melancholy samples of the loving bathos annually
renewed by survivors. The reason why the dreadful
lamentations are kept hanging on a hook is that they
are copyright, and the advt. clerk at the counter
must see that no tearful advertiser pirates the wails
of which other mourners have bought the lugubrious
monopoly.
* * * *
William Arnold, for 50 years a proof-reader on
one or other of the Adelaide dailies, was the subject
of a humorous paragraph in the Register on a re-
cent Monday, because a stout piece of firewood, with
powder somewhere in it, had exploded and shot out
of his hearth on the Sunday evening. Two days
later he was the object of a serious paragraph,
having been knocked over and fatally injured by a
motor-car.
T. H. S. Escott is publishing a book on news-
papers, from the earliest period down to the present
day, to be issued by Fisher Unwin. Escott, who has
had a deal of journalistic experience, has succeeded
John Morley as editor of the Fortnightly. Years
ago he also succeeded George Augusta Sala as editor
of the Home News, a paper that had a big Aus-
tralian goldfields circulation in the early ’6o’s. The
success of the News bred half a dozen imitators;
but the cable killed them all.
* * * *
“Bookware”: Professors Rentoul and Tucker, in
Melbourne, continue to make sly, underhand digs at
Australian poetry. They haven’t even Touchstone’s
virtue —“A poor thing, sir, but mine own.” Ren-
toul says Austral poets are to arise. In Shakspeare’s
time, Greene, who called him Shakseene, said a
dramatist was to arise. The decriers of Wagner said
a composer was to arise. Croker, writing down
Dickens in the Quarterly, said a novelist was to.
arise. Outside a pub in my district there is a per-
manent board, inscribed “Free Drinks To-morrow.”
Nay, even in the time of the Highest, did not the
scribes say the Messiah was to come?
* * * *
“Condot”: In “A Note from the Gallery”- (B.
6/7/’ll) the writer mentions that a member of the
N.S.W. Assembly on one occasion threatened to
“pull his windpipe out.” I don’t think that even
N.S.W. ever had a member so ferocious as a Banana-
land legislator who flourished in the ’9o’s. It was
owing to the extreme intemperance of his oratory
that I was instructed to report him verbatim, put-
ting in blanks where the paper would otherwise be
blistered. This made him more ferocious than ever,
and on several occasions he yelled at me from the
floor of the House that I was going to be killed pre-
sently. One night he went stark mad, and sought
me with a hammer. He was a large man and a
blacksmith, and I am under sft. I wasn’t there
when he called at the pressroom. He died shortly
afterwards, but I didn’t do it.
* * * *
“Left Hand”: W. J. Sowden, of the S. A.
Register, complains that Australian poetry is too
drab, has no dramatic power, no humor and
no pathos. Presumably, to remedy these de-
fects, and in particular to make the writers
gay, Sowden runs a column for aspiring poets
in the Saturday Register, and if you woo the
muse you are allowed the barren honor of seeing
your woo in the Tory journal. Naturally, on these
terms he gets dramatic things like this:
MAKING BILLY TEA.
Ah! the dear black thing is boiling—pop in sugar
and the tea;
Stir that round, and from a bottle pour in milk
accordingly.
If you haven’t teaspoons handy, take one of the
cleanest sticks,
For it really doesn’t matter when the milk and
sugar’s mixed.
After that gem of his own choosing, what has Sowden
to complain about ?
* * * *
“Reka”: I was up at Waikaretemarama last week,
and called at the local newspaper office for a copy
of its latest issue. The proprietor, who wore blue
dungaPbes and a green flannel shirt, handed me the
sheet, and said confidentially: “See here, the girl
lost the box of s’s the other day, and we’ve had to
use o’s instead. It looks a bit queer at first, but
you soon get used to it.” The first item to puzzle
me was, “Septimuo Smith, who wao arreoted laot
Saturday, wao found to have oeveral opuriouo coino
in hio poooeooion.” I puzzled that out, and was
confronted with: “The Shakopeare feotival wao
otarted at Stratford-on-Avon yeoterday, oixty-oix
flago being unfurled. Seventeen of Shakopeare’o
playo will be preoented.” When I found something
about “An actreoo’o oucceoo,” I took the sheet to
the office and requested my money back, on the
ground that the rag was printed in a foreign lan-
guage. The proprietor seemed hurt, but gave me
the thruppence. By the way, the paper printed a
warning of its peculiarity that week; but as they
had no “s’s,” they could only hint darkly at the
disappearance of “a certain letter,” which had to be
nameless.
THE UNKIND CUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Victorian Premier’s office has received an inti-
mation that King George has made a rule not to ac-
cept any composition in MS., neither will he accept
the dedication of any work unless the writer is
known to him. Randolph Bedford is expected to feel
very annoyed over this.— Bulletin (6/7/’ll).
Bedford ? A rank disloyalist 1
This hits him not.
It is not meant for him, I wist;
Tis I am shot.
Bedford ! That wild Republican ?
Said George to me:
“Hassan, old chap, Randolph’s a man
/ will not see!”
O God ! Th’ ingratitude of Kings!
When George was here,
Who took him round and showed him things ?
Who stood him beer?
I told him of my little work
On “Harems” ; he
Said, “Dedicate it, obscene Turk,
To none but me.”
But, now he’s got a steady job,
His pal’s forgot.
This isn’t one for Bedford’s nob ;
’Tis / am shot.
VICTORIA.

is the Most Delight-
ful of all Pastimes.
The kodak is the most widely used of Cameras, because it
serves for use at home or abroad, is the best and simplest in
use, and is operated in daylight all the way.
The Kodak Camera serves for utility, pleasure, exercise and
health. It is high-grade in appearance, faultless in construction,
efficient in equipment, perfect in use, right in price. It is equally as
satisfactory for the beginner in photography as for the expert.
For your protection, you should ask for the Kodak Camera by
name, and LOOK FOR THE WORD “EASTMAN” on the
instrument you purchase. “IF IT ISN’T AN EASTMAN, IT
ISN’T A KODAK.”
1 here are Brownie Kodaks, from 5/- to 50/- • regular Kodaks
range from 26/- upwards. The Kodak Catalogue will be sent free
upon request if you cannot call.

hand cameras. Its popularity is world-wide. Takes the full-size post-
card picture, 3^in. x Works in daylight all the way.
Provided with every feature for the most serious work, yet so simple

is loaded with Kastman
KODAK. N. C. FILM.
ONLY KODAK N.C. FILM will give you the greatest possible success that
your equipment will allow. The latitude, the rendering of light, shade and
tonal values, the speed, the non-curling and non-halation properties —all
of these and many other features of superiority in Kodak N.C. Film are not to be
secured in equal degree in any film which is said to be “ just-as-good.”
Look to it, if you hand over your Kodak for loading in a strange store, that no
inferior substitute film is inserted. The quality of your pictures is at stake. Experiment
with no experiments. Look for “ Kodak ”on the spool-end, and “N.C.” on the box.

on AIVGIEK’S.

course of Angier’s Emulsion. Bland and pleasant,
soothing alike to throat, lungs, stomach and intestines,
an aid to appetite and digestion, and a splendid tonic and
builder, it is the ideal remedy for children's ailments, and
no mother should be without a bottle in the house. The
medical profession prescribe Angier’s Emulsion not only
for coughs, bronchitis, whooping cough and all lung
affections, but also for scrofula, rickets and all wasting
diseases. Of Chemists, 1/3, 2/9 and 4/6.
“THE CHANGE IN HER IS WONDERFUL.”
8 Hampden Street, North Sydney, Aus.
Dear Sirs, —My little girl has been a constant anxiety owing to
loss of appetite and a very trying cough. I was so pleased to see
her relish her food after she had had a sample bottle of Angier’s
that I got a large (4/6) bottle, which she has just finished. The
change in her is wonderful; the cough is quite gone, she has put on
firm flesh and has a good colour and appetite. To have accomplished
this with a really palatable mixture is a great cause of thankfulness
to me and you have my full consent to publish this letter in case it
may persuade other mothers to try Angier’s Etriulsion.
(Signed) Mrs. K. WILKINS.

IN BANANA LAND
Queensland Labor party has just ex-
pelled M.L.A. Crawford over his vote on the
Bible in State Schools Bill. He is the
23rd man cast out from the party. When
will the procession end?
Les Seary, who is likely to meet Harry
Livingstone in a shearing match next Janu-
ary, put up a pretty good performance re-
cently at Galway Downs, where he “rung”
the shed. His best tally, was 219, but he
went over 200 a score of times, and aver-
aged over 200 throughout, working 7hrs.
20min. per day.
Entomologist Tryon, lecturing on the mos-
quito. the other day, gave Bananalanders the
pleasing information that their country sup-
ported 50 varieties of the pest in ease and
comfort. Tryon strongly advocates kero-
sening, and, when asked if the process would
not spoil the water, replied that the liquid
known by that name in certain Bananaland
towns would probably be improved by the ad-
dition of petroleum.
The life of the itinerant teacher in West-
ern Bananaland is not all beer and skittles.
It isn’t even all beer. M. J. Healey, one of
them, recently sent in a report from Curra-
willa Waterfloles, near Longreach, to the
Education Department. Here is a piece of
it:—
Conditions are worse than when I visited these
parts in March last. There is no coach running
’twixt Mooraberrie and Birdsville (140 miles). At
Currawilla there is not a feed for a goat for miles
around the station. Camels are being used by the
boundary-riders. I was strongly recommended not
to attempt the journey, owing" to waterless and
grassless stages ’twixt Beetoota and Diamantina
River. Camels and pack-horses convey mails only
from Mooraberrie to Birdsville. I could not get a
camel for the journey. The few horses required at
Currawilla are being fed on hay. I could not pur-
chase any, and turned my tired, hungry and thirsty
horses S.E. towards Mornie. At the only waterhole
for 10 miles, I stopped to give them a drink. I
unyoked, and the boy was leading the two when they
got a sniff of camels that were feeding at a bush,
and which I had not seen. The horses bolted with
the harness on. One dived into the waterhole, and
clambered out on the opposite bank ; the other van-
ished over a sandhill.
“Drought” headlines haven’t got into the
papers yet; but the state of the country in
the sou’-west of the State ought to be a
healthy reminder to plunging politicians
that the Devil hasn’t gone out of business.
It looks as though the usual political
swindle is being worked in Bananaland.
Hugh Nelson, knowing that the State was
always liable to have to either go into debt
or" borrow, in order to tide over bad seasons,
put a Bill through, providing that the sur-
pluses in good years should go towards re-
ducing the debt. It worked all right for a
while ; then Ividston collared the threatened
surplus of a good year, and ear-marked it
for some special expenditure. Thus he
practically wiped the surplus out, so that
the debt wasn’t reduced anything worth talk-
ing about. Now, it seems, the same game
has been played by Dig. Denham this year,
the surplus available for debt reduction be-
ing sweated down to a mere thousand or
two.
Harris, P.M., remarked at the Brisbane
City Police Court a few days ago, concern-
ing a case in which a married couple taking
a position on a station were compelled to
leave a child in the city: “A pity that sta-
tion owners should have such an aversion
to children.” Whereupon Sub-Inspector
King, who knows the West, said he
was well awmre “that many station owners
had a pronounced aversion to having chil-
dren about the place.” Pure Merino cannot
stand youngsters —they don’t carry fleeces.
A Chef (Chinese).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Chef (Chinese) disengaged, excellent reference,
city or North preferred.—Coußimi advt.
A Chef (Chinese) ! Romantic recollections
Float with the words on ev’ry passing breeze;
We dream of Canton cooks and quaint refections,
Of rat ragout, sam-shu and wondrous teas ;
Once more we sniff the pie-hid feline’s savor,
An odor comes of shark and beche-de-mer,
While now and then a weird and witching flavor
Of buried eggs appears to fill the air.
And all our soul goes out in one great yearning
To sample what the Orient can do ;
To taste her springs of culinary learning,
And sip the gay concoctions she may brew ;
And all we need to ope the curious portal
And all her cherished dainties so to seize
Is but to snare that magic-working mortal
Who dubs himself in print—a Chef (Chinese).
Our palate wearies of these Western dishes,
This everlasting chop and Irish stew;
We harbor other aims and higher wishes,
We fain would see what Almond Eyes can do ;
Within our brain there rings a clamor dreary:
“Confound your stupid British beer and cheese;
We want to peck at something quaint and eerie—
We pine for him, this simple Chef (Chinese).”
Perchance the dainties dished up by this wizard
Were served one-time to mighty Genghis Khan ;
Perchance his soup hath pleased the potent gizzard
Of leaders fierce who lead the Mongol van;
Perchance in curious, ancient, pig-tailed revels,
His culinary art knew how to please,
Wherefore no marvel if we foreign devils
Would fain secure that gem—a Chef (Chinese) !
If he were ours, mayhap when life has faded
And all things smack of yellow leaves and sere,
When pulses slowlier beat and taste is jaded
And Joy’s sweet cup is filled with bitter beer,
llow might we yet recall in mem’ry grateful,
Mid many a sad, regretful, senile wheeze,
That Mongol artist with his mystic plateful
Who danced attendance as a Chef (Chinese) !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
SICK ROOM SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BY EDMUND FISHER
/ ''AIII Day Nurse, with her morning smile,
-L Comes gaily on the scene
Where shapes of darkness lurked awhile
With fireflecks in between.
The window raised admits the breeze
That blows so kindly through the trees.
And fragrant is its breath;
Dark Night Nurse now, her duly done,
Retires before the rising sun.
The world’s astir, its task begun —-
Begone! all thoughts of death!
Fair Day Nurse brings the patient food;
And will not take a nay;
Protesting “This will do you good,”
She brings it on a tray;
And though one be extremely loth
To try the fish, or sip the broth,
That fact is not discussed.
The angel props one up in bed,
Her plump warm hands behind one’s head
And feeble “can’t” is captive led
By firm, decisive “must.”
Each evening, on the stroke of eight,
There steals into the room
Dark Night Nurse, soft a-foot as Fate,
A harbinger of gloom.
The hours she marks give scanty rest
To. mind or body, brain or breast.
How spirit-like she seems
When steadily she comes a-creep
To see if one be yet asleep I
The end of life is slumber deep
That knows no fitful dreams.
Twixt time and time, in light or shade,
One fain would live or die.
Fair Day Nurse, in her smiles arrayed,
Is graceful to the eye.
Her chatter is a song of life,
Of strength to do and healthful strife,
For one, and each, and all.
Dark Night Nurse, with the pensive face,
Has more of gentleness and grace j
But her reflections on the case
Cast shadows on the wall.
What ho t good Day Nurse, laugh you loud,
And flood the room with light,
And tear from off my soul the shroud
Of sad foreboding night!
Now let me not on sickness brood,
But tease me with your talk of food
You mean to make me eat!
1 The hours are gone, when pain oppressed
My hopes lay prone and Death seemed best,
To the great Sun-god I’ve confessed
That life again is sweet.
VICTORIA.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 22, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
The Macgregor clan is gathering again.
This time ’tis in preparation for a bail,
which will take place on August 9, the night
after the Assembly, so methinks the same
decorations will gaze down upon the same
frocks. It is understood that the Chieftain
was disappointed at the scarcity of young
blooms at the Coronation whirl, so this
frolic has been specially arranged to give
them another chance to blossom to his Ex.’s
satisfaction.
The Centennial Hall nearly broke its belt
on Thursday night, struggling to hold the
tons of seething Brisbane that wanted to
view the tableau entertainment for which the
Vision and the tender olive twigs of our
Most Exclusive were responsible. The
show was in the interests of the Queen
Alexandra Home, and, as the expenses must
have, been inconsiderable, the balance will
require the best part of a wheelbarrow for
itself. His Ex. and Lady Mac. apparently
agreed with me that the tableaux were good,
but I don’t know what they thought of the
dreary 20 minutes’ wait between each set-
ting. The only person who seemed to enjoy
these long intervals was the man who sat
next to me. He went out each time, and,
towards the end, when he walked on my
toes, his tread was nearly as heavy as the
m, a ?? ran P e - of whisky which accompanied him.
The Vision got big helpings of applause
when she appeared with a bunch of old love-
letters in a representation of “Memories,”
and, again, as a chrysanthemum in a garden
of sister blooms. ITilma Ohman ran her a
close second, and Ida Chambers divided hon-
ors with Mabel Brady, who appeared en-
twined in the folds of a hefty-looking cobra.
Unlike the original snake, this one was se-
curely chloroformed, so it wasn’t as exciting
as it might have been.
Wee Macgregor still takes hospitals very
seriously : presides over their meetings ; cock’s
a severe medical eye at the wards ; discusses
learnedly with the resident medico on the
habits of the wild microbe: and generally
leaves behind an impression that if he wasn’t
a frilled and tasselled Australian Governor
he could be of some use.
It is fearful and pathetic to watch the
hapless A.D.C. at a women’s hospital, quietly
dogging the steps of the Vice-Reine as
she doles out sympathetic utterances. One
lobster-complexioned gentleman-help was, in
the execution of his duty, forced to sit tight,
at a meeting of the Girls’ Friendly Society
some time ago, when a Governor’s spouse
remarked in dulcet tones, on being told of the
resignation of several members: “Could not
some effort be made to induce them to join
the Mothers’ Union?”
March King Sousa arrived here last Tues-
day in cold, bleak weather not unsuitable for
keeping Polar regions up to specifications,
lie was promply “received,” escorted to West’s
Olympia, and subjected to the usual speech-
making, at which Mayor Diddams, the At-
torney-General and J. W. Blair were the
chief offenders. Sousa then rose and con-
fidentially murmured a reply into the May-
oral ear. It may have been a brilliant
speech, but, as it was perfectly inaudible,
I wouldn’t swear to it. But I heard his
band later in the day, and you can take my
word for that part of the business.
“Maritana” was again dragged before the
footlights by a company of Brisbane amateurs
last Tuesday evening, and the performance
showed courage and perseverance, if nothing
else. The entertainment was in aid of the
Hospital, and is expected to return to that
institution sufficient to make its treasurer
feel he has had a birthday.
Queenslanders in London gathered together'
on June 15 for their annual dinner, and,
after doing their best in regard to each
other’s health, passed on to a reception given
by Agent-General Robinson to Andy Fisher.
People who barely knew one another at
home, or referred coldly to “that woman”
when in Brisbane, were enthusiastic in their
greetings, so glad were they to see an Aus-
tralian face in big, lonely London. Bris-
bane was represented by Captain and Mrs.
Colonna, the McWhirters, the A. R. Men-
zies, Nettie Evans, Mrs. Lockhart Spence
and others.
The Warehams, who are in the shipping
line, danced and fed the gilded youth of this
town on the occasion of a son’s coming of
age. Mrs. Wareham’s frock was something
in jet over pink, and daughter Elsie wore a
foam of cream chiffon.
The Commercial Travellers’ Club burst in-
to hospitality on Saturday, when President
and Mrs. Horton extended the annual glad
hand to the female relatives of members, and
led them gently to where afternoon tea sim-
mered on the hob or the next handiest place.
Mr. and Mrs. P. Tully, of Ray station,
near Adavale, had a “golden wedding” re-
cently. Almost the whole of their married
life has ben spent at Ray. Their first house
was a hut of mud and thatch, and the nearest
town was Wilcannia (N.S.W.), 500 miles
away. One of the Tully boys recently mar-
ried a daughter of deceased Speaker Leahy.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
One adult one vote in Bananaland has by
no means borne, so far, the expected fruit of
equal opportunities for women. The other
day there were 38 vacancies in the senior
division of the Public Service, and girls
were eligible for only three. Woman has a
lot of arrears to make up yet.
The Lady Bowen Hospital (Brisbane) is
pointing a proud finger to its record for
last year. Out of 547 patients only three
died, and all these were beyond medical aid
when admitted. Since the institution was
opened in 18(54, 10,181 little immigrants
have arrived through its doors.
Brisbane Courier on the renewal of the
Anglo-Jap treaty: “The practical extension
of the alliance to 1921 will probably enable
those Australians who have been in dread of
the Yellow Peril to sleep in their beds for
another decade.” And the Jap—will he
turn over for another 10 years’ slumber,
too?
“A Juddite”: The insectarian no doubt
has a hard row to hoe in out-back Queens-
land ; still on the coast you stumble over
things like this (motion adopted by the
Anglican Synod at Rockhampton) : —-
# That this Synod desires to express its strongest
disapproval of the widespread habit prevalent
throughout the diocese of members of the Church of
England giving contributions towards the funds of
other religious bodies, especially when the diocese
and parochial funds are far from satisfactory.
“Feed My Lambs” —but be sure, first, that
they’ve got the right ear-mark!

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and 7io MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any Stale) accomnatiy.

I was born at street
On the 13th of November :
With difficulty we made ends meet,
I very well remember ;
My father got from bad to worse,
My mother toiled and slaved,
At times she’d mutter “Drink’s a curse,
He’ll squander all I’ve saved.”
At last one day a cab he got,
The No. was thirteen.
“Thirteen bad luck ? That’s only rot!
’Twill turn in many a bean!”
The thirteenth day the horse fell down,
My father’s life was spent;
The only money half a crown
Was left to pay the rent.

Three of the N.S.W. Supreme Court
Judges may be classed as Churchy. Chief
Justice Cullen is as much at home at a
Church meeting, or an “Army” function, as on
the Bench; Justice Bring is entirely un-
worldly, notwithstanding his long acquaint-
ance with law; and Justice Cohen is a pillar
of the Synagogue. The other occupants get
their sleep otherwise.
“Mr. Ocky” : Fat and his wife used to cry
“Shame” to the assertions at Referenda
time that thousands of pounds were being
splashed to hinder Australia and help the
monopolist and the sweater. Now the bal-
ance-sheets of some of the bodies which
supported Fat are being presented to mem-
bers, and the value of the indignation is
coming to light. In my small village the
Women’s Fashionable League owns that it
got £45 10s. from “headquarters” towards
Referendum expenses—£4s 10s. for one small
league in one small township. It went, ap-
parently, in afternoon teas. The old Abo.
discussion, “Do snakes slime their prey?”
was answerer in the affirmative, wasn’t it?
The old and monstrous enemy called Rent
is likely to receive a severe blow if Labor
wins the next elections in Victoria. This
dragon is specially fierce in Port Melbourne,
a suburb containing a greater proportion of
wretched, dilapidated huts than any other;
but for all its decayed condition, rents are
high. Recent advances have varied from
five to 50 per cent., and the landlord (with
a groan) says that the land tax is ruining
him. Therefore the Port Melbourne Council
(the only Labor municipal government in
Melbourne) has asked Opposition-leader
Prendergast to draft a Fair Rent Bill. For
the present the alleged “Liberals” will greet
such a Bill with howls, but it will come
along all the same.


Rifles, Shotguns'and Cartridges
THE RED W BRAND
There is such an intimate relation between a gun
and its cartridges that neither will shoot well if there
is any defect in either. The cartridges must be
uniform in size and shooting strength no less than
the gun must be accurate and reliable. The combi-
nation of Winchester guns and cartridges seems best
to fulfill these requirements. And why should not
this be so ? for Winchester guns and cartridges are
made in the same factory and tested together. Win-
chester Repeating and Single Shot Rifles and Repeat-
ing Shotguns are made for all kinds of shooting, and
Winchester Cartridges for all makes of shotguns, rifles
a:~d pistols. Get the celebrated Winchester make.

see the Chalmers —men from the North of Queensland, men from Tasmania, men from all over
We take this condition to mean that the Chalmers car has not only now made a good reputa-

appeal to the careful buyers here.
Out of the 46 cars we have sold since Christmas, 35 of them were Chalmers cars; out of
these 35 purchasers, 12 were experienced motorists —men who had previously owned cars. Again,
there were four repeat orders from Chalmers owners.

miles in 10 weeks, and the front tyres still good. These records speak for themselves.

Another Australian Record
on the “Afcock” Table.
TO the remarkable series of record breaks by Gray, Roberts, Williams and
Lindrum, already performed on the “Aleock” Table, is added another record
by Lind rum. His latest achievement was in the Melbourne match with
Reece, when he secured the phenomenal break of 1239—1230 coming off the red,
establishing two Australian records. All these champions have expressed their
high opinion of Aleock Tables, for huge breaks are only possible where the table
is “ true to angle,” with cushions, jaws and every other pai t absolutely perfect.
Both Gray and Lindrum owe their present reputations largely to the
“Aleock” Table. It is on these tables they practised and made many of their
largest breaks. We will gladly post you our illustrated Billiard Catalogue.

ARE YOU
D E A F?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientific de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no mat-
ter how old you are. There is no mystery
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully and
explain just how it acts.
You -use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and
in the same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an aid to weak hearing. But,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Eat-Drums are invisible and comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the
strain off them —the strain of trying to hear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it lies in the ear. You will
notice that the Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Brum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to the natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
and tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may
wear the Ear-Drums day and night, and take
them out whenever you wish. Children can
wear them (we have special sizes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the. fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bought you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do hot send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Odtfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as "soon as vou
can.

BACK, LEGS AND HANDS BADLY AFFECTED—DREADFUL SWELLING

Mrs. Stapleton, who recommends
Bile Beans to all sufferers.
I WAS a great sufferer from rheumatics,”
* says Mrs. A. Stapleton, of 136 Forbes
Street, Sydney. “It caught me most
severely in the back, legs and hands,
and the pain was so dreadful that it was
impossible for me to get about. Sharp
twinges would shoot through the affected
parts with torturing effect. My back
and limbs swelled up enormously,
and 1 used to dread anyone to come
near me for fear they would touch me.
Even the slightest pressure, as from
the bed-clothes, would cause me great
agony.
“ I suffered thus for a long time with-
out getting ease from anything. Bile
Beans being recommended to me as a
proved cure for rheumatics, I started a
course, and presently I was conscious
of great relief. The twinges grew less
in their violence and frequency, the
inflammation and pain left the parts, and
the swelling went down. Altogether I
felt very much better, so I continued with
Bile Beans until 1 was quite recovered.
“ Bile Beans are a grand medicine, and I have also found them a
splendid remedy for biliousness. I suffered terribly from this complaint
and severe headaches, which used to prostrate me for days together and
render me unfit for anything. A course of Bile Beans completely cured, me of these distressing
troubles, and now a dose of Bile Beans Keeps me free from them. From my experience I can
confidently recommend Bile Beans as a splendid remedy for rheumatics and biliousness.”

EARS
Matchless for the Complexion.
By Sunda Strait.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE lies an isle by Sunda Strait,
Beyond the roads where steamers
Ply;
It knows no law of fare and freight;
Tears pass, and scarce a sail goes by
Betwixt the ocean and the sky;
It lies alone, to dream and wait
By Sunda Strait.
The sun with tropic fervor burns
Its ragged central summits bare,
And daily to his height returns,
And nightly sinks into his lair
With respite to the swollen air,
Which breathes amid the tangled ferns
And orchid-urns.
Strange flowers bespangle Nature’s hem,
And clinging verdure intertwines;
While scattering many a brilliant gem,
It clothes the rocks with woven vines—
Clematis-withes and wild rose-bines —
With supple arms embracing them,
Leaf, branch and stem.
Charmed winds about the island blow ;
They breathe across the still lagoons
And temper day’s too ardent glow,
Or sigh at night to mystic moons
By grots where some sea-monster croons
Old threnodies to waves below —
Insistent, slow.
From stress of towns that may not wait,
From highroads of the land and sea,
Ah, that this isle had been the gate
To havens of strange peace to me!
Here would I dream out life, and be
The hostage to a kindlier fate,
By Sunda Strait.
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.
Nemesis in the North.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HERB was a steely haze on Pearler’s
I Bay, and Drawing Room James
cursed in the lurid manner which had
given him his nickname of Drawing Room
in exchange for the baptismal one of Leslie.
He cursed again, spat viciously into the im-
palpable dust beyond the doorway of the
North Australian Hotel—the doorway that
showed an oblong of the tide-bared flats and
beyond them the dead purple of the Indian
Ocean. Drawing Room looked at it as if he
were weary of it all, and then hurried to
the roadway as he heard the quick breathing
of a willing and weary horse and the pad-
ding of its hoofs in the sharp sand.
“Jimmy, by God!” said Drawing Room.
“Jimmy it is,” said the new-comer, alight-
ing wearily—“and damned tired, too.”
“My grief! Come in and drink, man.”
‘‘Don’t mind if I do,” said James McNair,
entering the bar. He doffed his cabbage-
tree to wipe his forehead, and then you saw'
that he was a young, cheerful, middle-sized
man —that he was prematurely and almost
Totally bald, from much head-sweating in
the tropics.
“How’s Mourama ?” he said, with his
whisky in his hand and regarding Drawing
Room very intently.
“Before you drink with me, Jimmy, I want
to tell you that I’m no damned good. My
grief! Man, I went to sleep, and Mourama
cleared out on your tracks.”
“All right, old man—can’t help that —do
better next time”
“Why don’t you shoot him and be safe?”
enquired the publican.
“I can’t do that, old man —I killed his
father. The damned fool says I killed his
father twice.”
“Whv. how’s that?”
“Well, his father speared two bullocks o’
mine when I was squattin’ down Big Waters
way, and I shot the old man an’ took Mour-
ama—he was only a nipper then. Then his
tribe got hold of him again, and when he
came back he had a turkey’s thigh bone
through his nose and three cuts on his
breasts to show he was a seven months
child. His mother done that, and she done
some more, too. She told him about me
killin’ his old man. And then he began to
stalk me- —-followin’ me for weeks. I lost all
my nerve with that black cow followin’ me
an’ never seein’ him near —never hearin’ him,
only knowin’ he was on my tracks. So one
day I made up my mind to take his spear or
bring him in with me; and I goes straight
out to his camp and waits, finds his gin
Pmyarra, and ties her up in the mia-mia
so’s she couldn’t telegraph him I was there.
By-an’-bye along he comes, an’ as soon as he
gets in the mia-mia r hits him a bash on his
Adam’s apple and grasses him. When ne
comes round he sees I got him beat, and so I
says, ‘Look here, Mourama, you bin stay
longa me all the time —sposin’ you bin run
away, I shootem you.’ An’ he says, ‘Sposin’
you walk longa front of me, by Crise, I kill-
em you.’ ‘What for you killem me?’ says I.
‘You bin killem my father,’ he says. So I
says, ‘Oh, that’s yer game, is it.’ ‘Well, my
black dog,’ says I to myself. ‘l’ll never let
you out of my sight again, and I’ll take good
care never to be alone with you in the
bush.’ Let’s have another drink. ... Oh!
the second killing of his father? Well, I
got Mourama back 12 months ago, an’
about three months alter I’d stiffened him
in the mia-mia, me an’ Lrawin’ Room James
an’ Mourama was camped down on the
Katherine. We goes out to get some meat
for the camp, and I lifts my rifle an’ covers
a big kangaroo. An’ Mourama he comes up
an’ he says, ‘You no shootem that one—mine
bin thinkem that feller my father.’ Well, I
laughs an’ fires, and kills the ’roo, an’ me an’
Drawin’ Room laughs at Mourama. He
doesn’t say much, only sulks. When he goes
along to skin that ’roo I looked round an’
remarked this was the place where I shot
Mourama’s father, and Mourama, o’ course,
knowin’ the place, thinks the old mans come
to life again an’ bin killed the second time,
An’ I daren’t let him out o’ my sight or
Drawin’ Room’s since then.”
“You’d better shoot him and have no
further trouble,” said James.
“None o’ that, James,” replied McNair,
not unkindly—“l won’t kill him in cold
blood.”
“And you’ll never be quick enough to kill
him when your blood’s up, Jimmy, because
it won’t be up for long,” said Drawing
Room.
“I bin takem horse longa yard, boss?”
They turned and saw Mourama grinning—
the certificate of his premature birth accen-
tuated by the white pipeclay bars on his
glossy skin.
“Yowi, you bin takem,” replied McNair
with not a tremor in voice or eye. The
shedder of blood and the avenger looked at
one another with mutual respect for a mo-
ment. Then the black man turned and led
the horse away.
The three whites had another drink in
oppressive silence, broken by the publican
enquiring:
“What’s the show like?”
“I tell you mis is all right, refractory ore
worth seven ounces all through, and I’m goin’
over to see it again in a few days an’ take
it up ; it’s under 70 mile from here, about,
an’ in good country —plenty o’ grass an’
water.”
“You can’t go in a few days, old man;
I’ll be away a week at Darwin —I’m sailing
there to-morrow. And if yoA go who’s to
look after Mourama?”
“All right, old man, I’ll wait the week.”
So next day Drawing Room James,
having visited the camp and interviewed
Mourama, who was duly impressed with the
fact that death for Mourama and Mourama’s
family would follow any injury to Jimmy
McNair, sailed away in his cutter on one of
his mysterious quarterly voyages.
# # * *
Jimmy McNair was one of the most ener-
getic men who ever lived, and after three
days of the week had passed he began to
chafe at the delay. Then did Ben, the
bloated publican, discover a brilliant idea.
“Look here, Jimmy,” he said. “I’m a
J.P. I’ll get somebody to send Mourama
out ’orse-huntin’, an’ as soon as he’s gorn
you charge him with abscondin’ —he’s bound
to you fer 12 munce yet. Then we gets the
trooper to lumber ’im, an’ ’e’s brought be-
fore me, an’ I remands ’im till we can git
another J.P., an’ that won’t be till Drawin’
Room comes back —’e’s the on’y other beak
’ere.”
That night Mourama was arrested, re-
manded for a month, and chained to the
gum log that served the settlement for gaol,
lock-up and stockade.
And next morning Jimmy McNair, having
left a letter for - Drawing Room, saddled up
his big chestnut, Arafura, and his two pack-
horses and jogged out ,of the township, going-
east and full of joy at the prospect of a new
track.
Mourama, chained up to the big tree, saw
him go, but only shut his eyes tightly for an
instant so that the flies buzzed a little be-
fore settling on him again.
True to the week. Drawing Room returned
to Pearler’s Bay. He left the boat in charge
of his Malay crew and strolled to the North
Australian Hotel, and enquired for McNair.
“Oh, Jimmy got restless after you’d been
a couple er days gorn, an’ he cleared out
day before yestiddy for this new Mount Mor-
gan of his.”
Drawing Room’s face clouded, and then
grew very grey.
“The fool! Oh, the fool! And Moura-
ma?”
“Oh, we run ’im in for abscondin’, an’
put ’im on the chain. ’E come before me,
an’ I remanded ’im for a month.”
“Good man!” said Drawing Room, joy-
ously. “Jimmy ’ll be as right as pie.”
“I think so,” replied the publican—“’e
got too good a start for Mourama.”
“Too good a start—why what d’ye, mean,
Ben?”
“Well, Jimmy left day before yesterday,
an’ Mourama didn’t get off the chain till
some time last night.”
Drawing Room’s face was very grey
again.
“My grief ! Escaped !”
“Yes —got a flint somehow, an’ wore, the
chain through. So they sent to Mount
Misery for the trackers.”
“Then he’s done. Trackers can’t save
McNair. He’s done; that Mourama has
waited too long for him now.”
“Now, don’t you worry,” said Ben
“Jimmy McNair’s not the man to go under
to a nigger.”
“Not to any nigger—or to most niggers—
but Mourama’s worn Jimmy down, and if a
caterpillar can get on a man’s nerves and
make him funk, a caterpillar can kill that
man. We die of what we’re most fright-
ened of.”
“Don’t fret,” said Ben again. “’Ave a
snifter.”
A “snifter” was Ben’s cure for every-
thing—a broken leg, or bad news, or the
death of a mate.
“Not a drop till I’ve seen Jimmy. Let’s
have the black horse, Ben, and 50 car-
tridges.”
“They’re yours all the time, Drawin’
Room. If yer bent on followin’ it’s no
good me chinnin’.”
He took the cartridges from a gun case
under the bar counter, called to his nigger
to bring round the big black gelding, and in
five minutes Drawing Room left, well
mounted and armed with a rifle and a big
Colt’s revolver.
“Where to-first ?” Ben called after him.
“Blacks’ camp—if Jimmy’s dead they’ll
drink misery,” replied James over his
shoulder.
At his own camp he stopped, dismounted,
saddled and bridled his own horse, mounted
again, and leading the spare horse, galloped
to the blacks’ camp.
“Thought so,” he said, as he dismounted
and tied the horses to a sapling. “Only the
old women left here —-all of the men gone
helping Mourama.”
He addressed a particularly old, thin,
and dirty gin who crouched over a little
fire smoking a short blacx pipe.
Drawung Room spoke very sternly.
“Where Mourama?”
“Me no bin know.”
“Where Mourama lubra Pinyarra?”
The old gin pointed to a mia-mia, and
Drawing Room James, hearing the rustling
of frightened flight, wasted no time. Dash-
ing around the mia-mia, he caught Pinyarra
naked as she was born, her child held to her

BURDENS LIFTED FROM BAD BACKS.
WEARY is the back that bears the burden of kidney ills. There’s no rest or peace for the man or woman who has a bad
back. The distress begins in early morning. You feel lame and not refreshed. It’s hard to get out of bed. It hurts
to stoop to tie your shoes. All day the ache keeps up. Any sudden movement sends sharp twinges through the
back. It is torture to stoop and straighten. At night the sufferer retires to toss and twist and groan. Backache is kidney
ache —a, throbbing dull aching in the kidneys. Plasters or liniments won’t do. You must get at the cause, inside.

backache. My lasting cure proves that Doan’s Backache Kidney

. Now that she knew Mourama was to be
killed anyhow, whether she aided or not she
had no further doubt; but James showed no
desire to loosen the straps on her ankles.
By-and-bye she struck the track of
Mourama, signed to him to go faster, and
kent her eyes on the ground as the horses
togged along at four miles or so an hour.
* * * *
Fifty miles ahead Jimmy McNair sat in
the starlight bv his fire, his dog sleeping
contentedly in the shadow. McNair smoked
and smilefl to himself as a man does when
his thoughts are pleasant; for the big moun-
tain towering above him, and barring into
tails the river that flowed at his feet, was
Jimmy McNair’s “refractory ore proposition
worth seven ounces, every ton of it.” Occa-
TTrL u b , e bstened for the bells on his
riTT oTiT and the packers ; and, hearing
' j JV c being very satisfied with his pipe
Qi DQ r, t TrTT oUnta l- l i n v, tbe night, he went to

* *
And 15 miles behind Jimmy McNair,
Mourama crouched between two giant ant-
.T b* s Inst for McNair’s blood struggling
with his superstitious fear of the darkness,
his ears listening for the dawn.
At the first blush of day he arose, picked
up the tracks of three unshod horses, and
moved onwards easterly—six feet of black
strength, intelligence and ferocity.
At dawn Drawing Room and Pinyarra
were also moving. They had picked up the
tracks long before, and three hours after
sunrise they entered a great volcanic gorge
whose rich soil produced grass man-high,
and flowers of crimson and blue and ivory
and gold. But these two had no eyes for all
this beauty; they were intent on a track
through the swathes of the high grass—as
plain as a ship’s wake,
* * * *
Jimmy McNair, having all the morning
prospected his golden mountain, with wan-
ing enthusiasm, returned to his camp at the
meridian. And the first thing Jimmy
McNair saw on nearing his camp were the
bodies of his two pack-horses. The slayer
had ca rved a great steak from the flank of
o ne of them,
McNair’s heart stood still for a moment.
He knew that unmounted but armed he was
equal to only 20 savages now. His hand
went to his revolver. Then another thought
possessed him and he dashed into his tent
for the Winchester. It was gone, although
he had hidden part of it in the bunk, and the
stock under a pack-saddle.
A sickly sweat poured over him. He knew
Mourama had escaped and tracked him. As
h e stood undecided there came a sound like
the sudden stroking of silk with a long
thumb-nail, and a spear ripped through the
t ent and out at the other wall.
McNair ran from the tent and looked
around. Not a tremble in the reeds, not a
splash in the river, not a movement breath-
ing i n the tall and windless grass.
Another spear came—out of the sky, it
seemed—and grazed McNair’s shin so that he
soon f e i t j n a dull, horribly-satisfied sort of
wa y that his boot was full of blood.
He looked at the silver wattles around him,
at the gorgeous wild f uc hsias, at the crimson
water lilies in the back-water—the only clear
space of the river where the reed had not
grown—and wondered what was the use of
fi§htin S- Why shouldn t men live in peace !
Rut he had killed the father of Mourama!
Well! Mourama’s father deserved it.
McNair shouted: “Hey, Mourama, you
black swine. Come out an’ shoot me.
Where’s the rifle, yer black cow?”
Then he remembered Mourama had the rifle
only. He, Jimmy McNair, had the cart-
ridges. When he thought of Mourama’s
blunder he laughed aloud and turned,
And there was Mourama at the edge of
the timber, his spear shipped in womerah
ready for throwing.
McNair’s revolver cracked. A spurt of
dark red showed on the left upper arm of
Mourama. He retreated to the timber, and
the revolver barked at him again.
For two hours that dance of death, that
grim play, went on in the setting of all this
beauty; the wounded black man hovering
round that oasis flanked by the timber and
the reeds; the bleeding white man jumping
in the silence when he heard the frenzied
beating of his own heart, his nerves crack-
ing to the horror of the suspense.
Mourama, having been as silent as the
lightning, vied the noise of thunder:
“Hi, Missa Mackenare, I bin talkem you.”
lie held up Ins hands to signify that he
was unarmed; but McNair knew that this
only meant that Mourama had the spear be-
tween his toes, and was dragging it through
the tall grass till he got near enough to be
sure of a kill.
McNair’s revolver .cracked again, and
Mourama retreated very suddenly to the
scrub.
Then Jimmy felt for cartridges for an-
other reload, and found none. Great fear
left him then. He began to weigh the
■chances.
Half an hour passed—Mourama made no
sign. McNair’s eyes and lips and brain
were baked with suspense and nerve-wrack.
The sun semed to be pecking at his brain.
He threw the revolver away, and called
through dry lips-
“Gome an’ wrastle, you black cow.”
Mourama advanced very delicately, his
left arm hanging limply. He smiled,
avoided McNair’s clumsy attempt to catch
him, and thrust the spear through the white
man’s lungs. McNair grasped the spear in
both hands as if to help it on its way,
grinned foolishly and fell, his hands still
upon the spear.
Drawing Room James and Pinyarra
reached the end of the tracks by mid-after-
noon, and Drawing Room knew that he was
too late. First the horses and the dis-
turbed camp; then, nearer the scrub, the
body of Jimmy McNair, his bald head shin-
ing in the sun.
“My poor mate!” said James, his heart
swollen nearly to bursting. “Now for Mour-
ama! Where Mourama, Pinyarra?”
“Him bin go way,” whimpered the little
gin. “Bimeby him bin come back plenty
black feller—they bin eatem Missa McNair.”
But they waited till dusk and no black
men appeared, though there were many
•cooees from the scrub, and smoke signals on
the hills.
James sought his tutor again.
“What for they no bin come —seen our
tracks?”
“Yowi. they bin see-em track; Mourama
plenty frikened.”
“Pinyarra, you bin sit down alonga here.
I bin sit down alonga scrub —altogether
quiet. Bimeby you bin cooee longa Mour-
ama. When Mourama come I bin shootem
quick feller.” He threw open the chamber
of the rifle, looked at it and snapped it
again.
“Mourama bin killem me,” she whim-
pered.
“I bin killem you spose’n you no cooee
longa Mourama. I killem Mourama before
he bin hurtem you.”
That settled it; she whimpered and agreed.
Drawing Room James crouched in the scrub
after sweeping the fire to a dead tree, which
burst into flame.
Pinyarra cooeed, and in the silence they
heard the far-off crackling of reeds. The
fire leaped and fell against the blue black-
ness of the night, and Pinyarra’s baby began
to cry.
The rustling in the reeds grew louder —
Mourama and two other black men advanced
into the glare of the fire.
Mourama, seeing Pinyarra, divined in a
thought the how and why of her transport
there; grunting his rage, he advanced with
his spear, and, when he was a few yards off
her, Drawing Room James shot him through
the body.
Another shot, and the late Mourama’s
companions performed prodigies in the way of
escape.
* * * *
Pinyarra ate a good meal from McNair’s
stores, and then slept the sleep of the full,
which is about a dozen times as sound
the sleep of the merely just. At dawn they
returned to Pearler’s Bay. Drawing Room
was very silent and sat heavy on his horse
I»ut Pinyarra, who had fastened a crimson
water-lily on each side of her horse’s head-
stall, and had also, with a fine eye for color
effect, put another lily in her own hair and
yet another into the grasping hand of her
swan-black baby, was very merry.
“My word. Missa Drawing Room, I bin
makem that feller Mourama plenty tired He
no bin beatem me plenty for long time. He
very saucy feller Mourama.”
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Universal Brothers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IIE Eool of the Family played with a
I fad.
And a knife, and deal, and a piece of
wire,
In the midnight hours, till they thought him
mad,
And they turned him out to a brute fur
hire.
And the years went by, and the world was
blind,
And he lived or starved, and they did noi
care —
Till a great invention was given mankind,
And the Family Fool was a. millionaire!
The Family Coward was thin and white,
More contemptible than the Family Fool—
A cur that would neither swear nor tight,
And they mocked in his shrinking face at
school.
The years went by, and a charge was made
That altered a nation’s destiny,
In the dust and blood of a Light Brigade—
And the leader was Colonel Kerr, Y.C.
The Family Dunce was a delicate lad,
And he could not spell, and he scarce could
write,
And they deemed him an idiot born, or mad,
For he’d dream and dream by the fire at
night:
And the years went by and a song was writ,
And the music was made by the “Idiot’s"
friend —
And armies of Liberty marched to it
And won through a hundred defeats in the
end.
* * * *
The Wise Man gave to the Brave Man's
cause
His money and brains for war or peace.
And the Poet he sang of juster laws
That come and come as the war-druius
cease.
The red, red flags for a while are furled
And stored away with the guns and tents:
And the Brothers are sleeping all over the
world,
And under a thousand monuments.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

10,000 FREE TRIAL BOXES.
Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
1 am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of. Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and. considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoving a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This led him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair
Since then, the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, 1 have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound 1 hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to - say it is the only hair grower in the world—-I am
not interested in decrying others—but Ido assert
